The Red Page
In Memoriam. Billjim
Over 350 epitaphs for Billjim, deceased,
were submitted. This page asked for origi-
nal epitaphs; but many competitors quoted
verses that wqre apt. None, it seems, is"
more fitting than the scriptural verse:
Greater love hath no man than this, that
a man lap down his life for his friends;
and mourners have universally recognised
its appropriateness. Many of the other
quotations, though excellent in sentiment
and expression, are unsuitable for the occa-
sion. Exceptions are: —
From the contagion of the world's slow stain
Thou art secure. (Shelley.)
His life was gentle; and the elements
So mix'd in him that Nature might stand up
And say to all the world, “This was a man.”
( SIIAKSPEARE.)
Yet, with the setting sun,
Come, now and then, at eve,
And think of me as one
For whom thou shouldst not grieve.
(Bryant.)
Many quotations and original verses were
disallowed because of the use of the plural:
the epitaph in a Roll of Honor must be for
in individual Billjim, uot for a mob. It is
a personal grief. For this reason some
excellent In Memoriams, including one by
Mary Gilmore, had to be cast out: —
They are not dead; not even broken;
Only their dust has gone back to the earth;
For they—the essential they!—shall have
re-birth
Whenever a word of them is spoken.
Toasts to the dead are not drunk in a col-
umn of the press; nor is that column a tomb-
stone. “Here rests”.... or “Tread lightly”
.... are not admitted. Nor is the dead
man allowed to speak his own epitaph. All
that is necessary is that some individual
should express his or her personal feelings
at the death of a loved one.
It was to be expected that the Australian
would find humor even in an epitaph. One
correspondent writes that a young Billjim,
prior to his departure overseas, suggested
that if he were killed in action a suitable
inscription to his memory would be: “Our
little bud in heaven.” And there are others:
His face was open and his step was free;
There was biff about, so he went to see.
11. G. R.
He lived and died, a dinkum cove;
And fighting fell witli soul unshrove.
But what of that? Who knew him well,
Will back his chance of dodging Hell.
S. Ellison.
And it was to be expected that in this coun-
try of voluntarism an element of bitterness
would creep into many a sincere epitaph.
These sound sincere :
He was only a youth, but he heard the call,
And played a man’s part in this big world strife.
And it sure was nobler to strike and fall
Than to drag out a coward slacker’s life.
McMac.
He played the game. Is yours the shame?
’Bidgee.
He answered ! His all in khaki draped,
He lay upon the Shrine of Sacrifice
To save the Human Race.
He fell! We mourn ; yet we rejoice
He missed the tragedy of Martin-place!
M. J. Keek.
The fight is o’er: the guerdon’s won:
Allied soil holds an only son.
Australian bred: game, clean and brave,
Thank God he fills no coward’s grave!
J. F. Daly.
In memory of Billjim.
He died on 22nd December, 1917,
As the result of wounds inflicted
By Australia on 21st October, 1916.
O. S. Hall.
A miscellaneous assortment, f.a.q.:—•
He was just a private, it matters not —
When the world’s at war such things must be—
But —killed while landing, fired ne’er a shot!
Gallipoli! Gallipoli! !
Jack Arnott.
In deathless sleep he lies whose soul
Has gone to an immortal goal;
Where, shining in the blue abyss,
His epitaph for ever is
The Starry Cross implanted o’er
The land he fought and perished for.
Edwin A. Connolly.
Clear-eved, from knowing clear, blue skies,
He meets Old Death, and smiling dies.
Christina Creagh.
Whispering gum-trees breathe his name,
Golden wattles speak of him;
Lonely bush and sunlit plain
Bring back to me my cobber Jim.
A. Borig.
He fought.... and died ; and now the world
Seems strangely dark to me:
My heart, my thoughts lie buried in
A land across the sea.
Peter Faux.
He heard the call of duty,
Our beloved ;
Answered : and in life’s young beauty
Passed away.
Merle de Q. Belford.
Record that he untimely died,
His talent’s ripest work unwrought;
But grieve not —for it was for him
The death he wished, the gate he sought.
May Siede.
A man he lived, and like a soldier died:
In life, in death, he never failed a mate:
If you were one, let grief find room for pride;
If you were none, grieve now for grief too late.
Per Castra ad Astra.
‘ So-long, Billjim. The sun went down before we
reached the “Halfway,” but we’ll follow the track
you blazed and win out.— Riverina.
Honored liis name,
Glorious his memory,
He died for Australia.
E. M. B.
Death, no horror, but winged with glory and com-
passion carries her beloved, and ours, through those
sacred and mysterious portals where not even our
love can pass—let our prayers and remembrances
drift like leaves in the wind towards them. Who
knows, but unseen hands shall tenderly gather them
in the silences beyond.— A. Howe.
How did he live? His was a quiet life,
Emptv of glow and of all that’s grand.
How died he, then? Ah, but right smilingly,
Serving his native-land.
Catherine B. MacL.
You went because frank Duty nade
You go, not seeking fame.
Death took away his toll, but left
A priceless thing—your name.
Game from the first, when he fought his fights—and
won them, too,
Telling them o’er in the candle-lights as schoolboys
do.
Game when he shouldered a gun because his faith was
fast.
And Heaven knows, I am sure, he was game to the
last.
Enid Hills.
No mark may show where you are laid ;
Your name from others’ thoughts may fade,
Leaving no regret:
Enough to know that this is true—
That such us live and die like you
God will not forget.
N. Devek.
He lived, and loved this life;
He fought, to do his bit;
He died, and was content.
F. B. King.
But it is from these that the choice must
be made: —
Poor Bill, he hadn’t a chance.
Jack Arnott.
His wide eyes turned to stranger stars above,
His cold cheek blanching in a stranger dew,
Dead for the secret thing his spirit knew—
Faith in the land that bore him and her love.
R. L. Jamieson.
Now in the grave he lies —his life-blood given;
Something divine he had, his clay to leaven ;
Something of God there was—something of Heaven,
Weeroona.
“So-long!” you called, with cheerful smile;
You—stalwart, brown and strong.
Ah! how we hoped it would not be
So long—so long—so long !
Jeanong.
Each of her fallen soldier sons, overseas, is thus
mourned by his national mother—Australia.
Angela Kennedy.
Into thine hands, 0 Lord, the soul unsheathed;
The transient dust to the red fields of France;
His life to freedom’s altar, laurel-wreathed ;
To us* the pride and sorrow of remembrance.
Erle Cox.
But not in vain his blood is shed,
For we, who may his record scan,
Learn how a hero fought and bled,
And died as he had lived—a man!
Norman Campbell.
The foemen trample where your bones are laid,
And so we cannot place upon your grave
The customary tribute of a flower,
But daily on your grave within our hearts
We place a wreath whose flowers are tender thoughts.
H. W. Stewart.
We sometimes sat for hours and smoked in silence—
Our comradeship had little need to crave
From spoken words the comfort that they gave—
Shall comradeship be less now you are gathered
Into the greater silence of the grave?
11. W. Stewart.
Shed not for him the bitter tear,
Nor vex the heart with vain regret;
This but the rusty scabbard here:
The sword it once held glitters yet.
B. J.
Surely a true son of Australia —he died for her..
L. W.
He went at Duty’s call, nor deemed it less than
just that he should fight and die for fair Australia.
H. D.
Good-bye—and thanks !
Uckle.
Face that I’ve kissed and caressed,
Bone of my bone was he,
Fondled and fed at my breast,
Coming not back to me.
G. D.
Gone to his rest—a true Australian,
The finest thing God ever made.
Life blessed him with her choicest gifts,
A clean straight life, a soldier’s death.
Thrice.
He asks no grief, no praise, no poet’s pen,
Naught but his soldier’s grave, and so around
The place wherein he sleeps is holy ground—
Akin with Christ, in that he died for men.-
Pearl Page.
When Honor looked you squarely in the face
You met her eye to eye,
And true unto the birthright of your race,
Showed how the brave can die.
D. J, Lutton.
From the uttermost seas,
When honor called, he went.
Far from the ferny dells he loved,
In France his body lies.
On the moonbeam path across the sea
Hi 3 soul comes home.
Mabel N. Z.
Not then —in Gallipoli—•
When, boy-like matching life for high adventure,
He fell, staining the daisies’ white to red,
And his soul, elate, waiting the whisper of Death,
Touched like a soft wind the buds o'erhead.
But now —his life fulfilled, his latest breath
For honor spent—somewhere in France he lies.
Marjorie N. Z.
The jovous blue and gold of Austral skies
He bartered for a glorious grave in France;
With singing lips and steadfast, dauntless eyes
He died a hero in the Great Romance.
And I am glad and proud as when he left —■
He cannot now of honor be bereft.
Mari Marina.
He counted life as little worth
Save as he spent it for that earth
Dear loved, to which he owed his birth.
M. L. Lane.
“Gone west,” they say. “Gone west.” But no, ah no!
Lad of the brave bright eyes and merry month !
You will come home to where the gum-trees grow:
You were Australian through and through, and so
You will be coming south, lad ! coming south.
Bartlett Adamson.
Here goes!
Mark Lane.
Jt
In making a final choice of the best epitaph
submitted it must be noted that this is but
a personal and temporary preference. The
real judges will be the mourners of Aus-
tralia : the best epitaph will be the one chosen
most frequently and paid for for insertion in
the Roll of Honor columns of the press. But
with that final judgment this page has noth-
ing to do. The considerations affecting this
page’s choice are these: An epitaph upon
Billjim should have beauty and dignity of
thought and utterance; it should give evi-
dence of personal feeling—grief, gratitude,
pride, love, etc.; it should be capable of
being utilised by every mourner, and it
should be applicable only to the death of a
soldier fighting for our country.
G.D.'s sob of grief is only for mothers;
and 11. \V. Stewart's We sometimes sat for
hours and smoked in silence. . . .only for
cobbers. There is a literary element in the
epitaphs of Bartlett Adamson, Jeanong and
“8.. T.”: they are too clever. Here goes!
is admirably expressive, but it was Billjim
who made that characteristic remark, not
the person who mourns him. Good-bye—
and thanks! is characteristic, too; but lacks
dignity. Poor Bill — he hadn't, a chance! is
a universal Australian thought; but this
page will be surprised if many mourners
choose it.
Choice hesitates between the epitaphs sub-
mitted by R. L. Jamieson, Weeroona, Erie
Cox, 11. W. Stewart ( The foemen trample
....), L.W., Mari Marina and M. L. Lane
(He counted life as little worth....). The
award goes to Erie Cox, 9 Gisborne-street,
Elsternwick, Vic., for:
Into thine hands, O Lord, the soul un-
sheathed;
The transient dust to the red fields of
France,
His life to freedom's altar, laurel-wreathed;
To us, the iiride and sorrow of remembrance.
But R. L. Jamieson, 11. W. Stewart and
Mari Marina deserve the prize almost as
well.
Pacifist Fiction
A curious aspect of recent English fiction
is its pacifist sympathies. May Sinclair, in
The Tree of Heaven, throws all her limelight
upon the eldest son, who refuses to enlist.
He is her hero, and though on the death
of his younger brother he dons khaki, this
conclusion seems rather a concession to
public opinion than a logical outcome of the
plot. Now Douglas Goldring, in The For-
tune (Maunsel, per Dymock’s Library)
makes his strongest character a conscien-
tious objector and an Irishman. Goldring, in
a prefatory note, remarks that: “No doubt,
the book will be dubbed by many elderly
people as a mere pacifist tract masquerading
under a thin disguise of fiction....l have
tried to give a straightforward account of
the spiritual adventures of an average ‘in-
tellectual’ in the first years of the war.”
But he claims that his hero’s tragedy is
shared by many thousands of old public-
school boys.
This is the story of an engaging youth
whose only weakness is his need of depend-
ence upon a stronger character. All through
his life he looks for guidance to his
friend, James Murdoch; and to James he
owes all his success. The pictures of public-
school life and of Oxford are admirably
done; and for Australian readers there is
always an interest in well-written accounts
of those typical English institutions, so
strange to us. The hero has the astounding
fortune, so common in fiction, so rare in
real life, of getting his first play accepted
and produced. Also, it is a success. But
Goldring makes this miracle a little more
feasible by attributing it to James’s expert
revision of the manuscript and the personal
friendship existing between the remarkable
James and a leading London manager.
The outbreak of the war occurs in the
middle of the book; and Goldring states his
case for the pacifists in every succeeding
chapter. He rightly holds up to our ridicule
characters like Gwen : “The desire to smile
bravely—with just a hint of tears sup-
pressed—was now become a habit with her.
She went about all over London, ‘smiling
bravely,’ and all her friends said, ‘lsn’t she
splendid!' just before the door shut. She
Was so busy being splendid that she could
think of nothing else except how terribly
wicked were the people who were not splen-
did. These were the men who did not imme-
diately enlist: the women who still danced
instead of doing war-work; the girls who
bought new clothes; finally—and worst of
all —the people who dared to hold unconven-
tional views about the war.”
The weak but charming hero enlists, thus
for the first time in his life opposing
James’s advice. James, the conscientious
objector, is pained, but suffers in silence.
The hero’s experiences at the Front are not
put in a rosy light: “The ennui induced by
the nervous strain of mastering one’s emo-
tions during moments of intense and deadly
fear was always waiting to overwhelm one.
.... Horrible things happened. Sometimes a
man's nerves would suddenly give way....
Harold came across a number of men who
were ‘queer’; who would never be the same
again, men whom he had known and loved
for their humor, their quick intelligence and
charming ways. These were now morose
wrecks with voices sunk to a whisper, who
at any moment might suddenly begin to do
‘something odd.’ and who watched them-
selves with pitiful intentness for the moment
which they could not control.” But worse,
in the novelist’s view, was the change in
Harold. He grew a moustache, and got red
and beefy and acquired military slang: his
wife didn’t like it at all. Distressing, of
course, but he was fighting for his country.
Jt
More interesting is the author’s portrait
of the English professional military caste.
“They all had much the same appearance—
little, close-cropped moustaches, puckers
round the eyes, which were clear, clean and
simple ; fine hands and feet. Their clothes,
when they were in mufti, were not too smart
or too new, but perfectly cut, and their
manners were perhaps the best which the
human race at the present day has been
able to produce. No wonder they were
popular favorites, no wonder everyone re-
garded them as the ideal type of English-
man. But that was just where tin- trouble
came in.... The whole English public-school
system for generations had bent itself to
the task of producing and propagating this
type ; every popular newspaper wallowed in
adulation of it day after day, till at last
the national cult of the ‘gentleman’ had
reached its final expression in the present
war. .. .The conduct of the war had been
handed over to gentlemen. One defect of
the very best of the old military caste was an
inability to see that excellence could exist
in any' other type than their own The
Colonel would only be able to appreciate
Harold in so far as he conformed to the
regulations of the military type. . ..‘The
finest type’ -was not quite so unassailable as
the Englishman supposed him to be. Per-
haps that would be one of the lessons which
the war would teach.... At least they were
born leaders; even if owing to a certain
mental imperviousness they frequently led
in the wrong direction... .The Colonel be-
longed to the sword and cloak age, or to
the period of the Indian Mutiny. But the
Bosches opposite,; with their damnable effici-
ency and full-bloodedness; the French with
their mental orderliness and precision—
these had the indefinable something of mod-
ernity about them. They were realists, not
romantics; or, perhaps, rather, theirs was
the new romance.... Here was a great em-
pire hypnotised by its slavish adulation of
one decayed type.” Goldring is wrong
there; there is at least one Dominion that
is not hypnotised by that pathetic survival.
Billjim knows.
Meantime the conscientious objector is
brought before the tribunal and examined.
An extremely interesting picture of the work-
ing of these 'tribunals is given by the author,
who is quite indignant that in a conscnp-
tionist country such tribunals should exist,
and whose sympathy is entirely for the
shirker. The pacifist’s exemption from
service is refused; but he needn’t have
appealed at all, as he is an Irishman. He
goes back to Ireland, and so escapes. Gold-
ring does not point out the meanness of his
idol in evading the issue: he would not have
forfeited the reader’s sympathy if he had
gone to gaol for his opinions. Well,. Harold
is killed, but not in France. He is mur-
dered in the Dublin rebellion by a panicky
soldier. And his pacifist hero survives,
and tells the widow that the saddest thing
about Harold’s death lay in the. fact “that
not only was he on the wrong side but he
knew it.” That glib "comment from the
friend who never risked his own life appro-
priately closes a clever but unconvincing
pacifist appeal.

Two belated suggestions for a substitute
for Australasia are ingenious, but, m this
page’s opinion, not so apt as Amalia. It.A.M.
proposes Auzealia. “It allows the Australian
his ‘oss’ (aus) and doesn’t deprive the New
Zealander of his ‘zeal.’ ” Sam A. Browne
wants Austoria, the first part of Aust-raha
and the last part of Ma-ori. However.
Amalia seems to have met with general
approval.

TYRES
VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Woman.
I am not very patient;
Yet patient I must be
With him beside my pillow,
And the babe upon my knee.
I am not very patient;
I would have wings to fly;
Yet am I tied to cradles
Until the day I die.
One is a rocking cradle
(Sleep, babe, without alarms!)
But for the Other Baby
The cradle of my arms.
Strange that I was given
Thoughts that soar to Heaven,
Yet must I sit and keep
Children in their sleep!
N.S.W.
MARY GILMORE.
Desire.
When the first dawn’s gold was falling
On the plumed and nodding sea,
From the shallows your voice w r as calling
With a measureless witchery.
You were ancient when earth, new-moulded,
Came superb from the shaping fire.
When the first young leaves unfolded
They turned to your altar, Desire.
From the flaming heights to the hollow,
From the high green land to the low,
When you call there is but to follow,
When you beckon all men must go;
Though they know that the sea’s blue stream-
ing
Is the mask of your cavern floor,
Where the bones of your dead lie gleaming
Afar from the sun-sweetened shore;
Though they know that you lie, they believe
you,
Though you poison their chalice of joy.
Though you vanish when mortals achieve
you,
Though the flames that you kindle destroy.
Yet your throne is the heart of the living;
You are sister, Desire, to the sun,
For Life is the gift of your giving,
Destroyer and Mother in one!
N.S.W.
P. E. QUINN.
Home of My Heart.
I pray, bent over my baby’s bed,
For one far off in the fight:
Heart of my heart, I am crying for you—
Heart of my heart, good-night.
And a whisper comes as the dawn grows grey,
And I dare not look at the light—
“ Heart of my heart, I am dying for y0u....
Home of my heart. . . .good-night.”
victoria.
ONE HUNDRED AND THREE.
A Journey Song.
Death puts a term to all our pains and
laughter,
And we fare forth into the enshrouding dark
That shuts us round through all the long
night after,
The night interminable that no spark
Of human prescience yet has pierced, nor
can,
The night that mocks the soaring dreams of
man.
We pass beyond the unceasing strain of
living,
The stress of fleeting joys and haunting
pains,
And so to full forgetfulness, forgiving,
Of what so made and marred us. There
remains
Dear sleep unbroken,'sleep of pure delight,
Inviolable silence and the night.
Life draws us subtly by a bait of pleasure,
A lure of lying kisses, to the grave,
Till Age brings in Despair to wreck at
leisure
Those last illusions that we fain would save,
Bruised fruits and sodden, mildewed at the
core,
That Time’s old treacherous ocean casts
ashore.
What matters it? Shall Death’s stale frip-
peries move us
To any fear, save that he come too late?
Life’s shams and shows are waiting still to
prove us,
And Love’s false witcheries croon at every
gate—
Poor woman-things who spice our tainted
blisses
With acrid savour of their hot fond kisses.
It may be He that formed and flawed us
knows
How best to spur the iaded appetites
That turned from delicate joys to feast on
those
That sting our barren days and feverish
nights.
Ah, Woman, till death find us (late or
quicker),
Queen of the Earth and Hell’s astutest
vicar!
Dear Woman, while dark fingers twist and
shove you
To work us woe, we know you, and we love
you!
We’ve still a little while for toys and
laughter.
To-night your lips! A fig for the night
after!
N.S.W.

THURSDAY, MAY 9, 1918.
Pbice 6d.
FOR DESERTING HIS MATE.
JUSTICE: “You sent this woman’s son, who was your mate, and 200,000 others, most of them your friends, to the war, and then you said the war was no
affair of yours, and you failed to send them reinforcements, with the result that many of them were killed. You stand charged, therefore, with desertion
of your mates in the face of the enemy. Speak, man—have you anything to say in your defence?”

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stampod letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of thin paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return nil unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn—will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MAY -9, 1918.
WANDERING IN THE WILDERNESS.
Atjstbaltan Labor is allowing itself to be
made a fool of. Dexterous wire-pullers on
the other side are manoeuvring it into a posi-
tion where, no matter what the end of the
war may be, the party will be decorated
with a very unpleasant odium. Within it
influential coteries are at work manipulating
the machinery on lines suggestive of com-
promise between cunning and crankiness.
Why it should please Labor to contemplate
itself being dragged by the nose is a mystery;
equally is it a mystery why the organisation
having by far the greatest latent political
and. civic power in the Commonwealth should
be uncertain of where it is and what it
wants in relation to a matter of overwhelm-
ing importance to the nation. Perhaps Labor
is not lf so the sooner it changes
the tactics of sullen equivocation for those of
reasonable candor the sooner will it have
cause for satisfaction. There may be people
who understand what its war policy is. If
there are their method of explaining it is
horribly unfortunate. But this paper pre-
fers to "believe that there are none —that the
party as a political body has become demor-
alised by words and conflicting ideas, and is
gro.ping'about in the dark afraid to say what
it means and meaning something different
from what it says. At any rate, there is
not in all Australia a single man who will
come upon a public platform and define his
own and Labor’s attitude towards the Com-
monwealth war effort in plain terms. All
Hie. men who can claim to speak on behalf
of organised Labor are either sitting on a
rail or struggling to explain their explana-
tions or trying to make bargains or find the
line of least; resistance for the next 48
hours or qualifying as “Yes-No” specialists.
Yet the issues at stake involve the very
existence of everyone of them as free citi-
zens and of the country they call their home-
!iind - , '
It is no good trying to make out that taking
up a negative attitude towards Conscription
j s a war policy. That is a mere negation,
ending where it begins and leaving nothing
but a biank. Nor is it any use for Laborites
to pose as martyrs and imagine that there is
nothing else to do but criticise the Ministry
and groan about the horrors of war and
extol the beauties of peace. Where does
that lead to? Nowhere but into the wilder-
ness of futility. Let it be assumed as true
that the present Federal Government is
grossly incompetent for the job entrusted to
it. And for the sake of argument let it be
admitted that this Government deserves all
the hard things Labor has to say on the sub-
ject of its failure; let it be admitted, too,
that war is a monstrous institution from
which the world suffers inexpressible misery.
What then? The inescapable facts are that
war is raging almost at our door and that
this Government is the instrument through
which the country must work for the time
being. The war will not stop to allow the
people of Australia to make their system of
government better than it is. It is a war
that must be either won or lost now, for the
trial is not of what might be done under
different conditions but of what can be done
while the struggle goes on. There afe many
ways by which it can be lost. There is only
one way by which it will be won, no matter
on which side victory may rest at the finish,
and that is by bringing to bear upon the
ghastly task the fullest measure of purpose
and all the strength of soul there is in the
nation. Australia can derive no hope that
the Allies will benefit from hearing harsh
words used about someone named Hughes.
It makes resistance to the enemy no
stronger to know that the Labor party has
passed a comprehensive resolution about
what th(> peace should be like, or that there
is nothing in the Labor platform to prevent
a member of the party supporting enlist-
ment, or that one day the party is announced
to be in favor of an immediate cessation of
battle and on the next is said by Senator
Gardiner to be “in the fight to the finish.”
Criticism of the Ministry is all right in a
way. The Ministry richly deserves nearly
everything that has been said about it.
Horror of Hughes has sound justification.
The damage that individual has done to
the Commonwealth is almost beyond compu-
tation. But he is off the premises now, and
in any case there is no sense in assisting
him to do more harm by sulking in holes
and corners, and by mere inactivity and
camouflage doing that which the enemy
would sooner have us do than anything else.
Labor when in office during the first
couple of years of war sent thousands of
men away to the Front and took credit for
it It is too late now for it to start hedging
about the war without making itself look
worse than contemptible. Those men went
away to fight for a cause which Labor urged
them to defend, and that cause is in terrible
jeopardy. If the people who are left behind
owe a moral obligation to anything or any-
body, it is to support those whom they in-
duced to tackle the job. It is not a question
of supporting Governments but of standing
by our own blood—the blood we pushed to
the Front —and of supporting this Australia
of ours lest another hand drag it from our
feeble grasp. Had the Australian people
been mad enough to put a Ministry com-
posed of vipers in office, it would still be
their duty to help, so long as they were able,
to drag down the monster struggling to
break out of Europe in search of his prey.
What the public wants to know is how
Labor proposes to defend Australia —whether
it is going to be content to pass resolutions
and talk about defending something else, or
will it come out into the open and,
in proving the efficacy of voluntaryism,
justify its creed and its patriotism? The
mass of the people have no other wish than
to see the German held up. The Australian
soldier is almost without exception a work-
ing man. A great part of him is unionist,
and it is to hold the German up that he is
over yonder. But here there are influences
at work among unionists which are para-
lysing the men to whom Australia looks for
assistance in the defence of the nation and
incidentally of voluntaryism, which is
Labor’s own policy for the moment. If for
no other reason, then for that alone. Austra-
lian unionism should he the most powerful
factor in recruiting reinforcements. It is
to-day inert. Some of it is hostile. And all of
this plays in the political sense right into
the hands of its enemies and in the national
sense is equivalent to giving the Australian
army—to a very great extent its own army—
a stab in the back.
WE SHALL WIN.
The down _ tail) can ’ t -be-helped attitude of a
peop ie j n Australia during this Ger-
-r °rr. is , not ‘™ ; bu i
most depiessmg and disquieting-thing o
all is the persistent attempt to explain away
and excuse every defeat and set-back. To record
a German victory such as that represented
b Huns’ recent advance on the Somme
J
then assure the public that it was ita y
a German defeat goes a long way to take
the heart out of the patient person who has
hoped for the best, but who, not being an
absolute idiot, cannot be persuaded that
buttle triumphs are really won by retreating
and leaving guns and armies of prisoners
behind you. Had an honest method of
reporting land operations been adopted at
the start the public would now be capable
of a greater enthusiasm; and enthusiasm is
a weapon that counts for a great deal in
prolonged warfare. At the same time,
although the hammering and the continued
mauling is evident even to the right-thinker,
who would accept every excuse and explana-
tion of each successive “strategic retreat” if
it had anything like an appearance of truth,
there is still no need to throw down our
hands in helplessness. Despite the deep
wounds made in the Allies’ lines at the
Somme and in Flanders, there must be some
real sinking of the Teuton’s heart when he
comes to consider possibilities. The argu-
ment of, the pessimist is largely that the
enemy has retained his punch after years
of fighting, and that, even if we renew our
offensive with America’s aid, it will take
ages to push him back to any point at which
he can be mortally wounded. The plain
answer is that he is being mortally wounded
now by being shut up within his own bor-
ders, and that if Britain and France can
hold on long enough, even suffering defeats
like those of recent weeks, the German will
be forced to make terms. As to that holdmg-
on power, it is notable that in past wars
Germanv did, as a rule, collapse pretty sud-
denly and completely when the foe pushed
too hard, while England and France required
to be trodden on with a road-roller before
they would consider the possibility of being
really licked. As things now stand, Germany
has got about as good as she has given. The
stepping of all her trade, the taking of all
her colonies and the steady killing of German
soldiers on all fronts makes a fair balance
against the doubtful gains of European
territory and the success of the submarine.
The mere fact that by throwing huge Ger-
man armies into the- shambles the Allied
forces in France can still be pushed back a
few miles is no conclusive evidence that we
are not going to win and win right out.
* * * ‘ *
But. on the analogy of old wars, victory
is still some distance away* We are still
going through the stages which in struggles
such as those against Napoleon broke men’s
hearts with waiting. The force is ours to
use, the will to triumph is ours, the great
national sacrifices have been made, but we
are still waiting for the man and the way.
The outstanding feature of the terrific tumult
is its flatness. Both sides have steadily used
battering-ram tactics; but only once or twice
has there been a flash of war genius. The
tanks’ advance on Canjbrai without artillery
preparation was just such a stroke as might
have turned a whole campaign; but. unfor-
tunately, its big success was not anticipated
by the high command, and what might have
been a crowning success was turned into a
semi-failure. In the same way, Churchill’s
idea of a simultaneous land and sea attack
on the Dardanelles was altogether good and
must, if executed, have gained, us Constan-
tinople; but the work was muddled, the war-
ships signaled our intention to the enemy
and Australia lost much of her best blood in
trying to repair the damage. Still there
remain just as many ideas to be tried on
the battlefield as there are physical features
of the Flemish landscape. The enemy, with
long years of closest preparation, has shown
comparatively little initiative. Initiative
was never the German’s strong point; and
it seems at least reasonable to hope that in
the coming weeks or months somebody on
our side will think of something. Com-
paring the races engaged in the light of their
past achievements, there is little doubt that
the hanging-on power of the Allies is greater
and that their chances of producing a man
and an idea are distinctly better than
those of the enemy. Add to this the fact
that the resources of the Allies are more
abundant and that with America coming in
their man-power will be enormously in-
creased. and it seems not merely reasonable
to think that we have a good chance of
winning, but almost traitorous to imagine
that we can be licked. If we are licked it
will be because we lack staying power or
winning ideas; and in that event we shall
probably deserve what is coming to us.
But to win the war in a way that is worth
while we ought to get down to business.
The new armies are inspired with the spirit
of those little old armies which so often
plucked victory from despair. But war is
not now fought by armies alone; it is waged
by nations. The nation must be behind the
army. For this reason it is necessary that a
live interest in the war itself should be kept
up among the civilian population who stand
behind the fighting men. Such an interest
is not being maintained ; and it can only be
maintained by giving fuller information to
the people and allowing them free discussion.
Here and there men and women are going
about moaning that we are beaten. Can we
wonder at it? Would men who were win-
ning, they ask; not tell the truth? Deception
and defeat, they argue, go together. And
what can we answer them except this —that
the military machine is something apart;
that it has no regard for the public; that
it does not believe the public should be con-
sulted or informed; that really the proper
thing to do with the public is to chloroform
it until the job is finished? This quite
fairly, The Bulletin believes, puts the
military attitude; and because the politi-
cians who represent the people have not told
the military heads that this attitude is in-
tolerable they have failed terribly in their
duty. In Australia, more even than in most
other places, it is urgently necessary to get the
people filled with the liveliest belief that this
is their war. How can we succeed in that when
the politicians allow the military heads to
push the people aside —to treat them as
though this was nb concern of theirs at all;
as though it was nothing to them whether
this military leader or that was a fool or a
genius; as though discussion of the treat-
ment of soldiers— their soldiers —was no
concern of the people at all? How can we
get the man in the street to feel and say
“This is my war!” when the whole official
tendency is to show him that it isn’t? We
are not beaten; we do not intend to be
beaten. But if defeat could be brought
about it would be by the very methods we
have adopted.
THE PAPER MILL AND SOME
OBSERVATIONS ON PORK.
The great War Loan has closed after being
kept open two weeks beyond the date origi-
nally fixed, and the £40,000.000 asked for has
been over-subscribed. As the manager of the
Commonwealth Bank put the case, it was a
“stupendous achievement.” Still Australia
isn’t in a position to rest on its laurels, and
it has more to do than rub noses, Maori
fashion, in congratulation. The loan wasn’t
so very heavily over-subscribed, after all; in
fact, it appears that, but for the extension
of time, it would have been under-subscribed.
And a few other matters may profitably be
taken into account: —
(1) The biggest demand was for the 44 per cent,
securities which are free of Federal and State taxa-
tion: No one knows what this concession will amount
to when the bill has to be faced. The 8 per cent,
received from bank or insurance shares may be worth
only 4 per cent, net; and such profits are subject
to fluctuations. A guaranteed 4A per cent, is ah
insurance against a multitude of risks ; it is a tower
of strength and, barring public default, it is a protec-
tion against almost everything except sudden death.
(2) The whole business was only made possible by
the issue of paper money. It is about the best-
secured paper money in the world; still the fact
remains that if a Government issues, say,
£50,000,000 of paper (face value) and establishes a
perfectly sufficient gold reserve of, say, £18,000,000
behind it, there is £32,000,000 (face value) of
new money to play with —money that can’t go out
of the country because, it is of no value outside the
country. And by reason of the credit allowed on
instalments there wasn’t £32,000,000 of actual cash
put into the loan. And when the first instalment is
spent locally (being mostly paper it must, in the
main, be spent locally) it will get into circulation
and help to pay the second instalment, and the
second one will give a leg up to the third.
So the paper mill worked hard at printing
the bonds and the documents by which the
banks agreed to advance money at 4 p.c. on
44 p.c. or 5 p.c. securities.
* * » *
It was, as the manager of the Common-
wealth bank truly remarked, “a stupendous
achievement.” Still the next loan had better
step lightly and not step too suddenly, for
the present one has pawned the future
pretty heavily. But such are, after all, side
issues. The big issue is that a local loan,
under present conditions, is of less use to us
than a similar loan is to any of the big
Allies. In Britain, France and the United
States the public debt and the great mass of
private securities are almost entirely held
inside the country where the Governme,nt
paper money is good for the payment of prin-
cipal and interest. Owing to the insane
Freetrade policy of floating loans in London
and raising other forms of capital in London
to “develop the great natural resources,”
Australia is more heavily indebted abroad in
proportion to population than any other land
on God’s green and pawned earth ; and the
equally insane Freetrade policy of relying
more heavily on imports than on local manu-
factures has made it more dependent on goods
brought from outside than any other land
on the Almighty’s embarrassed footstool.
As all these outside liabilities must be paid in
gold , Australia, despite its “stupendous
achievement,” is in a worse position than
any other land of importance, especially as
both the gold output and the number of per-
sons employed in gold mining are little more
than one-third of what they were 17 years ago.
The “stupendous achievement” already men-
tioned throws no light worth mentioning on
this problem, yet it is the one problem that
must be faced if our people are to avert in-
solvency and disaster. Russia, which was
loaded with foreign debt just as we are,
cut the matter short by Bolsheviking and
repudiation. Australia doesn't want either
to Bolshevik or to repudiate. To put the
matter on the most sordid basis, these
methods don’t pay. The visible remedy is
real hard work accelerated by a real hard
tariff, so as to produce more goods that are
worth gold and thereby to diminish the drain
caused by paying for imports, that being the
only drain we can diminish without filing our
schedule. Unfortunately this country seems
to be working a little less than usual instead
of a little more, though the output of indus-
trial disputes is considerable. Pigs are not
an inspiriting thing, neither are bananas,
eggs or split peas. Still they are local pro-
ducts, and when the number of strikes beats
the number of pigs exported (figures for
191.6) by five to one, and also leaves bananas,
chilled pork and split peas hull down on the
horizon, and there are 17 times as many
labor upheavals as there are tons of kero-
sene shale sent away, affairs look lopsided.
In Canada, the United States and Britain
the drastic war device of compulsory labor
has been adopted in one form or another —
but that is a different matter.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Song and Dance and Snuffle.
Barnabas, who writes leading articles for
the S. M. Herald, was responsible for this
(Barnabas means “Son of Consolation,” so
if he doesn’t write for the Herald he ought
to) :
So far, however, we have managed to pay our way
out of revenue. As loan has been added to loan the
interest has been paid out of taxation. There has
been no notion of making one loan pay the interest
bill on the loan that preceded. That is what they
have been doing in Germany. .. .
It is all very kind and comforting, yet there
is about it a faint, musty odor of Bunkum.
It smells like the fruit of the Bosh-tree, a
frequent vegetable in this country. Let the
matter be considered further :—-
(1) We have always managed to pay our way out
of revenue—a lot of it borrowed revenue.
(2) When a loan was floated in London the money
either remained in London to pay interest on loans
and to meet other liabilities, or else it came out in
the shape of goods.
(3) When a big loan was floated there was a great
boom in imported goods—the kind we bought from
other people to prevent our own artisans having em-
ployment. That was “commerce,” and the Herald
said it proved that we were doing splendidly.
(4) A portion of the value of the goods bought
with borrowed money was intercepted at the Cus-
toms. In other words, a portion of the loan was in-
tercepted. It really looks like comic brigandage, this
process of waylaying and plundering our own lOU
on our own highway.
(5) The borrowed goods and the borrowed revenue
made a boom, and the Argus and the Daily Tele-
graph said that the general expansion of things was
quite remarkable.
(6) When a country floats its loans locally and
spends them on locally-made goods, instead of borrow-
ing abroad as Australia is so fond of doing, then it
doesn’t intercept part of the borrowings at the Cus-
toms and call it “revenue” and use it to pay interest
with, for the loans don’t go through the Customs. So
if it borrows openly to pay some of the interest it
gets there just the same as we do, only without the
“commerce” and the “revenue” and the boom and the
hypocrisy. Queensland and N. S. Wales have often
paid half the year’s interest out of loans, of which
fact the Brisbane Courier might make a note.
(7) There are also direct borrowings to cover
interest. Revenue' deficiencies £1,478,400 (Queens-
land) and the same item £146,871 (Tasmania) are
cases in point.
(8) And the statement of Barnabas of the S. M.
Herald that “the interest has been paid out of taxa-


tion” is too silly to impose on a lame pauper. In
the Herald’s own State of X. 8. XV ales fully t'vo-
thirds of the interest bill until 1901 was met by the
proceeds of land sales, and nearly a quarter of the
interest bill both in X. S. Wales and Queensland is
still covered by land sales. If the consolatory
Foreign-trade paper regards the jobbing away of a
national asset as “taxation” then it would probably
call the sale of its morning penn’orth “taxation’ also.
To put the matter shortly, between paying
interest out of foreign loans and paying in-
terest out of land-sale money, this casual,
credulous land of false prophets and be-
nighted shirks has only just begun to pay
a little interest out of taxation. It hasn’t
nearly got down to normal yet, but it is
getting there. Perhaps the old happy delu-
sion might be kept up a little while longer by
putting a 30 per cent, import duty on our
own paper money. Then a Government could
borrow, say, £3,000,0f)0 of it from its own
people, subscribing part of the loan itself if
necessary, and send it in Saratoga trunks to
Fiji, where the rich uncles grow, and bring
it back charging £9OO,O(X> of it as revenue at
the Customs, which sum would do a lot of
good. After that, the remaining £2.100,(L
could go to Fiji again and pay £630.000 to
the revenue on its re-entry, and so on till the
whole amount had been turned in a per-
fectly legitimate fashion into perfectly legi-
timate income, and a new loan was wanted.
It would only be doing with song and dance
and no snuffle what we have hitherto done
with snuffle and no song and dance

Brushing Burrs out of Brains and
Wool.
When the day arrives for Australia to
make a start living upon what the nation
can earn instead of having a jamboree on
what it can- borrow, one of’ the first things
to be noticed will be; the expediency of
getting as much out of the local product as
other people can extract from it. In a
vague way this has already been learned
about metals, though not about coal. Now
some glimmerings of the truth about wool
are beginning to penetrate the public mind.
In its enormous annual harvest from the
jumbuck the Commonwealth has had the
material on which great industries might
have been built, the technical and scientific
skill of the people exercised and the earnings
of the community correspondingly increased.
The general habit has been to concentrate
on thinking out the most expeditious way of
shifting the wool from the shearing-shed to
the other side of the water, so that someone
else could get to work on it and send us
back the finished article. Whereupon the
country floated a loan to pay him for it, and
long, glad details were published to show
what a splendid asset Australia possessed in
its sheep and how if we increased the produc-
tion thereof we could borrow some more and
get continual supplies of tweed and hosiery
and mixtures of wool and rags from the
ends of the earth. Slowly a few woollen
mills came into being to compete against an
ignorant prejudice and shoddy imports and
the cheaper labor of other countries. Most
of them livgd precariously before the war; a
good many of them didn’t live at all; and
even now lamentations a-re to be heard that
supplies of imported woollens are running
short—in a. country producing about
1,800,000 bales of wool a year! The cold
fact about this mass of wool is that in ex-
porting it on the plans hitherto followed
Australians have been letting millions slip
through their fingers. The manufacture of
tops should be a great Australian industry.
The recovery from the wool of waste pro-
ducts such as lanoline is in other countries
highly profitable. Even the job of taking out
the burr and the dirt is one Australia leaves
to strangers. In Winchcombe-CaksON’s
review of the wool trade it is pointed out
that practically no attempt has been made
to establish wool carbonising works in the
Commonwealth, except in the case of a few
mills, to treat some of the very small quan-
tity of wool used in local manufactures.
Actually the average Yorkshireman knows
better than the Australian what the term
used in relation to w T ool means. Carbonising
is the process by which wool is separated
from the burr and other vegetables gathered
by the sheep. About 15 per cent, of the Aus-
tralian wool clip needs carbonising-—that is,
about 280.000 bales. With the burr, etc..
removed this wool appreciates in value about
25 per cent. Before the war the Belgians
and French and to some extent the Germans
were ready buyers of this class of clip.
Taking last year’s average of values 280,000
bales mean £5,000,000. Carbonised they
would mean £1,250,000 more—and that is
what Australia has been losing by burrs in
Australian fleeces and what the foreign
manufacturers and workers have been gain-
ing by burrs in Australian brains. But
there has also been a loss on freight, for 31b.
of uncleaned wool costs 15d. to ship abroad,
whereas if the wool were treated on the
premises only lib. of clean wool would be
left for a ship to carry and collect sd. on.
It also happens that most of the wool which
needs carbonising is specially sought after
for the manufacture of fabrics for under-
wear. There is a field, therefore, in three
directions for great industrial expansion
when Australia turns seriously to work. The
country will want every penny it can make
and save, too. There is no room for error
on that point. Not only must production go
on but the wit to handle what is produced
must be developed.

The Wheat Wickedness.
One of these days The Bulletin intends
to publish a story about the profits made
out of wheat-growing in Australia by (1)
the man who ploughed and sowed and har-
vested ; (2) the individuals who were agents
for the numerous pools; and (3) the person
who got in on. the ground floor in the gamble
in wheat scrip. Meanwhile it is about time
the bodies called Wheat Boards issued a
statement as to whether they regard the
enormous accumulation of wheat in Aus-
tralia as an asset of any value, and, if so,
what they are doing to safeguard the grain
from the ravages of time and wild animals.
When the mice plague had reached the Stage
of being able to consume vast quantities of
wheat it was tackled. When the stacks
leghn to fall down they were lifted, up.
When the coverings blew away they were
replaced. When the Boards heard that the
weevil could he combated they did some-
thing small in the way of prevention. But
the information possessed by this paper is
that they are doing nothing to eradicate the
weevil from wheat already affected, and are
not getting into a position to grapple with
the enemy when the warm weather of sum-
mer stirs it into life again. They are simply
taking no notice of the fact that weevily
wheat can be converted into good shipping
or milling grain. A number of machines
have been devised at the expense of the
British Government for reconditioning
weevil-infested cereals. These apparently do
the work satisfactorily. Yet the weevils
remain undisturbed. It is possible that the
Wheat Boards'count on being able to unload
weevily wheat on someone who will clean it.
If so, they ought to say so. They may con-
sider it all in the game that the pest should
ruin a substantial portion of the wheat on
hand. They may even reckon that it doesn't
matter what happens, anyhow, and that
GockY, having been guaranteed 4s. a bushel,
is not interested in the possibility of getting
more or in the grain being fit for delivery.
But there is no reason why these views
should be kept secret or that the Boards
should hide their eccentric policies behind a
solemn pretence of great efficiency.

The Cost of Railways.
One cause of the immense shortage expected
in the N. >S. Wales accounts at June next is
the fact that railway revenue, despite in-
creased charges, is below expectations. Per-
haps the explanation of this trouble, both in
the Ma State and some other States, lies in
the fact thkt the cost per mile on which
interest has to be earned grows steadily
larger. Here are a few figures so far as
KnibbS’s statistics go :—•
Cost Pub Mile or Line.
1002. 1916. Increase per mile.
N. S. Wales'£l3,4os £16,434 £3029
Victoria. . . 12,300 13,266 966
Tasmania.. 8,313 8,539 226
Decrease per mi lc.
Queensland 7,183 7,004 179
Westvaliu. . 5,449 5,138 311
South Aus. 8,766 7,881 ‘ 885
The South Aus. figures in the first column are
not really for 1902, for the sale of a big line to
the Commonwealth disarranged the calcula-
tions, and it was necessary to start at a
later date. But the discrepancy of one date
in the case of one State doesn’t affect the
result. It was always understood that when
the principal lines in and around the cities,
where land is expensive, were built; when
N. S. Wales had finished its enormously
costly Hawkesbury Bridge, the biggest thing
of its kind south of the line; when it had
built over the mountains north and west and
south, and its biggest construction work was
out on the plains where land was dirt cheap
or was already Government property,
the cost of construction would fall materi-
ally. And it was to fall in like manner in
Victoria when the costly Melbourne subur-
ban system was completed. And there was
to be a special saving because railway build-
ing is now so largely a matter of machinery
instead of the primitive pick and shovel.
But the average cost per mile grows bigger
in three States, especially in N. S. Wales.
Apparently no matter how cheap the new
lines being constructed out on the plains
may look, they can be made to prove very
expensive. And no matter how many years
ago a line may have been finished (out of
loans) there is always excuse for spending
more loan money on it. But though N. S.
Wales managed to add £3029 per mile to the
average cost of its lines in 14 years it
wasn’t till the last year of the 14 that the
situation became really dazzling. Here is
the situation:—

If even the oldest, grandfather among rail-
ways is to go on increasing in capital cost
till the Day of Judgment—if no line ever
becomes so old that there isn’t an excuse for
charging something more to loans and adding
someth'ng to the interest bill —what sort of
a prospect is there?

A Fair Deal for Billjiin.
There is a movement in Sydney, promoted
by the Mayor, “to show' a deeper apprecia-
tion of the services of the soldiers and a
fuller sense of obligation to them.” It is
about time. That the movement has been
delayed so long is, nevertheless, not the pub-
lic’s 'fault. The public, as a whole, does not
know how returning and' returned men are
treated. It is not allowed to know. The
press is muzzled. It must not do anything
“prejudicial to recruiting”—though, as the
recent Press Conference decided unani-
mously. this rule has “itself become highly
prejudicial to recruiting by hindering the
redress of grievances.” Since the press is
thus not permitted to show its own apprecia-
tion of the services of the soldiers and a
sense of obligation to them, and since it is
not permitted to show what the men’s griev-
ances are and thus arouse public interest to
demand and secure their redress, the only
course open is to have in every city a com-
mittee which, with a strong body of public
opinion behind it, may be able to enforce
some reforms—mainly in administration.
Apart from showing an appreciation of the
services of men who have already done their
bit. there is no more valuable recruiting work
outstanding that this ; which makes the new
movement doubly desirable at the present
moment.

Harbors of One Sort and Another.
A feature of Premier Holman’s deliver-
ances, while being bogged on N. S. Wales
North Coast roads, concerned the advantages
of opening up by railway communication
those ports which ate really river mouths,
instead of the other variety. The first sam-
pie of port bas; a big stream behind it, and
along the stream are rich alluvial flats
capable of supporting a large population if
the railway charges are not faked to divert
trade to Sydney, as happened with the New-
castle wool exports. For these flats blossom
like the rose or the plausible green bay tree;
they are capable of growing potatoes, maize,
wheat, hav, straw, split peas, milk, pump-
kins, ensilage, fruit, pickles, cigars, millet,
etc., and thus they supply goods for ship-
ment. Also (but this wasn’t mentioned)
they supply floods, silt,- shifting bars and all
manner of difficulties, involving great and
constant expense, and when money and im-
ported dredges are scarce they are liable to
suffefl from economy and become temporarily
closed, and occasionally, in times of great
rain, they close on general principles. For
want of illimitable cash most N. S. Wales
river mouths are merely haunts of small
craft which carry their cargoes to Sydney for
transhipment, and don’t interfere with the
one big city’s hold on the over-sea trade;
also no great centres grow up there,to com-
pete as banking, insurance and general finan-
cial centres with the abundant metropolis.
On the other hand, the harbor like Jervis
Bay, Eden. Port Stephens or Sydney which
has no big river flowing into it, has much
natural deep water, no floods worth speak-
ing of, no silting up, no unmanageable bar,
no sudden closures, ho eternal bill for dredg-
ing and no big outlay for breakwaters. But
having no big river it lacks those rich adja-
cent flats where the haystack grows and the
smiling landscape brings forth abundantly.
Of course the smiling landscape is bunkum
so far as concerns the question of opening up
serviceable ports. The ocean steamer can't
go up and down the river collecting milk
and onions. The bulk of its cargo has to lie
brought to it by rail or otherwise just as in
the case of the vastly better and cheaper
port like Sydney, which hasn’t the alluvial
flats right on the wharf. Certainly such
places as Olasgow and London are situated
very much as Grafton is. But the cost has
been something that this country couldn’t
dream of, and even then Glasgow is inferior
to Sydney as a Shipping port, and London
doesn’t much more than hold its own against
upstart places like Liverpool and Cardiff,
which have less political pull and centuries
loss of a start, but which also have less silt.
Australia lias had a long experience of river
ports like Maryborough, Rockhampton (which
exists chiefly to divert attention from Glad-
stone), Innisfajl, Perth (which has to do its
shipping at Fremantle), Melbourne (which
has wasted an immense amount of its sub-
stance trying to make the Yarra a real
harbor while Westernport, which the
Almighty made a real harbor, is left a
blank) ; and it has found them all costly and
few of them profitable. Many places which
call themselves ports got their start because
(1) they had 3ft. or 4ft. of water, which
seemed enough in the early days for shipping-
purposes; (2) the river was consequently
fordable, and it was too early to think of
bridges; (3) they had alluvial flats handy,
and that was an advantage in the times when
the ship could call at the farm in passing and
load up with turnips; and (4) the river was
drinkable' owing to the distance from the sea,
and in the absence of waterworks the popula-
tion could fetch the fluid in a jug. Thus
some ill-selected spots accumulated enough
people and political pull to prevent the
development of much better places close at
hand, even when the old spots became out of
date. As a sort of saving clause Holman
proceeded to enthuse a little about the neg-
lected deep-water ports of his own State- —
Port Stephens, Twofold Bay and Jervis Bay.
He yearns, however, only now that the loan
money is giving out. But The Bulletin
recofds the yearn in case loan money should
some day resume and Holman may be still
available to spend it.
Some Words and the Liquor Question.
The Newcastle Anglican Synod has been
worrying itself over the question of liquor,
gambling and picture, films, and in the
report of its Civics and Morals Committee
explains the position with regard to alcohol :
We live in a hot climate, and the percentage of
alcohol even in our beers is intolerable. The chief
methods of reform proposed are State control and
prohibition. Just now we are less ready to worship
“State control” in general than was the tendency
of recent years. We have learnt that the State, even
when spelt with a large S, is not necessarily a
inoral power of any very high order.
Now the difference between prohibition and
State control is sufficiently wide to suggest
that the Synod might have given an opinion
one way or the other. Under prohibition all
the licensed trade goes out and beer fades
into the dens of the sly-grog seller or remains
in a cask under the bed in the home of the
thirsty. Under State control a reformed'
pub sells liquor of the best quality more
openly than ever before and with the
evils of drunkenness eliminated as far
as possible. There is no compromise
between the two systems, and the mere men-
tion of them in a report does not help the
controversy forward one step. The remark
about the State being not necessarily a high
moral power has therefore a quaint look
when coupled with the previous yes-no
observation. The moral- power of a State
on questions of this kind does not rest with
the Legislature nor with the policeman. It
is simply the force of public opinion ; and
if an Anglican civics and morals committee
hasn’t any more opinion than may be
summed up in the remark that it is too hot
for beer, and therefore there ought to be
no beer, or if there is beer then it ought to
be better beer, what decent hope is there
that the opinion of the rank and file should
be any more fixed or effective? If an
ecclesiastical body sets itself a definite task
of advising on a moral question and then
presents a report which is a mere camouflage
of _ words, why throw mud at the State for
being indefinite also?

“For the Future.”
News of a projected German-Austrian com-
bine to control the import trade of “war pro-
ducts”—animals, vegetables, textiles, min-
erals, etc.—from Russia, Persia, China and
elsewhere can be accepted without any grain
of salt. The scheme came into Germany’s
dream of world-conquest as soon as the pre-
sent war had convinced the Kaiser that
something more than unlimited bloodshed
was essential to German victory. Baron von
Fre ytag-Lori nghoveJn, “the most distin-
guished soldier-writer of Prussia,” talks of
the necessity for it in his book, “Deductions
from the World War”:—
In the European wars up to 1870-71, when we were
still predominantly an agrarian State, questions of
world-policy and world-economics had played a com-
paratively subordinate r6le. It has been the develop-
ment of our trade, combined with the increase of our
population, which, in the course of this world war,
has thrown into special prominence the significance
of these questions in relation to our Fatherland. The
import of raw materials, foodstuffs and manufactured
articles, the export of the products of our industries,
had become essential conditions of our economic life.
In regard to these questions the outbreak of the world
war found us insufficiently prepared. Such measures
as we had taken were shown to be inadequate. . . .
This is not the place til examine how far, in view of
the all too rapid growth of her trade, worhl-policv
and world-economics may have been premature in
the case of Germany, inasmuch as our continental
position was still by no means sufficiently assured.
Even the power of prevision attributed to great men
is, after all, very limited. In every domain only the
war itself could be the great teacher in regal’d to
these hitherto unknown effects of world-economics
upon its range... .Without clear views and an
adequate understanding of the major sequences of the
War, not only as regards operations and tactics, hut
also as regards world-politics and world-economics,
tcc .tlinll nnt be in a position to draw accurate deduc-
tions for the future.
Tim Austro-Germau combine is more than
projected. It is already in operation, with
ils headquarters at Bremen, and is to be
“officially liquidated a year after peace is
signed, but will continue its operations un-
officially.' Practically it is a combine for the
purpose of winning the war next time, for,
"in th(> future as in the past,” says the cold-
blooded war-writer, “the German people will
have to seek firm cohesion in its glorious
army and in its helaurelled young fleet.” If
the Allies are driven to make a peace before
Germany is well beaten, we have at least
been forewarned as to the use to which the
truce will ho put.
Wild and Woolly Happenings.
As the good Australian goes to the war
front he loses his hold on his country’s
affairs, for the right to vote counts for
nothing worth mentioning when the organisa-
tion is left to the other man. So the bad
Australian, and the indifferent Australian,
and the person who isn’t any Australian to
speak of, but a linn or pro-Hun or Bolshe-
vik or Anarchist or I.W.W. or mere crank,
gain, more and more influence. Compulsory
service would send the men this country
requires to be rid of to the Front as well
as taose that it can ill spare; voluntaryism
leaves the worst elements a chance to govern
the community in the absence of better men.
Hence quite a number of remarkable things
are happening—small things, perhaps, but
many of them. The Geelong (Vic.) Trades
and Labor Council refused to support an
appeal by the wifi; of the Governor-General
for Red Cross funds, it being explained by
the local Federal member that the Red Cross
was used for the oppression of the “working
classes.” The Labor party, or some unde-
sirables who are allowed to use it for their
own cranky purposes, while nominating a
Labor candidate for Flinders (Vic.) took
the opportunity to remark that peace with
a British victory is impossible, and to recom-
mend an immediate cessation of hostilities.
If there is no British victory then the cessa-
tion can only be arrived at on the Hun’s
terms; either our side is to cease fighting
and let the Hun steal and kill as he pleases,
or else the Hun must be asked what he will
take to cease lighting likewise. TlTh condi-
tions, in his hunger for colonies, will doubt-
less include the handing over -of Australia.
So the authors of that Labor pronouncement
imply their willingness to give their country
to Prussian militarism, oppression, conscrip-
tion, plunder, murder and rape, even if they
are involved in the common destruction.
Whether this is treason or not, it is a
remarkably good imitation of madness. A
branch of the Labor party (Bendigo West)
has denounced enlistment for local defence as
an “artful dodge” on the part of the Defence
Department to get rid of superfluous uni-
forms. _ So apparently even a very small
and mild and purely voluntary effort to pro-
vide against a possible landing by the beloved
Ilun may fall under suspicion. A big Vic-
torian union (the Railways) has given a
straight-out vote for the repudiation of the
public debt —a debt which Labor Govern-
ments and Labor leaders have been most
active in incurring. Apparently the fact
that repudiation would make a bad wreck of
savings banks and I'fe insurance offices and
thus bring down the roof on the head of
Labor itself doesn’t matter so long as there
is the joy of smashing something. If Neb©
did burn Rome, which is uncertain, he is at
least alleged to have supplied an accompani-
ment of pleasant music, but these proposals
haven’t even a touch of melody to redeem
them. The representatives of Labor in N. S.
Wales have issued a blighting curse upon the
Holman Ministry for not finding still more
loan mqpey, and has incidentally stated that
money ought to be borrowed in abundance
without interest, and that it knows, but
doesn’t explain, how the marvel can be done.
The Labor leaders have decided' that if a
number of law-breakers are released they
may look with more favor on voluntary
enlistment to keep the Hun from their
doors and the doors of their wives' and
daughters’ bedrooms, but there was no wild
enthusiasm about the business. Labor-leader
Storey, of X. S. Wales Assembly, having
attended a P.L.L. meeting, found himself
called upon by a large and hostile party—
but not a majority—to "‘explain” his conduct
in delivering speeches in favor of voluntary
recruiting. Not compulsory recruiting, be
it noted, but the of Australians
without any eomnulsion to defend their
homes and their liberties. The advocate of
compulsory recruiting goes out of the politi-
cal Labor organisations on his ear, and there
is evidently a gang of tyrants in the move-
ment which would gladly send the voluntary
man after him ; for though free speech is a
priceless possession, the pricelessness only
applies when the speaker freely speaks the
way the gang wants him to.

The Insurance of Soldiers.
A bequest is made to the public to transfer
current insurance bonuses to a fund for
having policies for married men who will
g,»"to the Front. The Bulletin long ago
suggested that this should be done; but tne
time was then not ripe. It is now. The
time is ripe now for almost anything that will
bring in a few more volunteers. Rightly,
no doubt, an insurance office will not issue
at the old rate a new policy to a man wno
is just off to the trouble, it asks, as a rule,
a premium of 10 per cent.—that is to say,
a policy for £5OO costs £5O a year—and very
few married men can afford it out of their
military pay. In any case to ask a married
man either to go away uninsured or to pay
£lO per cent, for insurance is not a fair
thing. And because he knows it is not a
fair thing many a married man who is
thoroughly lit for military service stays at
home. We are, however, less concerned
about him than about the one who goes. It
is a crying shame that he and his family
.should be penalised. Surely he and they
suffer enough Without this! ‘lnsurance ought
to be provided, not only for the married men
with dependants who will go, but equally
for those who have gone: there certainly
should be no bonus paid for hanging back.
But who should pay? The hands of the
Government are full; and they have not
been skilful hands either. Besides, apart
from this, there is plenty yet for that autho-
rity to do: some of the pensions, for in-
stance, are a shameful travesty upon the
fair thing. If it is done at all this insur-
ance must be done by the citizens individu-
ally ; and there is no way in which it can
be done with less effort than by the transfer
of bonuses. These are moneys which are
not once in a hundred cases handled by the
policy-holder at the time they are declared ;
and, in the most literal way, nobody will
feel a penny the poorer for the sacrifice.
Apart from the most excellent patriotic work
which this disposition of bonuses will do, the
question arises whether surrender is not
merely a fair thing. Three-fourths of the
policies current in Australia are mutual;
the bulk of the others arc co-operative. Can
such policy-holders properly take advantage
of the fact that they insured before, their
society became aware of one of the great
pests of the world? As a matter of hard
fact, the net premium rates have been too
low all along. For years past, while the
preparations for war were in progress,
policy-holders should have been accumulat-
ing a great reserve so that men who go to
the Front, married or unmarried, might get
insurance at the ordinary rate. A nation
at war is one of the risks of the world that
had been overlooked by the actuaries; and,
as a result, their figures have been wrong,
arid either the gross premiums have been
too low or the bonuses distributed have been
too large. To-day we have to get out of a
difficulty thus created as best We can. Which
all means that it is very doubtful indeed
whether policy-holders are morally entitled
to their bonuses —even to some of the past
bonuses. If they compromise by surrender-
ing their current bonuses it is the least they
can do.
The Armies Go Into Dock.
The last seven days in France have been
rather tamer. The German machine has
been in the repair shop. But there seems to
be no doubt that it will soon be set rolling
again; and, meantime, the halt is not
at all unwelcome to the Allies. While one
side has been getting ready to punch and the
other to parry, the war c- rrespondents, offi-
cial commentators and politicians have been
busy making calculations of losses and of
the number of divisions available to the
enemy, and giving assurance of the army’s
complete confidence in its ability to hold the
German down. The arithmetic may be all
right, but similar statistical information in
the past has proved to have been so hope-
lessly unreliable that the safer thing to do
now is to prepare for something worse. If it be
true that the German losses in recent battles
have amounted to 500,000 and the Allies’ to
250,000, and i" it be also true that the fight-
ing is to,rage all summer, we may certainly
expect something to break soon afterwards.
The tale that there is confidence among
the Allied armies is probably more entitled
to belief than anything else. _ If there was
no confidence —well, the armies would not
be there. But the German is in all likeli-
hood being told the same thing with just as
much reason. So. long as Von llindenbukg
goes on delivering these fearsome blows and
gathering up shattered divisions and throw-
ing them back into the furnace it can be
taken as reasonably certain that the old
man is as confident as anyone else. What
this paper would like to bean something
from the inside about is whether behind the
confidence of the Allied armies there is satis-
faction with the rate of shipbuilding and
certainty of the submarine being licked by
the increase of tonnage. The hopes of the
Allies rest as much upon the sea as upon
land, and will continue to c&o so until Uncle
Sam gets right into the fray. The sooner
he is there the better. While they have been
waiting for him the Allied armies have been
asked to. carry almost more than flesh and
blood: can stand.
The price-fixing enthusiast h.as lately been
varying his yell. Mostly his trouble seems
to be that the fixing works tVie wrong way
and the consumer is getting smitten more
heavily than ever. The most noticeable
result is that the fixers are. busy all the
morning demanding that the price of some-
thing be "regulated” and all the afternoon
denouncing the .price that was "regulated”
for something else. Last week a lift was
officially given to the wholesale price of
butter in Victoria and the Speck from 14!)s.
4d. to 102 s. per cwt. The noise that fol-
lowed was like all the dairy utensils on earth
falling down a shaft. The explanation given
of this fixation was that as about a quarter
of the butter required weekly for consump-
tion in those two States has to be brought
from Bananaland, the cost of freight mint
be added. This means presenting the butter-
dealer with a bonus by allowing him to col-
lect freight on one box out of four and. get
away with bunce on the other three. As a
sample of how price-fixing can badly beat
the public these proceedings are enti tied to
admiration. Yet the p<H>ple who wer,<e most
noisily protesting against the increase were
frantically demanding that further experi-
ments in fixation should be started. The
B<sxetin takes the opportunity t.o again
protest against the public buing biuuboozled
by these rule-of-thumb economists. There
is only one way by which State pauce-fixing
can be anything except a. worse form of
robbery than the community has always
endured, and that is by the State taking
control at the point of production. This
has been proved all the world over. The
notion that the State can step in at any
stage and put prices on any idling and every-
thing without making a mesai is as grotesque
as the other one that bee: iuse s. shortage
exists in any commodity ti'ie State should
push up quotations.
