From Petrograd :
Search of the Foreign. Office archives has afforded
some interesting reading. We. now learn that Russia
under the Czar had arranged a secret treaty with
Germany which practically abrogated the provisions
of Russia’s treaty with France. The person most
responsible for the treachery was the slimy but able
Von Tschirsky, who was the Hun Foreign Minister at
the time, and in August, 1914, was the Kaiser’s
Ambassador at Vienna. Von Tschirsky had a hand
in framing Austria’s ultimatum to Servia, and it was
probably Iris secret expectation that Russia would
not fight that influenced: him in having'the terms of
that document stiffened. New light is available, too,
on the late monarchy’s curious tolerance of Constan-
tine of Greece and its support of that traitor in his
refusal to participate in the Dardanelles attack. The
Czar’s Foreign Minister, Sazonoff, indeed, stipulated
that, should Greece go in (it would be against Russia’s
wishes if she did), the Greek troops, in the event of
victory, were not to be allowed near Constantinople.
Incidentally, the Eurasian Government’s advices make
it tolerably clear that the Gallipoli venture was
never nearer success than at the time of the evacua-
tion. Russia knew , (and the archives show it) that
the Turkish Government considered the situation so
desperate that it wras preparing to bolt with all its
papers, harems and other valuables to Asia Minor.
Winston Churchill was probably aware of this when
he made a prophecy of sudden victory qt the Dar-
danelles, which almost all the English papers ridi-
culed in strong language. But if Churchill knew
something, the Intelligence Department of the British
War Office was, as usual, profoundly ignorant; and
Russia was not at all anxious that it should be
anything else.
From- the Gallery : —-
AVith Watt at the helm, the Commonwealth is
behaving like a punt on a lagoon. There is hardly
a ripple on the surface, except when a Tudorite
swoons at the pleasure of being treated again like a
human being. Both Cook and Hughes were more
inclined to hand thistles and brambles than olive
branches to the Opposition; wherefore the change to
Watt, with an unwonted tact softening his natural
belligerence, is something to be profoundly grateful
for. That Watt is willing to conciliate and concede
in order to develop a desire for work is shown by
his readiness to afford information on subjects about
which Hughes pretended to be a Safe Deposit. More-
over, his gentle reproof of Nationalists who make
personal disparagements in brief exclamations is an
earnest of his desire to get ahead and do things. A
pity that some of the Opposition were not more
responsive to his overtures ! We still get jeers from
Brennan, fleers from Considine and fears from Higgs.
Higgs doubtless does much of his burrowing in the
public interest; but his elaborate air of suspicion,
which he mitigates with an apologetic manner, is
more dangerous to political serenity than the ordi-
nary jeering and fleering from the other side.
The sugar-growers have been getting £2l
per ton and they say that they won’t be able
to make ends meet for next crop at less than
£24 per ton. This is set down to a lot of
things—increased cost of imported machinery
being one and increased wages another.
Certainly the Queensland wages boards were
fairly liberal in the matter of wages. One
crowd says this was because of the increased
cost of living, which is quite obvious; and
another crowd says that in addition to this
the Queensland authorities felt gay and
cheerful about wages, seeing that the Fed-
eral Government, which is Australia, which
is mostly not Queensland, was paying for
the sugar. Anyhow, the Federal authority
has much surplus sugar on hand, and at
present rates it seems to have cost a good
deal more than the price outside. This
doesn’t prove that wages are too high, but
it does prove that it would be a good thing
to have a big manufacturing population here,
so that, machinery could be made locally
instead of costing almost its weight in silver,
and so that the sugar would have a bigger
local market.
S. Australia thinks it might build ships,
too ; but this is the position. The Federal
Government offers £2B per ton for new steel
ships—£2B,ooo for a thousand-ton vessel.
There is, however, no knowing what wages
will be, as the industrial laws are rather
helpless, and as for the cost of imported
materials no one knows what a day may
bring forth. The shipbuilders reckon that
bookmaking is a safer business, for the race
is over in three or four minutes, while they
are invited to bet on a 12 months’ race, the
odds being subject to any increase in jockeys'
remuneration and the price of horse-feed
while the animal is running. They there-
fore ask that they should be to some extent
on day labor like their employees, instead
of wholly on piecework. Hut no arrange-
ment has been arrived at. The Government,
whether Federal or State, puts its foot down
in most cases only to take it up again, but
in the matter of shipbuilding by Private
Enterprise its foot remains down, and in
the matter of shipbuilding by Public Enter-
prise its foot is up all the time.
Labor in Victoria, having at last decided
that a pledge is a pledge and no more, hopes
in future to avoid cataclysms within the
party owing to disagreements between the
head* and the tail. The recent State con-
ference firmly threw out a proposition which
sought to bind its candidates, if returned to
Parliament, to accept any later alteration to
the platform as part of the policy on which
they were elected. If they want anything
more than that they must adopt the principle
of the recall. They must, that is. if they
want men and not jellyfish to be their candi-
dates.
“Steel,” talking of the hold the German steel-
makers had on Australia, said (B. 25/4/’18)
that “the Silverton Tramway Co. bad a
number of truck bodies held up for months
awaiting axles to replace some ordered from
Germany, but undelivered owing to the
war.” The co. has made available corres-
pondence which shows that the axles were
ordered to British specification on July 13,
1914. and were placed for manufacture by
Taylor Bros, and Co., an English firm. The
co., The Bulletin is assured, lias used
nothing of Krupp’s manufacture for the past
20 years—which suggests that, unlike a lot
of other people, the Silverton Tramway Co.
didn’t need a war to make it decently
patriotic.
George Fitz Patrick. of the Voluntary
Workers’ Assoc, (Sydney) :
Anyone who wants to give away empty bottles of
any sort or clean tins with lids can find my place
dead easy. It is on the sth floor of Eastwood Cham-
bers, 19a Elizabeth-street. The empties are sold
for the benefit of the soldiers’ home-building scheme.
There is only one stipulation: they must be clean.
There is no substitute for hard work,
after all. As one ship after another quitted
the coast on war service those that remained
were spread out thinner; fast were
expected to be in two places at once and
decrepit ships were urged to renew their
youth. Now. Mackay (Q.) reports that,
with its immediate surroundings, it requires
500 tons of food weekly, that its scrap of
railway only connects with sugar-growing
districts like itself, that man can’t live by
sugar alone, and that the 25 tons of steam-
boat space allotted to it is a mockery. Other
places will doubtless be heard from shortly.
The same day that Mackay’s empty stomach
was published it transpired;—
That the U.S. had launched 41,105 tons of new
ships for the week.
That the launchings are now at the rate of 54,000
tons per week.
That Liverpool has just launched the biggest
wooden ship in the world, and is rushing the con-
struction of six other timber monsters.
That a Texas (U.S.) firm reckons to be able to
begin and finish a ship inside a month.
Also it transpired :
That “consideration is being given’’ (bv Acting
Navy Minister Povnton) to the construction of three
ships at Sydney.
That at Melbourne thd Shipbuilding Dispute Court
“has been sitting for some time considering the
application of the Ironworkers Assistants’ Union, - ’ and
is “now considering the decision.” The matter of the
Boilermakers Society comes next, and then the dis-
pute between the Engine Drivers and Firemen’s Assoc,
anil the Electrical Trades Employees Assoc, will be
dealt with.
Which is all glad news for Mackay.
Australia lost one of its oldest native-born
when George Richard Tuckwell shifted camp
the other day. He arrived in Parramatta
on January 10, 1825, his parents being also
native-born. His grandfather, Richard
Tuckwell. came to Botany Bay as Q.M.S.
iii the First Fleet and married a daughter
of Samuel a’Beckett, the surveyor who laid
out the village of Parramatta, then the seat
of government. George started to battle
with the wolf when he was eight years old,
and was still carrying on sturdily when he
was SO. For many years lie raised fruit
and a family in the Castle Hill district.
With the family he was so successful that
he left 53 grandchildren and 64 great-grand-
children.
Clarence Wallach. who died of wounds in
France lately, was the Hfth of six brothers
successively known as “Doss” at Sydney
Grammar, where they all made a name as
athletes. After leaving school, Clarence
played Rugby with Eastern Suburbs (Syd-
ney), and represented Mil State against
Queensland, and Australia against Maori-
land. He enlisted as a. private in time to
go through the Gallipoli tragedy, and got a
commission in France. Mention in despatches
and a Military Cross came later, and when
the Last Post sounded, at 28, he was wear-
ing a captain’s stars. The other five brothers
are all in khaki; one of them wears the
M.C., and another has just got back after
Spending nine months in a Ilun prison.
Tallied his last wether, Sydney Smith, of
Alavina, Vic. Western District —notable
chiefly because he was son of the late John
Thomas Smith, the Dick Whittington of
Melbourne. Sydney himself was a “sport-
ing” old lad who crammed a lot of lively
experiences into his generous span of years.
Gone out to wounds in France, Clarence
Daly, who Nvas 28, a D.S.O. and a lieut.-
eolonel. He was a captain in the City of
Melbourne infantry, joined the 6th, and was
at Gallipoli, where he acquired a wound and
his majority. In France he gained another
wound, his lieut.-colonelcy, mention in des-
patches and the D.S.O. He continued to
serve brilliantly until wounded a third time ;
then a brave soul went west.
At Sydney Quarter Sessions last week
James .Tones was awarded six months for
assaulting James Jones and robbing him of
15s. 4d. James .Tones missed the chance of
pleading that he really thought he was
reproving his own wicked flesh and helping
himself to his own small change.
In Queensland now they call Hughes’s
Egg Police the German Band. Because they
are playing “Der Watch on der Ryan.”
A chance revolver-shot has given Adelaide
a brief sensation. Hocking, a well-known
builder, noticed the window open in a next-
door house which he had been asked to keep
an eye upon. He armed himself, went in.
found a man under a bed and led him off.
At the gate the stranger shook off the hold
and bolted. Hocking, after calling to him
to stop, took a chance shot. The measured
distance was over 50 yards, yet the single
bullet went through his brain. The shooter
was detained by the police, but released by
the Coroner, who gave him complete exonera-
tion. There were, said Dr. Ramsay Smith,
the full elements of justifiable homicide:—
Deceased had committed a felony. Hocking had
legal authority to arrest him, and did 50....
Deceased escaped and fled....and. if not for the
firing, would have escaped.... It was killing in the
process of the legal duty required of a citizen in
carrying out his obligations towards the State.
It happened on Saturday at midday, and
the Saturday-night papers, which are a
feature of Adelaide, had the town humming
with the affair all next day. Obviously, what
they got was a version gleaned in a hurry by
a reporter from the friends of the shooter:
but they had the character of the deceased
nicely blackened as a burglar before his
case could be judged by any court.

Tchekoff, Tolstoi and the other Russian
dramatists must look to their laurels; John
Cosgrove in collaboration with Ben .1. Fuller
has given them something to think about.
“Rasputin,” produced at Sydney Opera
House on Saturday to an audience which
found the monk’s villainies humorous, be-
longs to the historical school of tragedy.
Historians of the future will no doubt learn-
edly discuss and annotate the origins of this
great tragedy: all that is apparent now is
that the two playwrights have examined
every history of the famous monk appearing
in the popular magazines, and have not dis-
dained to visit the picture-shows to gather
their material. On several occasions, how-
ever, the conscientious fellows have sacrificed
drama in order to preserve historical truth.
There are front scenes in which the char-
acters tell each other of happenings else-
where that would be much more exciting to
watch, while the monk Teofan’s frequent
libations and the Naked Believers are not
exciting at all. The Naked Believers scene
is about as startling and undraped as a
suburban wedding in a good Methodist
family. But the variety turn that follows —
living statuary groups—is both pleasing
and artistic. Though it seems like sacrilege
to suggest any mutilation of this pains-
taking masterpiece of John Cosgrove and
Ben J. Fuller, “Rasputin” would be a
greater popular success if 75 per cent, of the
talky front scenes was cut, if Teofan
(overplayed by John Cosgrove) was less
frequently drunk and refrained occasionally
from shouting his asides, if the Czar of
Russia (Arthur Orbell) grew a better beard,
if Rasputin (George Cross) occasionally
mislaid his chuckle and the dragging wail
in his voice and preached much less, and if
Tuffcheff (Harry McDonna) worked a
little less energetically to extract laughs
out of a part that is singularly lacking in
anything but barks. Roland Conway makes
a manly I’rinceski, Rutland Beckett an
excellent Sergeantski, and Collett Dobson .
a quite possible German spyski, who, out
of deference to the dramatists, wears his
German uniform and bis Kaiser whiskers
in Petrograd during the war. Dunstan Webb
is an ideal warder in the Siberian scenes.
Clare Duggan, Elsie Prince, Edna Waters
and the other ladies are efficient in colorless
parts. The stage settings and effects are
excellent.

“Time, Please,” is at eight o’clock on
Saturday evening, at Sydney Tivoli. The
promising list of principals includes the ever-
reliable Barry Lupino and Espinosa, the
dancer from the World. Vaudeville holds
the stage until Friday.
That great and sincere reformer the mov-
ing-picture man knows how hungry people
are for the Great Moi-al Lesson. So we have
the Thaw tragedy hashed up at Sydney Royal
as “Redemption,” with Evelyn Thaw play-
ing Evelyn Thaw. The photography is good
and the religious picture-plotter makes what
he considers a happy ending by marrying
the son of the wronged lady to the daughter
o: the man who made all the trouble.
The Scarlet Troubadours are the third of
the series of entertainers who have come to
Sydney Playhouse to carry on its new tra-
ditions. The Scarlets, an essentially' Aus-
tralian company, arrive with a record of 25
weeks at Melbourne. They offer some bright
voices and dull moments. Hylda Shannon,
Linda Bradford, Maxine Mackenzie and Ivy
Davis are attractive comedians and good-to-
fair operatic singers; and Reg Hawthorne,
in light comedy and song, and Roland Henry,
in burlesque, lead a company that is larger
than usual in this class of entertainment. The
programme ranges from classical-opera selec-
tions to crude burlesque; but even at its
crudest the company dresses its scenes
attractively. A change of programme will
occur every Thursday.

Olga Petrova, having gone in for medi-
cine and obtained her degree, masquerades
as Laurie Carlisle, M.D., in the Sydney
Crystal Palace story called “The Light
Within.” Undisturbed by the possession of
a husband and a lover and beautiful clothes,
she pursues and runs to earth a meningitis
serum and another for the cure of anthrax
When she is not wrapped up in science she
“RASPUTIN,” AT SYDNEY OPERA
HOUSE.
Rasputin (George Cross) hypnotises the
Czar.
is looking after her little son, quarrelling in
dignified fashion with her husband, renew-
ing an old love and generally enjoying a
crowded hour. Julian Eitinge as “The
Clever Mrs. Carfax” scores a triumph. The
story is spirited, and Eitinge makes his
female quite a charming person to
meet. Hoyt’s and the Hay market harbor
this double. “The Hopper” is the Strand’s
trump for seven days. A Ming vase and a
Japanese plate form the groundwork for a
family quarrel, only patched up after many

sional bqrglar with a nice manner.
Alexander Watson, with his tall, gaunt
dignity more marked, and his voice richer,
and more versatile than ever, recited all sorts
of lines for two hours at Melbourne Athe-
naeum Hall last Saturday. A formal arm-
chair on the platform and a pair of curtains
at the back were the only accessories to his
entertainment, to w’hieh a crowded audience
did honor as of old. Watson was stirringly
descriptive in “The Guard Came Through,”
by Conan Doyle, and a “Ballad of the War-
time Trawlers,” by Capt. Ilepworth. whose
verse is Kiplingesque. He dropped into
humorous blokeiness when giving ’em
Dennis’s “Singing Soldiers,” which is terse
in construction, and Kipling’s prose yarn of
“My Lord, the Elephant,” where the drollery
might be more condensed. And he was
grandly declamatory in Tennyson’s “Ode on
the Burial of Wellington.” But this
poem is not sufficiently informative to the
modern audience. It takes for granted (mat
everybody is acquainted with all the main
incidents of Wellington’s career—as in the
lines beginning “Who. is lie that cometh?”
where it assumes general knowledge of the
fact that the old Duke came to be placed
in the crypt of St. Paul next to Nelson. But,
as a subject for the reciter to spread his
talent on, the Ode left a deep impression. Alex-
ander Watson changes his programme every
night, and must keep Heaven knows how
many tens of thousands of lines green in his
memory. To-night (Thursday) he gives all
the Ddtheboys Hall scenes from “Nicholas
Nickleby.”

Guy Bates Post,
in spite of liis un-
happy name, ap-
pears to be a grow-
ing attraction at
Melbourne King’s.
“The Masquerader”
is an oddly inter-
esting play that
improves on ac-
“The Masquerader ,”
at Melbourne King’s.
Guy Bates as his
double, John Loder.
qunintanee, being full of matter and
crowded with acting opportunities. The
story becomes almost credible under Post-
treatment of the two characters —the
morphia-maniac and the Masquerader who
survives in his stead. With the aid of an
efficient “double” and some tricky stage-man-
agement the actor realises his dual imperso-
nation of the Chilcote who dies and the
man who lives to marry the widow of de-
ceased without letting the world know that
he wasn’t her own lawful Number 1, but
really her Number 2. For the success of
this pious fraud the drama and Miss Ruby
Gordon share credit with Guy. Miss Gordon
as the servant girl at Loder’s lodgings, seems
to have a genuine belief that the bedrugged
Chilcote is the lodger she had humbly
adored, and when at last she addresses his
corpse as ‘Mr. Loder” the idea of getting it
buried in the living man’s name strikes the
loyal Brock irresistibly. Brock’s simple
loyalty to the Chilcote dynasty is the main-
stay of the plot. To “preserve the family
honor ’ he rushes Loder into a masquerade
which is to be only temporary; and with
the blessed family honor still in view he
dictates the death certificate that will make
the masquerade a permanent institution. In
of its three acts “The Masquerader” is
a capital play when one gets used to the
audacity of the plot. And in the garden
scene it would be not too bad if Miss Ritchie
were the right sort of actress for the char-
acter of the minor morphia maniac who has
led Chilcote astray. But she isn’t. Chil-
cote’s evil-designing mistress amounts to a
big blot on the performance. Except that
she wears golden hair the fair impersonatress
of the difficult little part doesn’t offer one
a notion of it. _ On the other hand, Miss
Thais Lawton is persuasive in the very
difficult character of Chilcote’s wife, and
would be almost convincing if she spoke a
little faster and were less prone to the use
of an artificial tremor. A condition of sup-
pressed hysteria is natural to Mrs. Chilcote
under the circs., and every lovely woman
is expected to assume more emotion than she
feels when engaged in wheedling. But Mrs.
C. is a practical sort of person in some
respects, and after six years of disappoint-
ment with John she wouldn’t be always
tremoring in her speech with him.

At Melbourne Royal nobody will be able
to hang his harp on The Willow Tree after
Friday. It is a pretty little show with
beautiful, up-to-date lighting effects, and
only a little too much profusion of artificial
florality in the foreground. But the per-
formance never grips tin* public ilnagina-
tion. It never even catches fleeting fancy
by the toe. The figure first presented is an
unmistakable doll, and its association with 1
the live figure of Miss Macdonell is plainly
theatrical. The play is a very poor plagiar-
“Pygmalion and Galatea.” A revival
of “Outcast” on Saturday will run for a
week. And then “Peter Pan.”
5k 5k s[:
“Samples,” at Melbourne Tivoli, has
arrived at the last-nights period of its life,
when kind friends are expected to gather
round the bedside and soothe the end of the
dear old thing.
Billie Seaton, accompanied by her clever
pianist, is still laboring songfully at Mel-
bourne Bijou; also Bertram and Raymond
are persisting with their strange present-
ment of Australian up-country character.
Among the newcomers Leywood and Norell,
formerly of the Tiv., are bright and amusing
in a conventional kind of sketch. Baron
does well enough in the ventriloquial line,
whilst Plirozo, a figure introduced by a lady,
is alleged to be a mystery. A rather frosty
mystery, anyhow.
The brooding mystery of Egypt lingers
over “The Price Mark,” featuring Dorothy
Dalton, at Hoyt’s in Melbourne this week.
The yarn begins with a colored affaire in
Cairo, out of which springs an avenger with
a long-distance Hate who pokes his knife
opportunely into the conclusion. The second
film, “Clever Mrs. Carfax,” is mostly Julian
Eitinge in one of his adroit female imperso-
nations. Julian figures this time as a bogus
presswoman who lures a crook to his doom b r
a fraudulent smile.
“Shirley Kaye” is throwing Clara
Kimball Young into fashionable relief at
Melbourne Majestic this week. As a dash-
ing young debutante in High Finance, Clara
bests opponents with an ease that adds to
the sting of her victories. In “I Love You,”
which follows, Alma Rubens inspires love
in three men, only to discover that there is
n.i luck in odd numbers beyond one. Her
trouble is that an indiscreet past threatens
to lap over and submerge her present; but
Providence, which loves a cheerful liver,
saves her Good Name before the last reel
runs out.
** * *
A first-in-AuStralia trial has been given in
Adelaide to two plays that are fresh here
rather than new anywhere. “Her Wedding
Day,” at the Tivoli, is sound, old-fashioned
melodrama. Ida Gresham is the usual hero-
ine without a backbone, and the villainous
Fitzherbert finds two obstacles between them,
lie switches off Guy Hastings without much
difficulty, but the girl merely marries Dunbar
instead. However, he gambles, and is there-
fore easily ruined by any villain. After
which all the characters are removed to
Cuba, where ,the Spariish-American fight
straightens, things out. The other play is
on the screen at West’s, “The Zeppelin’s
Last Raid,” a thrilling thing. The bombing
scenes are wonderfully faked, and the up-
in-the-air details seem genuine.
How good a show-town Adelaide now is
may be judged by dropping in, any “off”
afternoon, at the Majestic and seeing the
house packed by the Fuller pantomime, as
it has been twice a day for a fortnight.
Where all the people come from during busi-
ness hours is their own business. The house
has now gone back to variety. The Royal
also has had three big weeks of “So Long.
Lctty,” the Boccaccio story with nearly all
its teeth drawn, and similar frivolities.
Maud Fane is always entertaining. Connie
Ediss is a fine actress underneath the exag-
gerations that get the easy laugh, Madge
Elliott a delightful dancer, and Harry
Wotton (a local man) a good actor who is
getting a chance at last. “Joan the Woman”
is a stirring picture at Wondergraph, and
Kipling and O. Henry make a good combi-
nation at the Pav.
Another widow is blessing the N. S.
Wales Testator’s Family Maintenance Act —
and probably bracketing with it the name of
Arthur Griffith, who never let this question
rest until there was a law on the subject.
When Thomas Howlett, grazier, of Bre-
warrina, died in 1916 he left an estate worth
£8894 and a will which cut it up amongst
his five children. For the good wife and
mother who had never had a health trip or
a holiday in all her 19 years of married life
or any money to spend on herself there was
this: “I have intentionally omitted my wife,
Margaret Howlett, from any benefit under
this my last will, owing to her having aban-
doned and neglected to take care of my
children, although I had made provision for
her doing so.” His Honor Street summed
it up as “an undeserved slur cast upon a
good mother by an ill-tempered husband.”
pointed out that she had helped to make the
money and made an order that the widow
should have £2OO for this year, with annual
rises of £25 until £3OO is reached. Memo
for the Dying: Do the decent thing; if you
don’t, the Law Will Do It for You.

“Sliprail”: In the wild, roaring days of
the first week of Anzac half a dozen tearful
Turk prisoners were momentarily expecting
to he cooked for breakfast. Suddenly their
outlook on life brightened; their sentries
became affability itself and the grub was
such as a brigadier alight feel peckish at. In
GIVING IT A NAME.
“That silly ass persists In calling mg tur-
keese ring a toorquwaaz. What do you
call it, Ikenstein?"
“Veil, my poy, speakin' as an egspert, I call
it glass.”
the fulness of time—about three days—our
deep scheme was deemed mature and at dusk
the sentries hurriedly took the prisoners
around to one of the flanks and,
pointing the way back to their own
lines, gave them a start off. That
night the staff was jubilantly poking
itself in the ribs and gleefully making pre-
parations for a great influx 6f voluntary
prisoners. From-his dug-out one of the offi-
cers espied at daylight the next morning the
same old six prisoners laboriously trudging
towards their prison camp, each smilingly
carrying an enormous bundle of firewood!
“Mac-ko” : The first Sunday at sea on our
trooper, Meekun, the C. of E. padre, was
still unfit for duty. So the Colonel offered
to officiate., and all those of us who hadn’t
a valid excuse were lined up in the saloon.
The O.C. opened the prayer-book, twisted
the ends of his moustache, glared round at
the congregation and waded in: “ ‘Dearly
beloved, the Scripture movetli us’—Ser-
geant, who’s making that row at the back?
See to those men behind there, and keep ’em
quiet.” Some chaps who had escaped the
roping-in were having a small game of crown
and anchor on one of the hatches; and their
voices filtered through, and mingled with
the O.C.’s powerful exhortations: “What’s
the little di done? What’s the name of the
game done? Leaving the' little heart for
the old man? Two boh a tail—two bob a
tail. I’ll bet 10 bob he nobs ’em.” Any lull
in the proceedings was brisked up by another
card party: “I’ll make it two bob for cards”
—“four bob” —“what ’ave yer got? Take
it, Bill, the dog’s yours.” “Sergeant!”
roared the temporary parson, “what creed
are those men up there? Here am I tear-
ing my throat to ribbons preaching the Gos-
pel, while those illegitimates are disturbing
the Sunday peace. Go up. Sergeant, and
take their blanky names. They’ll fetch 10
bob each.” Then, catching an amused gleam
in the one and several eye of his congrega-
tion —“E-rrr-hup-rrr Dismiss !”

The Board of a N. S. Wales country
hospital, two days drive from the nearest
railway and Sawbones, not long ago handed
the matron her travelling ticket. Minister-
ing angels are scarce in that vicinity and
this one had capably run the show single-
handed, converting herself several times a
day into everything from resident doctor to
scullerymaid. The chief witness against
her, when asked by the Board if the matron
habitually drank, guardedly replied: “She
may or may not.” Recently the Board got
hogged in trouble, and the late matron’s
genius was recalled. She was appealed to
and asked to advise if a certain course
would meet the difficulty. Back came the
reply: “It may nr may not.”

“.Country Ped.” : The Bulletin (11/4/TS)
asks: “Should the child come within the
influence of professional
distributors of ‘know-
ledge’ before he reaches
the age at which the
great majority leave
school nowadays?” As
a “professional distri-
butor of ‘knowledge’ ” I
know that if a child
(what w r e teachers term
an' average child) comes
to school at eight in-
stead of the usual six,
that child takes 12
months to reach Grade
111 instead of two
years, and would very
likely reach Grade IV
within the period only
for the time wasted in
learning fripperies. I
have one such —a girl—
in my school at the pre-
sent time. She will be
in Grade IV in less
than six months’ time,
so reaching in 21
months what in the
ordinary course takes
three years. This one
example (and there are-
many similar) serves to
show that children can
start school later than usual and get through
the State-school programme by 14. Then
why should they have to start so young?
“Bion”: “Bombardier” (B. 25/4/T8)
makes a poise, rightly or wrongly, because
lie had to pay a proportion of the value of
stores, etc., lost and damaged on the voyage.
As a late member of the crew of a transport
I resent his implication that the blankets
found their way to the crew’s quarters, be-
cause they “could not possibly be stowed in
a man’s kitbag.” Blankets do disappear,
and there is no mystery about the cause. A
few are dumped with the idea of drowning
the inhabitants, and I have seen dozens torn
up to make mops for the hospital; but the
majority are blown overboard in windy
weather. As to whether the troops should
pay I say nothing, though I imagine fair
wear and tear is allowed the owners in the
price fixed under the charter. I doubt whether
there are more losses and breakages im a
ship when used as a transport than when
she is in the ordinary passenger trade, and
I never heard of a special bob a head being
collected from* passengers.

lined up the other day and informed that
“whether employed actively or department-
ally the principle is the same. They are
soldiers, and as such are expected to be”
etc., etc. It is time this nonsense was
knocked on the head. Half the dreadful
muddle in the Department has this stupid
idea at the bottom of it. No private em-
ployer, requiring an efficient clerk, wants
him to be anything hot a clerk. He doesn’t
want him to be a butler, or to think of him-
self as an undertaker. When a commission
was appointed lately to inquire into the
business side of the Defence Department its
three members didn’t start (o think of them-
selves as field-marshals or colonels: they
kept in mind that they were business men,
and probably didn’t care two straws whether
they had the top button of their vest fas-
tened or not. If clerks have to be soldiers
you mus have a soldier in command of
them—and that’s where the trouble comes
in Also, that is where' inefficiency comes
in, with the muddle and waste that ineffi-
ciency means.

From a padre of very long service (he is
back at the Front again, after ai trip
home) :
I left my regiment in Flanders eight days before
my boat sailed. “Drinks all round” to wish me
luck. Then in a camp in England with its well-
run “wet” canteens. Then on a troopship sailing
under regulations framed at Australian “mothers’
meetings,” which meant that the ship was “dry.”
Men so restricted had been guilty of excesses on
previous ships at their first port of call. So every
hotel, w'as closed, just to give a monopoly to sly-
grog shops. The latter had their touts to meet
the soldiers, who had leave every day from 9 a.m.
till 11 p.m. Now a sly-grog shop makes those lads
pay dearly for their drink; the drink is usually
bad, maddening stuff; outside Australia an accom-
panying industry, of such a place is invariably a
brothel; everything is i n .favor of those brothels
being diseased brothels. Conclusion: The wowsers
of Australia are responsible for a large percentage
of the venereal disease among our soldiers. And a
Commission is sitting to determine whether the
Government, having closed the pubs, will open some
of its own and control them itself, as we do in France
and England. And some chaplains, with the
authority of their calling, backed by their pre-
sumed military experience “on active service,” are
giving evidence against at that Commission. The
military history of those persons should be known,
and it should be known if they were considered
over here to have been ever intended either by
nature or by grace to know, to influence, to help
men, Or were they considered merely “shirkers” in
khaki”? '

FRANCE— 1918.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The tangled smoke-wreaths grovel on the plain ;
They weave fantastic patterns in the rain,
And ever seem to hurry in their train
Faint echoes of a distant convent bell,
Where some old priest, made bolder with the
night,
Crawls slowly from his devastated cell,
And keeps an only half-remembered rite,
With eye cocked wary for an errant shell,
And cassock girded for an instant flight.
The bell tolls slowly, in a sober vein ;
And yet it l ings a message through the rain
Which half blots out grey Death upon the plain.
Some say it rings the end of Wrong and Sin ;
Some say it is the Herald of God’s War;
To me it sends no thoughts but those of kin.. . .
A station on the Darling ; through the door
The mellow beat of horse-bells drifting in,
Which, hearing once, I did not think of more.
The convent bells have ceased their solemn strain;
The priest lias scuttled to his lair again....
But still the horse-bells murmur in the rain.
N.S.W.

Some of Billjim’s matrimonial allies have
already reached Australia, while there are
many brides on the other side fran-
tically searching camps and interviewing
amused O.C’s. in their fruitless search for
the missing khaki owner Of an extensive emu
farm in Woolloomooloo. One of these dis-
tracted females recently found an O.C. will-
ing to listen to her tale of how she had
married an Australian soldier during his
four days’ leave. He had gone back to camp
and she insistently demanded maintenance.
To prove her story she produced a marriage
certificate verifying her story that she had
married Private Dinkum, and the ceremony
had been witnessed by Private Cobber!
“Pills”: My own experience of “barrack
room charges” is similar to “Bombardier’s.”
On our last transport over 1000 returned
soldiers were charged Is. each for shortages
and breakages on the voyage. Without com-
menting on the injustice of - this charge,
it may be of interest to the Australian pub-
lic to know that there is no record of this
transaction in the soldiers’ paybooks. The
soldier is asked to sign for 30s. received,
whereas he is handed only 295. I have
seen soldiers proffer their paybooks to have
this charge noted, but under no circumstance
apparently is it done. Now where is that
odd shilling per soldier (over £5O in this
case)recorded? The most charitable suspicion
amongst soldiers is that it is money screwed
out of troops (sick men often) to fatten fat
owners; the least charitable I won’t men-
tion. The most sinister aspect about the
whole affair is that under no circumstances
can the soldier secure a receipt; in fact, the
O.C. refuses to accept responsibility and
states that it is not a military charge,
but a shipping charge. If the latter is the
case, it is strange that the officers in com-
mand of troops risk a riot every time this
kind of charge is made, for on every occa-
sion there is opposition from the boys who
feel they are being taken down.

“0.K.” : Melbourne Sabbatarians' are col-
lecting their forces for a conflict with the
Red Cross brigade. The Red Crossers are
mostly in favor of running picture-shows on
Sunday nights for the benefit of the Cause,
whilst the wowsers protest that no cause on
earth can justify gatherings of entertain-
ment-seekers on the Sabbath. ' They approve
of special Red Cross services in church, with
incidental * collections, but screen-pictures
and payments at the door are things of the
Devil —on the Sabbath. From the cheerful
sinners’ point of view the present moment
is opportune for restoring picture-shows to
the sphere of usefulness from which they
were ejected by Obadiah and the Brethren,
and it seems likely that most of the muni-
cipal councils in the Melbourne radius will
be sufficiently wicked to sanction the new
war-charity move. Then will the wowsers
have to threaten to withdraw their patron-
age and support from the Red Cross move-
mint, in which case Obadiah, who is an
unctuous Imperialist ns a rule, will have to
be told that his piety is unpatriotic in prac-
tice and his policy on all-fours with enemy
alienism.

“Hamer” : Some, at least, of the girls in
Australia whose sweethearts have married
Englishwomen can thank the improvidence
that looks after a soldier’s mails for the
tragedy. Billjim went adventuring with a
heart that remained faithful in spite of any,
or only very infrequent, written word from
his beloved ; he lived patiently through days
when there were no regular mails, and didn't
expect much correspondence. But lie wanted
something to carry on with. “Tragedies of
the Letters That Did Not Come'’ would fill
a few volumes. I know one man who used
to talk of little except his wife and child.
The sickening disappointment of mails that
brought no letters at last got him down, and
•n “a scoot” he went far over the edge.
Write, write, write!
* * * #
The N.S.W. Congregational body has
unanimously denounced the draw-
ing of lots for prizes in connection
with the War Loan, the chief
denouncer suggesting that this sort
of thing would make Gladstone
turn in his grave. Which was an
impious thing to say, for Unless
the church doctrine is a baseless
dream the revolving power of
Gladstone isn’t in the grave but
in a better land. And it was
illogical. There is an interval
between death and burial—in some
cold countries quite a lengthy one.
During this interval the turning
over of the deceased when a lottery
happened must attract attention.
Some good people—quite as good
as Gladstone —are awaiting burial
when every great Melbourne Cup
sweep or other sin takes place,
and unless they make a point of
turning when unobserved and then
righting themselves in a hurry the
matter couldn’t escape mention in
the press. So it boils down to
this: the parson or other person
who says the dead turn over when
a gamble is in progress may be a
fraud or may not, but at any rate
he is a very reckless asserter. And
if he isn’t a fraud then the revolv-
ing corpse is an ass. The careful
concealment of its protest proves
that.
The way of the reformer is hard. A
Sydney butcher tried to put the nutritious
horse into circulation as heifer, just as so
many people have for years been putting
Austi’alian boots into circulation as “im-
ported.” But by hard luck an official
noticed that the animal had more ribs than
even a young cow should have, and the Law
started to prance on its hat.

Gunner Someone from Somewhere :
We are “resting” behind the line in a little vil-
lage assisting of a church, a couple of estaminets,
a few farms and a graveyard. The graveyard con-
tains most of the population. We sleep in a barn,
rise early, feed and water the horses, have breakfast,
and at 9.30 hold a parade, which is easily the most
important function of the day. We line up -and the
sergeants report “All correct.” The Sergeant-Major
salutes the Orderly-Officer, and the Orderly-Officer
salutes the Sergeant-Major. Also he salutes the
Second-in-Command, and the Second-in-Command
salutes back. Next he salutes the Captain, and the
Captain salutes the Second, and tells him to carrv
on. Upon this the Second salutes the Orderly-
Officer, and the Orderly-Officer salutes the Second and
tells the Sergeant-Major to carry on. The Sergeant-
Major salutes the Orderly-Officer and the Orderlv-
Officer salutes the Sergeant-Major and then the
Sc-rgeant-Major tells the sergeants to carry on. Eventu-
ally the common lot do the carrying on; and so
we win the war. This saluting and springing to
attention and springing back again breaks the mono-
tony of things. When the day’s work is over we do
some more saluting and springing about, and then
we are free to leap about on our own account. If
we leap into an estaminet we buy a weird liquid
with a yellow taste. It is called beer, and is the
homely drink of the French. Nobody save a henr
would adopt it as a homely or any other kind of
drink. There is also sold the famous “vin blong,”
and every “vin blong” is worse than the last. But
there are other things to cheer us. We have a
\ .M.C.A. and a canteen. The canteen sometimes
sells good English beer; and the Y. Emma sells
candles and coffee, and the candles are not bad.

A DIRGE OF THE DEEP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The boat went up and the boat went down on the
wet, wild, lonely sea.
“Well, Bill, me lad,’’ said Joe, “this looks a dashed
rum go to me!
Bust it!” he groaned in a ghastly voice, “me feet
are soaked right through,
And never in all me born days did 1 feel so frightful
blue!”
The boat went up and the bout went down, and Bill
he gave a groan.
“In nine more months more or so,” he said, in a
mournful monotone,
“They’ll launch a ship at Williamstown—one ship
at least,” he said.
“But then, o’ course, good gum!” growled Joe,
“we’ll both be drownded dead.”
Joe eased his burnt and blistered hands and scanned
. the heaving sea.
“They said you could ha’ built ’em long and long
ago,” said he.
“The thing’s a gross and callous lie!” snapped Bill.
“Malicious, too ! —•
Completely false and groundless, Joe. Besides, it
isn’t true!”
“1 -would,” said Joe, “a ship might come a-riding
down the main
To put us off this cockle-shell on dry land once
aga-a-ain!”
The great deep heaved them up, up, u-u-up and swung
them down, until
“I wish, oil how I wish,” said Joe, “you had ha’
built ’em, Bill!”

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Reported that the Ivaiser is now com-
pletely obsessed by'the notion that he is God.
The delusion seems to be rather common
among nersons named William.
Memories of the most successful perform-
ance ever staged in the Melbourne Divorce
Court were revived by the death the other
day of Vivian Parker, who had been a sick
man for a long time. He was very popular
among those who go down to the sea in
expensive yachts, and acted as skipper yf
many of the craft, including Governor
Brassey’s Helen and Charley Wallace’s
Sayonara.
Died, as he naturally would, in an Aus-
trian prison, Gabriel Princip, a man who
didn’t make history, though he was suspected
of doing it. He was the Austrian subject
who shot the Austrian heir-apparent in Aus-
trian territory, whereupon came the ultima-
tum to Servia and the war. If Princip had
never lived the war would probably have
started because the Hun didn’t like the way
King Peter wore his hair, or, as in Belgium,
it would have started whether the King had
hair or not. For the Hun thought the time
was ripe.
THE ONLY WAY.
THE LADY AT THE REGISTRY OƑICE : “Can
you let me have a maid that I can keep
in the country?"
THE DITTO OF THE DITTO : “Not unless you
keep her in irons!"
“Mulga” Taylor, who lately climbed to
the Speaker’s chair in the Westralian Assem-
bly, is a much mellower Mulga than he was
when he went to gaol for his part in the big
Queeensland shearers’ strike. He was the
arch-militant when he first went West. Once
when someone taunted him during a party
debate with the fact that Labor had even
got a barber into Parliament (meaning
Holman, whose sign hftng out at Cue),
Mulga reefed out through his whiskers:
“Yes, we have got a barber in, and we could
get his pole in as well if we wanted to.”
A busy little woman is Sara Lewis, secre-
tary of the Melbourne Female Hotel and
Caterers’ Employees’ Union. A “domestic”
before taking over the organising, she one
night stood up in the Trades Hall and called
her fellow-delegates a lot of old fossils.
Expulsion followed, but she bobbed up again
later. Sara is a regular soap-box orator
round Melbourne, and her latest activity will
take the Trades Hall into the dining-
room. She has just succeeded in persuading
that body to help her in the job of organis-
ing Mary Ann.
The first manufacturer of gunpowder in
Australia was Robert Cooper, of Sydney,
grandfather of the present Chief Justice of
Queensland. That was in 1829 or there-
abouts.
CAIN AND ABEL.
“And Australia brought forth two sons
called Cain and Abel. And in the process
of time they made a burnt offering, each of
the fruits of his labor. Now the smoke of
Cain’s offering ran along the ground, while
that of Abel’s ascended straight up. Where-
fore Cain was wroth and compassed the
death of his brother by refusing him help in
his need. And Cain became a slave and a
driller unto the Hun."
McGarvie Smith deserved very well of his
country when, some years back, he dis-
covered that good friend of the stockman—
anthrax vaccine. Now he earns more cheers
for making a present of the formula to the
Ma State. Not only will this good Aus-
tralian not accept a copper for the .recipe
which a war patriot would have felt justified
in asking half a million for, but he is bestow-
ing his time and experience (also without
fee) in showing the Agricultural Depart-
ment’s vets how to brew the stuff.
“Groper”: The recent mention in war
items of the Military Medal being awarded
Trooper Jack Francis in France for con-
spicuous bravery, regalls what was probably
the first court-martial held at Gal-
lipoli. Joining as a farrier in the
10th Light Horse, F raneis, who
came from Narrogin (W.A.), was
supposed to be an onlooker at the
Landing. The sight of so many
getting off in the boats was. how-
ever, too much for him. And he
was presently in the thick of it.
P’our days later he was escorted on
board again under arrest for being-
in the wrong place at the right
time, and was duly court-mar-
tialled. The O.C. nearly patted
him on the head as he advised him not to do
it again. Jack was then barely 21.
Colin McCulloch, killed in action the
other day, was an old boy of the Anglican
Grammar School at North Sydney, and had
nearly finished a law course at Sydney Uni.
when ejectment proceedings against the
Kaiser began. McCulloch and some of his
mates got the date of their final exam,
advanced in order to enlist; then, having
collected his LL.B. degree, he made one
formal appearance before the Supreme Court
to be admitted to the Bar, and hopped
aboard. His first fighting was against
pneumonia in a Cold Country training-camp;
but he routed that enemy and got to France
in time for Bullecourt, where hie won a com-
mission. Wounded at PPooge, he had not
long got back from hospital when the Last
Post sounded at 25.
Ilarry Holmes, one of the few survivors
from the torpedoing of the steamer Oronsa,
is a S’Australian. —one of Jack Virgo’s young
men —who bulks large in the Y.M.C.A. For
many years he was its General Sec. at Wel-
lington (M.L.). After an Australian tour
in 1913 he became National Sec. in S’Africa,
and had charge of the work behind Botha’s
lines in the German East-African campaign.
Later he toiled in France', and just before
he dodged D. Jones was expounding to Uncle
Sam concerning Y.M.C.A. matters and the
wah generally.
Lieut. Archie Fisken, a cousin of the late
Captain Harold Wanliss; a Ballarat boy
who won the D.S.O. in his first action, has
contributed a Military Cross to the family
record. He is a nephew of Chief Justice
Irvine, whose wife is a sister of the mothers
of these two soldiers. Archie Fisken is an
old Ballarat College and Geelong Grammar
boy.
“Is there never a Chink in the world
above?” Jean Ingelow twittered. Well, the
chances are in favor of it now that Louis
Ah Mouy has gone west at the age of 92.
A builder by trade,
he came in from
China in 1851 under
contract to erect
houses for Captain
Gle n d e nning, the
master of the ship
in which he trav-
elled, and he claimed
to be the first Chi-
nese to land in Vic-
toria. His were
among the earliest
buildings in South
Melbourne and Wil-
liamstown. A great,
speculator, Ah Mouy
opened many mines
in the Yea district.
Marrying a woman
of his own nation-
ality, he left seven
sons, three daugh-
ters and 12 grand-
children. One son
is a lawyer, another
an architect. A
grandson is an elec-
trical engineer. Though most of the family
remain in Melbourne, others are as far apart
as Hongkong. Calcutta and America.
Frank Rolland, now Scotchbyterian padre
with Billjim in France, had plenty of harden-
ing experience before he left with the ammu-
nition waggons. During his strenuous youth
he was with the Australian Inland Mission,
having Oodnadatta as his headquarters and
living most of the time on top of a horse.
Dad was old-timer W. S. Rolland, of the
Prahran (Vic.) tabernacle.
“Mustard Gold”; The Westralian Gold-
fields warden is a tame curate compared
with Finnerty and the others who adminis-
tered mining law a few years back. Now, in
the dual capacity of Warden and R.M., his

thief. Walter, of Ivalgoorlie, catches the
odium of the well-organised fraternity for

JUSTICE
N. K. EWING,
Tas. Puisne Judge.
Gone west at 84, Michael Levy, said to be
the first regular drummer on the roads in
Australia. Pie came from London with a
shipment of merchandise 60 years ago. when
Parramatta was the terminus of the rail-
way.
“N or M’s” send-off to an old friend :
Dead at Hobart, aged 73, R. M. Johnston, Govern-
ment Statistician, a man of wide sympathies and
learning. Son of a crofter on Black Isle, Moray
Firth, he was born in a small fishing village, and
made his way with only such advantages as a strong
A SAVER.
MRS. FOOTLITES : “You don't give me
presents now like you used to before we
were married."
MR. FÒTLITES : “Now, look here, my dear,
did you ever hear tell of a fisherman
giving bait to the fish, he had caught?"
brain and a lovable disposition gave him. At the
village school the old dominie, who was proud of his
clever pupil, lent him books. Hugh Miller, stone-
mason of Cromarty, which is near by, became his
hero and exemplar in the business of winning
through; and Miller’s books had much to do with
Johnston’s early interest in geology. The ambitious
lad first became a telegraph-operator. Later he
worked on the Scottish railways, studying hard in the
evenings at the Andersonian, Glasgow. Coming to
Australia (the first vessel on which he started was
wrecked on the coast of Ireland), he was on the
railways in Victoria for a time. He crossed to Tas-
mania in ’7O, and there he found the niche from
which the offer of the position of Statistician, first
of N. S. Wales and then of the Commonwealth, could
not tempt him. Outside his bread-and-butter line he
did valuable work. Fisheries, technical education,
geology, music, sports—he was interested in all, and
was a Fellow of, many scientific societies, from the
Royal of Tasmania to the Linnsan of London. Among
his scientific, social and economic books, the “Sys-
tematic Account of the Geology of Tasmania,” pub-
lished by the State Government in ’BB, is the stan-
dard. But while this generation lasts he will be
remembered for his lovable personality as much as for
his achievements.
HIS BEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Thank God, I did my best to prevent these
atrocities.”I—The 1 — The Kaiser.
I did my best to stop them with a sword,
I called red flame to aid me in the task ;
It seemed that I had but to speak the word
And cringing nations must do all I ask.
If at the first loud warning that I gave
Armies had yielded tamely to my will,
There had not been this slaughter of the brave,
Nor all these graves behind the lines to fill.
I did my best. If they had let me" talk
And shape the world according to my plan,
These noble troops would but, have had to walk
Where lately, much to my dismay, they ran.
The world is too much with us, late and soon ;
It thrusts and bangs and will not be put out;
But had I owned the earth and sun and moon
We should have found it good without a doubt.
1 did my best. Atrocities abound
Wherever guns withstand me or the foe
Gets to close grips upon the shell-torn ground
With these'heroic souls who share my woe.
And more atrocities there yet shall be.
I feel them, feel them cold along the spine,
If these who bar our path get hold of Me
How long a rope, how short a shrift were mine!
I did my best. I yet will do my best.
Bring up the guns! Pour in the men to die!
Courage, strong souls! Within the grave is rest.
While one bold private lives we still must try.
Ach, these atrocities ! Add steel to steel!
Press home the charge no matter what the cost.
I did my best. Ach, Gott, how does it feel
To stand Chief Murderer when all is lost ?
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.
AN ATTACK OF NERVES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Captain von Salzmann, writing in the Vossische
Zeitung, mentions a few of the rumors that are
current in Berlin, and complains that the people are
beginning to lose their nerve.”
The arrogant Hun, the sneering Hun,
Who erst vos bound to vin,
And spoke of war as “lofely fun,”
Is waxing pale and thin ;
The swaggering Hun, the cheering Hun,
Has lost his fleshly curves;
Whilst thundering with his 10-mile gun
He’s wondering why the war’s not won—
The Hun who ghastly deeds has done
Has now “done in his nerves.”
The beautiful Day, the future Day,
Vhich English fools vill rue—
Der Day vhen dey der cost must pay—
Appears not yet in view.
The glorious Day, victorious Day,
Vhen Hindenburg’s reserves
Will march through London streets, and say
(Before they start to hack and slay) :
“Ve toldt you so!” in accents gay,
That promised Day has gone astray ;
The blundering Hun who vants the Sun,
And ne’er from duty sverves,
The thundering Hun we hate and shun,
Who killed the child and r.ped the nun,
Is wondering why the war’s not won—
The Hun who all things else has done
Has now “done in his nerves!”
VICTORIA.

C. A. Elliott, who) has succeeded general-
manager Teece as actuary (11. W. Apperly
has followed him as general manager), puts
the most striking feature of the year in
two or three lines:—
The war claims amount to £945,507. For the
first time in the society’s history the total death
claims experienced have exceeded the amount pro-
vided for by the table used in estimating its lia-
bilities. The largest source of surplus has thus been
cut off.
Over 25 p.c. of the claims have arisen
through the war. But that is not the only
war charge. The outgoings on account of
taxes have moved this way:—
1913 . . £36,305 1916 . . £88,344
1914 .. 47,454 1917 .. 109,534
1915 . . 57,617
This, of course, affects another source of
surplus; and that means a further reduc-
tion of bonus. The custom of the A.M.P.
has been to include taxes among the exes.
Some other Australian offices, following the
plan pretty generally adopted in England, de-
duct taxes from interest and rent earnings. On
the A.M.P.’s plan its expense rate has
moved as follows :
Expenses Compared with Total Income.

Exclusive of taxes the exes, were £7 15s. Id.
in 1913 and £b 13s. lOd. last year; so that,
from this angle, the performance of last
year looks much better than that of four
years ago.
v But the expense rate calculated on the
basis of total income is open to various
objections, the most important being that
an expense rate may be improved by adopt-
ing a policy of doing little or nothing in the
pursuit of new business. Adopting the arbi-
trary method of charging renewal premiums
with 7 p.c. for management and single pre-
miums (annuities) with 3 p.c., and assum-
ing that the balance is the cost of new
business, we get these comparative results :

In recent years 1013 showed the new busi-
ness got at the lowest cost, by this method
of reckoning. If taxes are excluded, for
that year the cost was 64 p.c., while for the
past year, on the same basis, it was only
40.7 p.c. Certainly there- was a small
amount of business which was got at a
much lower rate than usual; for policies
issued to men going on active service are
issued at a special rate of premium, the
usual thing being to charge 10 p.c. p.a.; and
the average premium in peace time is some-
where about 3i p.c. This means that where
the normal income would be £35 it is now
from this particular class of business £100;
and that tends to reduce the percentage cost
of getting new business. On the other (hand,
owing to the large number of young men
who are leaving the country in khaki, the
field for the insurance canvasser is reduced;
and the call for money for war purposes
has also contracted the insurance offering.
In 1913 this society issued 24,328 new
policies assuring £6.994,285; last year only
19.293 assuring £6,265,506. The average
premium in 1913 was £3 4s. 3d. p.c.; last
year it was £3 ss. Sd. p.c.
But while the society was hard hit by an
unfavorable death experience and greatly
increased taxation, it got a little back in
the shape of increased interest earnings:—

Further, the rapid increase in the holding
of public securities no doubt improved the
expense rate; for public securities don’t
require much managing.

The divided surplus for the lasit full
year preceding the war was £975,881, an

South Australia finds it rather difficult to
understand what has been happening in Syd-
ney and to some extent Melbourne. To
Adelaide war hasn’t meant boom. No con-
cern is more representative of South Aus.
than this bank; and look at these figures:
Liabilities.

Now compare them with those of a Sydney
bank of approximately the same size, the
A.B.C.: —
Dec., 1914. Dec., 1917.
Deposits, etc. .. £3,088,261 £4,280,794
Liquid assets . . 1,545,343 2,142,943
Advances . . . . 4,073,361 4,635,836
S. Australia has sold its wool and its copper
at very high prices and its wheat at a mod-
erate price, and for its wine it is command-
ing a figure that would have made it smack
its lips a few years ago. That has made up
for the real losses incurred through the stop-
page of production in other directions —
mostly through the departure of Billjim.
But few of the millions borrowed by the
Commonwealth have been spent in S. Aus-
tralia. Nobody has waved a lordly hand
there and said, “Do the thing properly!”
In the two years 1912-13 the direct oversea
exports from S. Aus. totalled £19,425,000;
in three years, June 1914-17. £18,237,000.
The corresponding figures for N. S. Wales,
which means Sydney and Newcastle and not
very much of Newcastle, are £65,846,000
and £119,355,000. It doesn’t mean, of course,
that S. Australia’s exports have been less
in the last three years than in the two years
immediately prior to the war. But it does
mean that the war, besides adding very
largely to the value of N. S. Wales’s own
production, has put a good deal of money
into Sydney’s pocket by making it a port
for the collection of Australian goods for
shipment overseas. For several years prior
to 1913 the cost of living was actually
heavier in Adelaide than in Sydney. Here
are Ivnibbs's index figures:—
1910. 1915. 1916. 1917.
Sydney .. 995 1323 1394 1407
Melbourne .. 942 1277 1309 1294
Adelaide .. 1008 1259 1285 1245
There has been no drought in Sydney to
account for the extraordinary inflation. On
the contrary, there has been great abundance
of supplies—except meat. The whole explan-
ation lies in the wholesale employment of
paper in Sydney and to a somewhat less
extent in Melbourne; it has meant a boom
—that is, a period of fictitious prices—from
which Adelaide may congratulate itself that
it is comparatively free.
So, though this bank’s recent progress has
seemed very poor compared with that of
banks on the Pacific slope, it can take com-
fort from the fact that there certainly is
less froth in its neighborhood. And that
means less likelihood of trouble in the
future. Its total liabilities at the end of
March were £4,873,377, and its liquid assets
were equal to no less than 58 p.c. of_the
debt; and in addition, there was £718,518,
or another 14 p.c., in Government-guaran-
teed wheat advances. This large liquid hold-
ing coupled with the fact that the public’s
money in the business isn’t much over four-
times* the shareholders’ funds makes big
profits out of the question. A 10 p.c. div.
looks all right, no doubt; but it is very
modest considering thaf the reserves largely
exceed the share capital. The holder in
the Bank of Adelaide used to shudder some-
times when he was told that his shares_were
£4 paid liable for £1 and a further £5 “in
case of emergency.” But with assets now
£4 10s. in excess of liabilities and capital,
the shareholder is entitled to feel a good
deal more comfortable.

Following Upon the death of Adam Forsyth,
and probably also having some regard to the
fact that Colonel Burns has had his 72nd
birthday, there have been changes in this
co.’s organisation. P. G. Black becomes
general manager; L. Armstrong takes Black’s
place as chief inspector, and D. W. Cross,
lately an inspector, has gone to America to
represent the co., Burns-Philp Co. of San
Francisco having been incorporated to facil-
itate business. Over 12 months ago it was
announced that, the better to cater for the
islands trade from which the Ilun had been
shut out by the war, contracts had been
placed for a number of wooden vessels. Most
of them were ordered in America; but the
other day Sydney discovered that, while
Hughes had been talking (and talking and
talking), Burns-Philp, being people of
action, had built and launched an auxiliary
vessel in Sydney Harbor for the Gilbert and
Ellice Islands trade. The development of
the Islands business has led the co. to estab-
lish a branch also at Auckland.
While this expansion has been in progress
in the South Pacific the co. has been enjoy-
ing itself in the North. The Common-
wealth’s occupation of German New Guinea
was followed by the extension of 8.-P.’s
sphere of influence to Rabaul; and though
this cost the co. the Matunga, which was
captured on the run from Samarai and sunk
off the coast of Celebes, the accounts do not
indicate that the mishap spoiled the year’s
figures. “Insufficiency of tonnage during the
year was detrimental to the co.’s interests.”
the directors report. Perhaps. That is to
say, if the co. had somehow managed to
grow vessels while the Huns were sinking
everybody else’s. it would have made more
money. But the insufficiency of tonnage has
put up freights by 8.-P. vessels to heights
that could only have been dreamed of a few
years ago ; and the remarkable development
of the Malayan tin-dredging business with
Australian money and brains has provided
the co. with tonnage which hasn’t, been
afraid of high figures, owing to the abnormal
price of tin. There is a dredge under con-
struction at Ruwolt’s in Melbourne whose
owners know that they will not have much
left out of £20,000 by the time they have
paid freight, insurance and re-erection costs.
And the trade with Java has probably not
shrunk. The quantity of flour and other
things that Batavia was consuming a while
ago was simply astonishing!
So, though insufficiency of tonnage has
been “detrimental to the co.’s interests,”
8.-P. managed to show'a profit of just on
20 p.c. on its capital of a million and fifty
thousand. And of that capital, as the table
shows, £350,000 was issued by way of a
bonus. Since then, in seven years, an aver-
age of 7} p.c. has been paid on the increased
capital and £373.857 has been added to vis-
ible reserves. Other movements of the past
eight years:—
Liabilities.

That is at March 31 in each year, before
the dividend was paid. “Other debts” —
really the liabilities to the public—have been
rattling ahead lately ; but that is due mainly
to the extension of business; for the “other
assets,” which have rattled along, too. are
“copra, pearlshell and other Island produce
on hand and in trail sit, and shipping, con-
signment and other advances.” What is going
to happen after the war this paper doesn’t
know. Before the war’ the German, with
Government assistance, was pushing trade
very hard in the Pacific. What takes place
after the war will be governed largely by the
things that happen in the war; and even
Foeh couldn’t tell you what they are going
to be. But 8.-P. lias a future outside the
South Pacific: for our trade with the North
has vast possibilities. That way, indeed, lies
our natural market.

from what in the foregoing table are called

in excess of the liabilities and capital, there
may or may not be any reserves at all. Ilns
Arthur Rickard and Co. Extended, Ltd., is a
holding co., the holdings consisting of the
150.000 £1 shares in Arthur Rickard and
Co., Ltd., and 49,407 out of 50,000 £2 shares
in the Haymarket Land, Building and Invest-
ment Co. Twelve months ago it was re-
marked here that, in those circumstances,
balance-sheets of the two subsidiary cos.
were necessary in order to allow of an
opinion of any value being formed about the
holding co. The directors have now issued
the three balance-sheets together, and they
are to be congratulated on doing a thing as
rare as it is necessary.
The co. stands something like this :

That, of course, looks as though the co. has
all its capital intact and £23,294 after pro-
viding for the 2| years’ pref. dividend. But
that all depends upon the value of the land,
city properties, buyers’ and borrowers’ bal-
ances and “shares in othe.r cos. and joint
enterprises.” In this last vague item A. It.
and Co., Ltd., has no less than £89.530. Pro-
bably some of that is N.S.W. Realty Co.,
Ltd.; more, probably, is in Hotel Sydney,
Ltd.; some of the balance, no doubt, in vari-
ous land cos. and syndicates. There may thus
be a great deal more in unsold land than
appears in the list.
The very heavy liabilities may be sum-
marised this way:—
Mortgages and balances owing on properties £218,447
Debentures (Commonwealth Bank) . . 10,250
Deposits.. .. .. .. .. .. 10,732
Sundry creditors .. .. .. . . 25,084
Dividends .. .. .. ~ . • 23,588
Total £288,101
The buyers’ and borrowers’ balances plus a
few sundries and sundry book-debts amount
almost exactly to the liabilities, leaving the
capital and reserves to be represented by
unsold land, city properties and “shares in
other cos. and joint enterprises.” Plainly
almost everything depends upon the amount
which can be realised from the use and sale
of these assets; and that, again, depends
very largely upon how they were bought. It
looks as though the bulk of these assets were
acquired prior to the war; and whether land
is going to bring more after the war than it
did before the great trouble struck the world
is a question that nobody can answer defi-
nitely, though lots of people think they know.
With over £lOO,OOO of assets in excess of
liabilities the Haymarket has shown an aver-
age of 1 p.c. profit during the past two years;
yet A. R. and Co., Ltd., reckoned that this
capital was worth par and claims it as such
in its accounts. The Arthur Rickard and Co.
assets show up a bit better; yet there wasn’t
enough there to show p.c. on the ordi-
naries last year, and even that profit depends
upon whether enough has been set aside to
provide for ba® debts and depreciation. Pro-
bably nothing has been provided for deprecia-
tion of property, since it is assumed that
any shrinkage in values is due to abnormal
conditions. The point is whether conditions
were not abnormal when the assets were
secured, and whether they will for many years
be in the same state again.
* * * *
Carpathia, Ardlethan (N.S.W.), is feeling
on good terms with itself, mainly because
some consignments of fair-grade ore have
been made to the battery at Barmedman.
These parcels are expected to yield 18 tons
concentrates. For the last week in April 6|
tons of concentrates were sent to Sydney.
A small battery has been purchased at
Tumut, and is to be re-erected at Ardlethan.
The Lady Bevys, one of the old-time mines
on the Barrier, is owned now by the Mar-
shall’s Monarch Co., which, apparently
through lack of funds, lately got three
months’ suspension. The old co. found the
ore carried too much zinc to be profitable in
those days: but better grade silver-lead
has been found in a branch lode. This has
been driven on 41ft., the body running from
sft. to 7ft. wide, with only one wall show-
ing in the face. The mine is 11 or 12 miles
from Broken Hill, which isn’t much to
bridge if values are right. At present more
is said about measurements. But before the
prospect is abandoned some first-rate man
certainly ought to be engaged to run the rule
over recent developments.
Mount Hope (N.S.W.) was recently com-
pelled to suspend milling owing to shortage
of water. The co. has now equipped its
bore, and the production of concentrates has
been resumed. During the interval the
leaching of the tailings dumps proceeded as
usual. This co.’s copper precipitate aver-
ages about 75 p.c. copper, so that something
has been coming into the exchequer mean-
while. The railway from Matakana to the
mine is under construction by the New
South Wales Government. If this 10-mile
spur is to be finished by the end of June,
as originally contemplated, the officers of
the Department will have to get a move on.
It is pretty easy going, so they may succeed.
* * * *
The Jasper copper-mine, on the Forbes
Itiver (N.S.W.), abandoned years ago, is
being reopened by Marshall and Reynolds,
who worked the Mount Brown copper show,
up Coif’s Harbor way, until they sold out
recently. The abnormal price of copper,
together with cheaper cartage now that the
N.C. line is opened, gives the new men a big
advantage over the co. that opened the mine.
* * * *
The newly formed Perth Mining Associa-
tion has asked the Westralian Treasurer to
help in undoing some of the damage done to
mining by Hughes’s insane “settlement of
the metals problem” and other measures.
.V. E. Morgans and others who formed the
deputation claimed that not only are treat-
ment charges now excessive, but every ounce
of base metal has to be sent compulsorily to
N. S. Wales for refinement, and when re-
fined the owner is not allowed to sell except
through the Metal Exchange, or, if copper
or zinc, through an association formed in
Melbourne to handle these metals. It was
suggested that if the Federal Government
would not agree to exempt the metals men-
tioned from special taxation the present
exemption of £lOOO in respect of the profits
tax on new industries be increased to
£lO,OOO, or, alternatively, that the rate of
profit which was exempt should be increased
from 10 per cent, to not less than 15 per
cent. The Treasurer sympathised with the
deputation and promised to make strong
representations to the Federal authorities on
its behalf. But for the tax several lead
deposits at Northampton and copper shows
beyond Peak Hill, now idle, would certainly
be working.
* * * #
From Westonia :—-
A perusal of a comprehensive report by .A.
State Mining Engineer Montgomery on the water
difficulty at Westonia affords little comfort to in-
vestors. Montgomery estimates there is an inflow
of between 60,000 and 65,000 gallons per hour
threatening Edna May, Central and Consolidated,
and this has to be pumped by the three cos. either
jointly or severally. So far Edna May has borne
the burden, but apparently it is becoming tired of
the honor. Concerning Deeps, Montgomery says the
co. may or may not succeed in damming back the
water from Edna May. He points out also that
should Central persist in mining the Edna May reef
to a greater depth, Deeps will be threatened with
a flooding unless a block of ground is left solid in
Deeps ground on the boundary of Deeps and Central.
Montgomery adds that owners of Deeps should re-
member that they will have to face the necessity
of providing a second opening into the mine for
safety and ventilation.
Edna May and Central are installing additional
pumps in order to cope with the inflow, and it is
likely that these will enable the two mines to again
work their bottom levels, which have been aban
doned for some months. Edna May has about 12
months’ ore to work out in its lower levels, and
what will happen when the water is permitted to
rise in its workings after the ore reserves are ex-
hausted is not comforting. Consolidated, should
Edna May and Central pumping operations drain its
workings (which appears likely), will have one level
to work before its lode passes into Central ground,
and development work in connection with this level
is much behind.
About 65 feet of driving in Deeps has disclosed
thff reef to be from 3ft. to 10ft. in width at the lowest
level, which does not compare favorably with the
width at corresponding points above. Investors are
anxious to know how far the north face of the Deeps’
bottom level drive is from the Central boundary. It
must be very close, if not already there.
* * * *
No one could have expected Edna Mav
to show up with much of a yield for April:
nevertheless the tally of 150 oz. from 528
tons was a bit of a shock. Williams must
liave stripped his mine pretty well of decent-
grade ore above 300 ft. to have to put through
such a crushing. Cyanide (£1600) helps to
gild the figures. Even then the yield isn’t
worth more than £2300, or about half as
much as the March run and only one-third
of the February figures. Deeps sent in
llOOoz. from 1200 tons, with 3dwt. in the
sands. This really isn’t bad ; but the market
expected better. That is the penalty a man
has to pay for setting himself a high stan-
dard, as Stuckey did when he took to churn-
ing out £4OOO yields from SOO ton crushings.
At the Central Giblin has had to close the
bulkhead door, as water is again on the
make, so that it only needs a low return
from Consolidated to put the Westonia pot
off the boil for another month.
* * * *
Things at Hampden (Q.) aren’t too rosy,
as the row over manager Evans, of the
Duchess, is on again, and with greater vigor
than ever. Judge Macnaughton sent the
miners back after telling them that their
objection to Evans was too frivolous for
words. Peace lasted just as long as Evans
was absent; when he showed up the miners
downed tools again. To soften things a bit
Evans’s position was changed from that of
manager to superintendent; but it made
no difference. Earle Huntley is to ask the
union officers to play the game under
Macnaughton rules; but he hasn’t much
hope with the malcontents. If he can't fix
things the Hampden div. will probably be
dropped.
Gympie has had another serious blow —
the shutting down of the Scottish. There
have been 300 hands on the pay-sheet, and
the only work to be done in the show now
is the cyaniding of the tailings. Last year
the co. paid 2d. on its 660,000 £1 shares,
bringing the total distribution to £502,242.
* * # *
A conference at Ararat (Yic.) between
New Langi manager Mackay and Jude of
the F.M.E.A. over whether the miners should
go back to their jobs at the New Langi
Logan and South Langi has ended in smoke.
The fact Mackay had to present was that
calls come as regularly as butchers’ bills, and
that as £130,000 has been thrown into the
New Langi alone Jude ought not to stick
out for something that means a weekly ioss
of £5OO. But it was no go. Jude holds the
fat end of the stick because good miners
are, very scarce, and when he called out the
underground hands ne had places ready for
them elsewhere. To get them back lie' asks
rail fares from Broken Hill (estimated at
£800), a day-wage system, the resumption of
wash-extraction (no matter what the cost),
and shift bosses in the union. This last
would leave only the manager and the
underground man to represent the share-
holder ; and as a big alluvial mine can’t be
run without adequate supervision Mackay
reckoned further discussion waste of time.
Jude may have been putting up a big bluff,
but there is little chance of the Langi mines
applying the wage system ,to stoping work.
Macka.v's shareholders also are not going to
part up £5OO weekly to take out wash that
won’t pay as mining shapes to-day. So
Ararat looks groggier than it did even in
the days when B„ M. and Co. threw up the
sponge over the Cathc-art deal.
* * * *
The affairs of Trong tin are being straight-
ened out, but not in the way the Sydney crowd
desired. Their idea was to plant the Trong
dredge on the Austral Malay’s Ulu Yam
lease of 600 acres which on wide boring
worked out at about 0.751 b. tin oxide per
cubic yard. Trong shareholders were to have
a half-interest in the area in return for their
dredge. But chairman Meagher has another
proposition—the Kalumpan lease of 250
acres, of which 120 acres are alleged to be
lib. ground and about 80 acres 0.751 b. This
block had been bored by Lewis, of Kampong
Ivamunting. To check him 250 fresh bores
have been sunk by Betliune, sampler of
Freeman’s Bolivian property. As he verifies
Lewis, the Trong directors look like spurn-
ing the Sydney offer. If they summon up
pluck to buy Kalumpan, as they can do
under the articles, the vendors of the latter
show will get £lO,OOO cash and 20.000 paids
in a new 100,000 co. with a working capital
ot £20,000. Presumably Palfreyman will
underwrite the new co.
* * * *
J. E. Cocker has handed over his option
on the Alpine to the Umeari, which spent
three years and £50,000 in putting a dredge
to work in the Philippines to find that
ground represented as worth Is. a yard was
not worth Id. Alpine is on the old Cobun-
gra lead, on the highest point of the Aus-
tralian Alps, near Bright (Yic.). Tracey,
at one time old John Wallace’s head
man, and Morwood Thompson report it as
an alluvial deposit from 20ft. to 300 ft. thick
and 40 chains wide, worth Is. a yard, and
the control of the race gives the co. the con-
trol of the deposit. The Umeari co. has
issued 60,000 new shares, and is bogging into
the work. This paper doesn’t know any-
thing yet about the Alpine. It does know,
however, that three years back it showed
that the Umeari ground was so well proved
to be valueless that the Australians who
persisted in running the show must have
been something exceptional in optimists.
“M.C.”: As noted in last week’s
Bulletin, Canada is now enjoying the full
benefit of the high prices obtaining for ferro-
inolybdenum and molybdenite concentrates
in U.S.A. I£ is, however, only recently that
the embargo against the export of molyb-
denum from Canada to U.S.A. has been
removed, and these high prices have become
available to Canadian producers. There
sn ’t ? n y sound reason why Australian
molybdenite concentrates should not be ex-
ported to U.S.A., too. We want the monev
badly enough.
Some of the biggest men in Westralia
have petitioned the State Government to
suspend the labor condition of mining leases
and prospecting areas during the war and
for six months after. Continued efforts to
compel people to work their holdings under
present conditions have no doubt led to the
throw:ng-up of many leases; and unless the
Government moves in the direction asked,
many more, it is alleged, will follow suit.
There is something to be said for the peti-
tioners —more, anyway, than for the mora-
torium which was declared soon after the
war started.
