A WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, May 6, 1918.
My Dear Arini, —
Mayor Joynton Smith’s party in the Town
Hall was, to begin with, a stand-up-and-
deliver-your-hand affair. About 2000 people
of all shades of politics and none, with and
without red ties, with patent toes and corns
and easy leather boots, slowly wormed their
way from the bleak front steps to the vesti-
bule. Half an hour was about the time for
the pilgrimage. The vestibule was the
hitch. It had, with the best intentions, been
turned into a cloak-room. Hence when you
stopped to peg your coat or hat you blocked
progress as far back as George-street. Late
arrivals, learning wisdom, stuck to their
wraps, advanced like wardrobe-hangers,
found a few spare fingers for the Mayor, and
bustled forward. Dr. Fiaschi’s tall lean
figure (the man himself looks younger for
his war work), the beautifully curled
horsehair of Town Clerk Nesbitt, the sub-
regal party on the wallaby round the ,room,
lawyers, laborers in other vineyards, infants
placidly asleep in theft mothers’ arms,
official sec. Budge and his lady, a dash of
khaki, heads of Arbitration Courts and
“defendants, your Honor,” somebody without
a collar enjoying an apple alongside the
Governor’s decoration (his Ex. wears his
bauble over his left lung)—and all hands to
refreshments as soon as the hour struck.
There is one quite easy way of helping the
recruiter. Most men have insured their lives
for the benefit of their wives and families.
Every policy brings in a certain bonus every
year. Nobody outside the insurance office
handles the money—it is just added on to
the amount of the policy. Thousands of men
might feel more inclined to transfer this
year’s bonus to the fund for insuring
married men who will go to the Front if
their wives said a word in favor of it;
because, as I’ve told you already, the in-
surance policy is essentially the wife’s. Sup-
pose they talk it over, then, with hubby?
Dolores curtseyed to a Town Hall audi-
ence for the first time for three years on
Saturday night. Diamonds flashed on her
plump neck, and the soft smile is in the
same place. For the rest it’s just the same
Dolores —brilliant artist and sweet soprano.
French songs suit her best. The audience
demanded an encore for the souffle ending
with “Sous un ormeau.” Pianist Ilenri
Penn, from Melbourne, a sombre, raven-
haired musician, can hit hard when he likes.
But he knows how to tickle the keyboard
softly, too, and the first recall was a small
tumult for his attack on “Tarantelle.”
Louise Mack had an unpleasant experi-
ence at AVagga. Of course it was bound to
come some day. She had been lecturing
through the country on her war experiences,
handing 25 per cent, of the gross proceeds
to the local Red Orossers, who, as a rule,
worked up the show. Then came the fiat :
Any person who shall, without the authority of
the State War Council, organise any scheme for
raising money for any patriotic fund other than the
Repatriation Fund, shall be liable to....six months.
The AVagga lecture was cancelled, while the
Minister for Repatriation was appealed to
for the necessary authority.
Harry Burne, an old Julian Ashton pupil
who has been decorating New York with art
posters for some years, was wounded in
recent fighting in France. AVhen the war
began Harry put aside his brushes and made
for London and a rifle. lie enlisted as a
private in the Horse Guards Blues, later
transferred to the Field Artillery, obtained
a commission, and has been directing corres-
pondence to Fritz continuously for over
three years. In the 1916 offensive he was
A.D.C.-ing General Strong, apd is now a
captain. He is a son of Dr. and Mrs. Burne,
of Waverley.
After putting up a brave fight, Lily, wife
of Percy Ashton and daughter-in-law of
Julian, died in St. ATncent’s private hospital
on April 29. Percy enlisted early in
this year his wife intended to carry bn till
his return. The carrying-on included the
management of a plantation in the New
Hebrides, with only Fuzzy-Wuz as a helper.
Malaria intervened, and a very sick woman
came to Sydney a few weeks ago. She was
a widow when she married Percy, and there’s
a young daughter at school in Canada put-
ting on a black frock for a loved mother.
Flower-trimmed Alexandra Hospital seethed
with good wishes and discreet tabloids of
oratory the other afternoon with Gov. David-
son to listen to it all. The annual meeting
is always held on the premises—a simple
wile -which is good for the never-too-robust
finances. Dr. Clubbe, perennial president,
breathed in public the first whisper of the
Board’s latest aspiration. Above all things
else now it wants a convalescent home, pre-
ferably by the seaside. Hundreds of the
infants don’t need a hospital so much as a
spade and a heap of sand and the salt air
coming in upon their little pinched faces
from the big, clean ocean. It wouldn’t cost
much; and what a memorial it would be!
Red Cross Day brought all manner of
relics, given after a farewell hug, to help
grill another chicken for our wounded. But
there are still a few ancient rags their
owners love more than life. “Kenneth
McKenzie,” writes a country correspondent,
“has a strip of the silk dress worn by Flora
Macdonald in the days of Prince Charlie.
This is alleged to have landed in Australia
in 1835 in company with Isabella McKenzie.
The lady went first to Camden; then settled
at Brembin, Hastings River. Flora’s bit
skirt is now a wall-pocket, and in spite of its
age is in first-rate condition.”
Young artist Cayley, son of the late
Neville, sold £l5O worth of bird and bush in
his just-closed show. Amongst the buyers
who chose bush was Dr. Chisholm Ross.
Trees are a hobby of his.
An extra-cheerful Billjim occupied a motor
seat in tin* Anzac Day procession. He \vas
short of a leg, hut the stump of it remained,
and it proudly floated a small flag, which
waved in acknowledgment of the cheers en
route, and bowed now and then to extra-
special applause.
The John Waughs’ capable daughter goes
to Egypt as an 18-carat war-worker. She
has put in the usual ALA.I), term of washing-
up and scrubbing floors for Billjim, and was
a first-rate hon. cook for a while at the Mill
Convalescent Home, Moss Vale. Parra-
matta Croquet Club handed the local lass a
fountain-pen and its sincere esteem.
The second of the Waverley Playfair boys,
Capt. Strath, is back, with more shrapnel in
his limbs than is good for any boy’s comfort.
Matraville is on the road to Long Bay,
and the tram tosses out its passengers
amongst the sand hummocks of the new vil-
lage where voluntary workers are perspiring
over spade and pick. Mostly it’s spade, with
a barrow. On Saturday afternoon the
Davidsons, after a look at the AVar Chest
sports, hurried out to the voluntary diggers’
camp. Here his Ex. buckled to. after fife
and drum had played him in, and shovelled
a few peck's of sand from their long home,
finally laying the foundation-stone of a
home for a disabled soldier. Of such domi-
ciles 170 will gradually spring up, with
architects John Sulman arid Oscar Backhouse
as watchdogs. The company balanced on small
boulders round the platform while Dr.
Arthur had a tilt at more than windmills
and the Blue Gum girls made the tea.
Dr. Mary Booth, Mrs. Ethel Turner Cur-
lewis, Mrs. Cutner and the übiquitous opti-
mist Kettlewell were in the crush.
Matra had a finger in the earliest Botany
Bay pie. According to some authorities he
had as a youngster been a middy on the
Endeavor and saw the Promised Land. Any-
how, in 1779 he put before Lord Sydney his
scheme for hurling turbulent spirits into the
new Arcadia; and in 1786 the notion took
form.
This is the pic-
turesque head of
“Old Boomerang.”
At 96, within a week
or two of his recent
death at Hunter’s
Hill, its venerable
owner, J. R. Ilould-
ing, was still com-
paratively active.
Born in England in
1822 —the Fourth
George was King—
young Houlding was
articled to a London
lawyer. But the
world was wide, and
the Houlding family
wanted to see it; so
they chartered a
ship, in the old-
fashioned way, and chose Australia for the
great adventure. The crew mutinied on
the voyage. “And the lawyer’s clerk,” says
one Australian chronicler, “had to go aloft
in a storm and help to reef the main tops’l.”
He reefed so well that he was kept at the
hard and dizzy job till they reached Sydney
on- Anniversary Day, 1839. The experience
was, according to “Old Boomerang,” cheaply
bought: in after years he navigated his
own ships on the Australian coast. The
Ilouldings brought out valuable stock for a
station property. But the youth wanted
more excitement than “going on the land.”
Eventually he settled at Raymond Terrace,
made a fortune (with the assistance of a
start given by a legacy from England) and
took a trip to England. When he returned
the gold rush was over, business went to bits,
and, to add to his losses, he had endorsed
bills for a friend. As “Old Boomerang,”
whose busy pen pushed many articles into
the Sydney press of long ago. and writer of
“Christopher Cockle’s Colonial Experiences”
and many other stories of early Australia,
he will be remembered best. In his case
longevity was worth while. Surrounded by
a devoted circle of grandchildren (one of
his daughters married the late Dr.
Ivelynack), “Old Boomerang” retained his
love* for literature and his interest in life to
the end. Physically slender, almost
shadowy, he owned up to being an invalid
only about a fortnight before his death.
Gone where no
discords can disturb
his long rest, Syd-
ney’s wonderful old
music-master, Josef
Kretselimann. Born
80 years ago in the
once-pleasant land of
Bohemia, and with,
every sign of having
a wandering - gipsy
minstrel strain in his
blood, the story of
his studies in Leipsie
and his efforts (with
a grand duchess to
finance the scheme)
to contemplate the
remains of Babylon,
has already be e n
told. It sounds like
the opening chapter of a modern Don
Quixote’s adventures. Sydney knows him
better as its greatest and most unique music-
master. Two generations of infant phenom-
ena (all his ducklings were going to be
swans) learnt piano or violin from the
gifted child of nature. And very often they
paid nothing but the uncertain dole of grati-
tude for such infinite pains as would have
almost knocked harmony into a cabbage-
head.
ifis pupils’ concerts in happier days were
tremendous gatherings. The Town Hall
surged with ringlets, short muslin frocks and
dimples. The adored master, good man, with
usually just sufficient funds in hand for the
coming week or two, had always some pet
philanthropy to help with the profits. A
fund for the assistance of distressed
musicians was his favorite dream. To chil-
dren he was an eternal Santa Claus whose
baggy pockets bulged with toys all the year.
It was always Christmas 1 )ay with this
benign soul —till the war came. The horrors
of the Ilunnish methods of waging it became
a daily scourge to the gentle old man who,
having been born in Bohemia, had to be
called German. He died at 80, still too
young to give up teaching, after a week or
so of intangible illness —a tired child. To
one of his favorite pupils, Esther Kahn—-to
whom he bequeathed Ins pupils—he said:
“In a few days, my child, I will go. But
none of your ‘Dead Marches’ at my funeral.
Just ‘Home, Sweet Home.’ ” Of his impe-
cuniosity he had only one fear. “AVhen I
am gone,” he said to Southwell four years
ago, “no one shall have to dip the hand in
the pocket for me. See here, my friend. I
have just paid for my little bit of earth at
South Head—where we will hear the waves !
And for you, too; I have bought you a
grave beside me!” As “Southie” was very
ill at the time he was not wildly elated when
the dear old man, with the simplicity of the
child, thrust into his hand the receipt for
his six-feet of sod. “But this poor horse
must not drag us up the hill,” was Kretsch-
mann’s sudden exclamation when he and a
fellow-musician with a big ’cello had reached
the bottom of Bellevue in a cab. “We walk,
my friend ; the poor animal has the cab—it
is enough.” So out they got. The charac-
teristic photo is lent by J. S. Watkins. The
original portrait is in the National Art
Gallery.
A r eteran artist George Collingridge has
just closed his brief one-man show in the
Iklucation Gallery. The walls were wanted
for someone else’s frames, so Sydney’s old
friend had only a four days’ exhibition. In
his small water-color sketches Collingridge
piovides a good background for some chap-
ters of early N. S. Wales history. For in-
stance, “The Oldest Farm on the Ilawkes-
bnry” : Ben Boyd’s old “AVool Store,” built
in the ’forties on the shores of Neutral Bay;
and “The Oldest House in Sydney.” A more
concentrated attack on the historic side of
his work, put before the public later on.
would bring a queue of buyers. Not that
the aesthetic Collingridge worries about the
commercial fate of a sunset after he has
painted it.
l r oung Billyard Leake, who has been
“mentioned” for his part in the Zeebrugge
raid, is a son of that Charles Billyard of
this State who married a wealthy Miss
Leake and added the lady’s maiden name.
In the long ago the Billyards—grandpa and
grandma of the naval lieutenant —lived at
picturesque old Elizabeth Farm, on the
Parramatta River.
Local society lias folded the youthful
Aide Saltmarshe to its ninon bosom. He is
young and serious, fought in the retreat
from Mons, and has been at it almost ever
since. Damages- earned the present fur-
lough. So he has made an Impression, and
our most expensive people are, so to speak,
showering seats-in-motors on the nice new
boy.
Mary Moodie Heddle, Superintendent of
the Talbot Colony for Epileptics (Vic.),
passed this way recently. She is going to
Canada on a visit to one of her big brothers.
Two of these huge Scots are well known in
the inter-State ship-service, one being the
youngish skipper of the Katoomba. Before
coming to this sunny clime Mary Heddle
was one of the Queen’s Nurses, in Eng-
land—they do work similar to that of our
District Nurses.
The Flying Corps Comforts Depot has
bioken out in an orchestra. It wears no
wings—just carries its fiddle under its arm
and plays when required at patriotic func-
tions. The perch is at 59a AVentworth-
avenue, and thd Ladybirds want any semi*
detached girls who feel that way to come
along and lend their aid.
A brilliant show of English and American
posters, collected by C. Lloyd Jones, one of
the editors of “Art in Australia,” will be
opened on Saturday at the Education Gal-
lery. Admission, fid.; takings go to the
War Chest, the collector defraying all
expenses himself. Even before the war the
best English and American artists didn’t
consider it below them to paint posters.
Now, with the nations asking for their
patriotic best, the giants of pencil and paint
have turned out masterpieces for the hoard-
ings.
Master Painters, Decorators and Sign-
writers are having a corroboree. As part of
their week’s jubilation there’s an exhibition
of their craftsmanship in the Town Hall
basement. A notable section is the polished
wood; and alongside decorators get in a
fancy touch with effective imitations of old
tapestry squares. The modern craze for the
revival of the frieze is illustrated in moun-
tainous scenery entirely surrounded by lakes.
Altogether an interesting show.
The useful Bed Cross secretary, Miss
Chester, who is a permanent institution at
Dalton House, got a gold watch and an
autograph-book in recognition of her strenu-
ous labors for the Day.
The sixth reinforcements of the 17th Bat-
talion—the 17th that was so badly cut up at
Gallipoli—want to have a reunion of re-
turned men. Sergeant Woods, Bream-street,
Coogee, is the secretary.
A surplus of Red Cross Day goods, sold
in David Jones’s the other afternoon (Ser-
geant-Major Van Gilder was the lively auc-
tioneer), revealed the variety of stuff which
pours in for a patriotic cause. A most in-
dignant black orpington lady was dragged
out of a crate to kick her toes in mid-air as
the auctioneer ticked off her talents as pro-
vidore of the breakfast egg. Island bags
and kirtles, auto-harps, a new bicycle, an
oil, painting, crockery, cut glass and other
luxuries fell in turn to a circle of women-
bidders. The only thing missing w r as a
recipe for winning the war before its fourth
birthday.
Boots, in spite of the price of leather, are
still climbing. Sumptuary laws, to decide
who wants the tanner’s hides most, may yet
come into force. There is plenty of human
nature .in the best of women. And Sister
Susie becomes irritable when she sees her
self-abnegation discounted by senseless waste.
Yours affectionately.

MELBOURNE

Melbourne, May 6, 1918.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Sassiety has so many Red Cross bookings
for this month that the calendar hasn’t
enough days to go round. Two big shows
have therefore decided to collaborate. The
matinde at King’s ("which had been scheduled
for the 10th) joins up with the big show
engineered by the wives of Federal members.
Mrs. George Fairbairn, president of the
matinge affair, offered herself and all her
minions to President Mrs. Willie Watt, and
Mrs. Watt accepted. The matinee happens
on May 21, and the Federal kiosks do busi-
ness on Red Cross Day.
The two big committees fused on Friday
afternoon at the Oriental. It was like the
junction of two flooded rivers, across which
heroines disguised as maids swam repeatedly
with tea and eatables. Before the end of
the afternoon there was (comparative)
peace.
Lady Helen and Lady Stanley were both
interested, but neither could attend the tea
party. Mrs. George Fairbairn (president
of the matinge) led her side well. Mrs.
Groom (Q.) and Mrs. Flemming (N.S.W.),
the autocrats of the Federals’ effort, were
kept busy pouring oil on troubled waters.
Ex-mayoress Hennessy looked as if she was
thankful to be (officially) out of the argu-
ments. Bigjohn Forrest’s wife was at home
helping nurse her Bigjohn; but they are
both doing their best for this Red Cross
cause. Mrs. Shepherd, Mrs. Jack Scott and
Mrs. Sellheim were there, giving moral sup-
port to their good-tempered President Mrs.
Fairbairn. Bates Post attended in a lovely
striped heliotrope shirt, like wallpaper, a
check suit and sundries. He is offering a
one-man play of half an hour to the matinee.
Mrs. Post accompanied him, and the big,
languid leading lady wore a wonderful big
black shiny hat above droopy duds.
The G.-G. and Lady Helen have been for
a dash through the Western District by
motor. Welcomes by town and shire coun-
cils and Red Cross branches were numerous,
but what tickled his Ex. most was an im-
promptu welcome at Dennington, between
Warrnambool and Port Fairy, where a Kelly
Gang of school kids formed up across the
roadway, stopped the car and flocked round
with shouts of “Hi, Mister, we want a holi-
day !” The G.-G. was greatly touched by
this manifestation of loyalty and arranged to
have the demand granted.
As soon as seats for the big Red Cross
matinee at King’s were available, three of
the four boxes were snapped up at quite
ordinary rates. Then a man offered £lOO
for the remaining box, and his bid was thank-
fully taken. Wealthy pals of his came seeking
boxes, one offering £5O and another bidding
£25 a seat (four seats in a box). But the
pens were not available. The women who
had booked them at the usual six guineas
were sitting hard on their bargains at the
time of writing. And Red Cross enthusiasts
were thinking of all the money that was
being lost.
A thin haze of secrecy surrounds the Red
Cross effort made by Captain Conant, gent-
help. The blue-eyed broth of a boy has
• written a play for the movies, with sassiety’s
leaders and platoon commanders as actors
and actresses. The film will be seen first at
Ccombe and then, thrown on the Auditorium
screen on May 23 at a monster Red Cross
bushrange. The decorative Roma Clarke and
Mavis Thiel are stars —the necessary' dark
and fair heroines. Eily Hughes says she is
the bundle that falls out of the motor-car.
Then she claps her hand over her mouth
and says she can tell No More. Conant
wants his play to be a mystery until it
bursts on Melbourne. One of the released
“secrets” is that a scene is to show Portsea
pier with all the sensational bathing-dresses
of last season and inspirations for next
summer thrown in.
The Quamby Clubbers had their big Red
Cross party last week. Roma Clarke, the
decorative secretary, thinks they’ll get quite
£5OO out of it. One stall was a gold-mine:
it had 500 quail to sell, besides chooks, tur-
keys, young lamb and other country presents.
The raffle for the wonderful pearl necklace
with the diamond clasp (presented by Mrs.
Fairbairn) still goes on. Piles of books
have been sold ; but the rafflers are keen on
getting more tickets into the country and
even into the other States.
A march of reinforcements on Saturday
mci'ning through Melbourne showed fine lads
—but pitifully few of them. They leave
almost immediately for France. There were
rallying points with,bands and speeches and
an Anzae band going on before. A squad of
Italians marched along after them in every
possible style of mufti. They have been
called home for service.
The girls of the Malvern Rifle Club
are in mourning for their former instructor,
Sergeant G. F. Williams, killed in action
after two years’ service. Before he went to
France he trained many a soldier to shoot,
and when he arrived at the Front, after a
further spell as instructor in England, he
bettered his precepts by winning the Mili-
tary Medal and the Croix de Guerre. Forty
years of age, he left two boys to remember
him, and a fighting brother to avenge him.
The luscious Australian brunette who was
Muriel Williams pops up as Lady Lawford,
wife of Major-General Sir Sydney Lawford.
a distinguished brass hat. Muriel’s first
husband was Oswald Watt, the Sydney
flying man, who has done so well with the
French and English Flying Corps. Lady
Lawford’s dad was the late Sir Hartley
Williams,/who. just missed being our Chief
Justice. By the way, her brother, popular
“Tup” Williams, has been appointed Associ-
ate to Irvine. C.J.
With young Franklin Peterson’s depar-
ture to join General Johnston’s staff at
Rabaul, the widow Peterson will find herself
rather a lonely soul in Australia. The two
Peterson children, six and seven when they
first saw Melbourne, spoke a strange Scot-
tish mixture for some years after their
arrival, but by degrees plain Australian con-
quered, though for emergencies they both
have a handy selection of other tongues.
Daughter Leonie, who married Colonel
Mackworth, lives at Cairo. She used to be
an energetic little miss in her early days at
the Conservatorium. When she grew Dig
enough papa allowed her to toot one of the
brasses in his orchestra, a job seldom pre-
viously undertaken by any of the girl-
students. It is possible that she may be a
baronet’s missus one day. Two of Colonel
Mackworth’s elder brothers have gone under
to the Hun, and eldest brother Humphrey,
the reigning potentate, has no children.
Songster Ida Osborne’s sister Nina, known
in Australian musical comedy, is at present
a masseuse on the staff of a war hospital in
John Bull’s country. She recently had a
lively time with a devil’s brood of. Hun
aeroplanes, which, to show their spite at
failing to pass the barrage that kept them
from London, eight miles away, dropped their
eggs on the hospital. Nina took her chance,
and stood by the soldiers until 2 a.m., when
the raiders 'departed. Talking of eggs, she
pays sd. each for the fruit of the hen, and
the rest of her diet is meat-extract soup,
rice, macaroni, beans and rhubarb. Her
patients are more or less limbless fighting
men.
Students of the ’Varsity of a decade ago
will grieve at the news of Carl Pirani’s death,
indirectly due to his trials on active service.
After gaining his B.Sc., he, with one of
Theodore Fink’s sons, ran a wheat farm in
the West. In due time they enlisted, and
Pirani returned to the land after being in-
valided home. His brother Max, an “infant
prodigy” in music, enlisted in U.S.A., where
he had been studying.
To the gloomy record of deaths amcng
the Mt. Noorat Blacks, of Terang, has beeu
added that of Flight-Lieut. Donald Black,
who passed out on the 23rd ult. as the. result
of an accident while on the wing. With his
brother lan, Lieut. Black left Australia t o
years ago to join up with the British army.
The Madden widow has lost her eldest
nephew, Captain Kenneth Stephen, of the
Artillery. He was killed in France on April
22 after over two years’ service, during which
he earned the M.C. Like his father, Sidney
Stephen, lie was attached to the Law, and
caught an occasional six-an’-eight at Maffra.
A few returned Aussies are now wearing
colors on their backs. They are men of the
Sixth Brigade—the soldiers who fought back
to back at Bullecourt and wear the King’s
colors in recognition of the part they played
in that frightful struggle.
Flight-Lieut. Gordon Ross-Soden had
hardly hopped back to khaki after recover-
ing from war injuries when he was put
on to the sick list again by a defective plane.
Young Gordon had been given a machine-
testing job as light employment suitable for
a flying convalescent. When the last mail
left he was still too sore to go aloft.
The Glaciarium opened on Saturday for
another war winter and found soldier-part-
ners scarcer than usual. Of the hundreds of
returned soldiers round town few' are fit for
such physical exertion, and the fit eligibles
not in khaki are shy birds these times. A
swartn of lively flappers glode round with
schoolboys—youngsters under military age.
Miss Adelaide obliges :
Fifteen hundred petitioners to the Premier have
demanded an inquiry concerning the 19X5 Onka-
paringa Race Meeting. The committee had promised
that half the net profits of the meeting would go to
the Belgian Relief Fund, for which Lady Galway was
then making a special appeal, and this is what the
petitioners allege: That a gross profit of £2OOO
was made; that a debit balance of £2 10s. Id. was
announced ; that £5OO for improvements and upkeep
and £179 for lunch were debited; in short, that,
apparently the whole of the profits were swallowed
by costs and charges for the day’s sport. The com-
plainants reckon it is high time that the club
was compelled to hand over its books to an inquisi-
tive auditor. The Premier has promised to give to
the protest his “consideration.” But surely the
propriety of auditing the books of clubs and com-
mittees that make patriotic appeals to the public
doesn’t call for much considering.
Lieut. Neil Campbell, who has been seriously ill
twice and gravely wounded twice since he enlisted in
1914, is now reported missing. It is eloquent testi-
mony to the feebleness of our present recruiting
system that a man so badly knocked about should
be back in heavy fighting while Australia teems
with untried eligibles. Lieut. Campbell, who is a
son of the late Dr. Allan Campbell, was married not
long before war broke out to the younger daughter
of the John Gordons. His wife and baby daughter
are in Adelaide.
Miss Pollitt, who exchanged school-teaching at
Mount Gambier for munition-making in England in
January of last year, is now working with Captain
Bean on the history of the war. In between taking
notes for Bean she helps Col. Butler, D. 5.0., who is
in charge of the collection of medical records. “Two
of the nicest men to be found anywhere,” she exults.
In a recent casualty list Lieut. Guthrie and Sgt.-
Major Russell, previously reported missing, were
posted as killed in action on October 12 of last year.
Both were sons of two stalwarts of the old Labor
party. Mr. Guthrie is still one of this State’s Sena-
tors, and the late Mr. Russell filled another of the
six seats till his death a few years ago.
The M.C. has been awarded to Lieut. T. S. Ware.
He was in Queensland when war broke out and
enlisted there. He is the second son of A. W.
Ware, who was mayor of this village at the time of
the Yorks’ visit.
The Wattle Day League’s hospital wing was opened
on Friday at Keswick. Its eight rooms contain up-to-
date appliances for orthopedic treatment, and include
massage rooms, a mechanical treatment room, where
weakened or paralysed muscles are gradually restored,
and a re-education room, where men suffering from
shell-shock or paralysis, or fitted with artificial
limbs, are taught to walk. The wing, which cost
£2OOO, is the second of its kind in the Common-
wealth ; the other is at Caulfield, Victoria.
At a meeting of women in the Exhibition on
Sunday every seat was filled. The Governor presided,
and the principal resolution —a message of gratitude
and admiration to the men at the Front was moved
by Lady Galway in stirring words. The only other
resolution urged a more responsible note in national
life and some limitation of amusement and personal
extravagance* The Governor, on his part, made an
urgent plea for recruits.

Fund from Miss Robertson, of Moy Hall station, near
Narracoorte.
G. S. Reed, the Chief Justice’s Associate, has
enlisted.

Ex-vice-regal secretary Geoff Luttrell, who
fishes for his crust and health at Portsea,
has landed a very fine catch. Her name is
Alys Bridges, daughter of the late rich
Admiral. She is just back from three years
in the Fog. Fiance Geoff is the elder son
of his dad, who owns a place in Somerset.
His health is too bad to allow of any
soldiering. The gilt-edged Bridges are being
married off at a great rate. Sister Alfreda
entered for the matrimonial stakes with ex-
secretary Share last January. In February
sister Marjorie followed on with a Canadian
officer.
Dr. Marshall’s youngest daughter Doris
was quietly married at dad’s Scots’ Church
a few days ago. She’s a small fair-haired girl
who put in a year as V.A.D. in St. George’s
Hospital, London, after a long bout of
similar service in Melbourne. The young
man is Dr. Douglas Aitchison, a son of Dr.
Alex, and one of the residents at Alfred
Hospital. He’s in khaki and hopes to go
to the Front shortly. Doris got through the
ceremony in a snow-white plumage, with
some of mother’s wedding finery added.
Our sex’s much-abused garments have
their uses. At Malvern the other day a
charlady with a suit-case attracted the sus-
picion of a John Hop. He called in femi-
nine assistance, and a search brought to light
a vest, a tin of herrings, a knife, a spoon
and a child’s blouse in the lady’s stockings;
tied round her waist under her blouse a pair
of —er —trousers, and -inside her corsets two
plates and a serviette. A Bench composed of
unsympathetic males said it w’ould be gaol
for a month. Another of us, w’ho was not
presented at court, was recently shooed into
a private room at a P’ran emporium and
forced to return a ham which was fixed some-
how between knees and waist!

Phillips, the novelist, acted as hostesses for

Grand last week. Morris Phillips, as presi-
dent of the Shakspearean Society, came to
do honor to the Shakspearean guest. \S atson
in return supplied a peep behind the
scenes at the front. Dr. Leeper, the G. A.

Burly Lionel Belmore, of the G. B. Post
Co., has become almost as popular in Club-
land as he was on the boat coming here. He
is a genial soul with a repertoire of stories
second only to that .of Cyril Maude’s man-
ager, the inimitable “Tubby” Turner. Lionel,
who besides scoring in the legitimate, has
acted as director of several big film cos., is
a sister of Daisy Belmore, who decorated
“The Sign of the Cross” in the somewhat
distant past.

Alexander Watson opened his season on
Saturday night in the breathless Athenaeum
Hall. Half an hour before schedule time
the “House Full” placard was out, and a
busy lad at the foot of the stairs warned
latecomers that only reserved seats were
available. Dejected crowds were turned
away. Perhaps this will encourage Alex-
ander to conquer some other lecture hall.
The Athenseum, sacred to highbrow shows,
isn’t designed for such a pack. On Saturday
night lawyer Shelton’s wife fainted most
effectively and was carried out with her fair
hair, her rope of pearls and her white gown
all drooping. Watson was just then on the
Death of Wellington, and his “Bury the
great Duke” was very lingering.
It is always good to hear a sound art-
craftsman speak enthusiastically of an eager
pupil. The toiler in this instance is
F. J. Tarrant, glass painter, of Sydney; the
pupil Millie Field, of Victoria, now instruct-
ing at Ballarat School of Arts. Miss Field
was sent over to Sydney by her Education
Department to pick up what points she could
about this somewhat out-of-the-way craft;
and she proved to be an eagle on the picking-
up job and took a complete kit of informa-
tion back to Victoria. But it is a national
scandal that Australia allows these enthu-
siasts to potter along instead of keeping back
the flood of imports that • overwhelms them.
Sergeant A. Ilolgate, of Bunyip, lately
decorated with a D.S.M., made three attempts
to enlist before he was accepted. His sister
is well known in Melbourne art circles.
Another Bunyip lad who has made good is
Captain Bruce Forsyth. He landed on the
Peninsula as a private.
The people who regard a trained nurse as
overpaid at £3 3s. a week elevated their right-
thinking eyebrows when Dr. Connie Ellis, on
her return from an American trip, told them
that in the States a properly-accredited nurse
asks for £6 a week —and gets it.

A SAFE PROTECTOR
ABORIGINAHTIES
“Steve Hurt” : I reckon the quandong has
the longest loots of any vegetable in Australia. Ran
one the other day and found it 105 ft., and it was
never deeper than oin.
“H.V.E.”: Every bushman has his dead
cert boil cure. Here are a few. Scoop out a hollow
in a small onion and bind the bulb over the sore.
Keep it on all night and by morning its work will
be done. Another stringy-bark pathologist recom-
mends a small lump of “rosin” (pine resin, same as
is used for fiddle-strings) ground into Powder, mixed
with jam and swallowed. Said not only to be
efficacious in regard to existing boils, but also a
prophylactic. One bush vet. swears by shreds of
nutmeg chewed fine and swallowed, while a fourth
lauds the virtues of flower of sulphur made into a
paste with butter and taken in pill form. The way-
back herbalist, however, seldom turns dog on the
awful concoction which results from brook-lime
(hyssop) boiled down with Epsom salts. Present
scribe finds most virtue in Venice .(commonly styled
“Venus”) turpentine used as a plaster; while the
sulphide of calcium (lime) pill is perhaps the most
effective remedy employed by the faculty. This last
cure not only hastens the departure of existipg boils
but prevents the formation of fresh ones.
* * * *
“Timberite”: Here’s a scrub-itch cure.
Immerse in the hottest water bearable, make a clean
change of clothes, and scald and dry the infected
togs. The blacks’ cure comes next—toasting the
bare hide before a fire, exposing every part in turn,
a trick effectively used in Wide Bay timber camps
by that superb old-time bushman, Moody Allen.
“.T. C. Smith”; A body (abo. for whirl-
wind) visited our little mission-school the other
day, and I was surprised to see the look of terror on
all the little dark faces. When 1 questioned them as
to why they were so afraid 1 was told numerous
strange stories, but the one that struck me must was
related by a tiny girl. Once long ago two old
women were looking for thistles. They had been out
all day and were returning very tired, when they saw
a whirlwind coming towards them. Try how they
would they could not avoid it. In the midst of the
dirt and sand they saw a man with a looking-glass in
his hand. This man worked the mirror so that it
dazzled the old women completely. One of them
fell face downward and the other was carried around
with the dust, unable to take her eyes off the mirror.
When the body had passed over the first old woman
got up, hut no trace was ever found of the other.
The man took her away with the aid of the magic-
glass. All aborigines are frightened of a booly.
They would run for miles to get away from one.
I have heard of both men and women getting off a
sick bed to stagger , out of the way.

“Melos”: To quell the argument let me
megaphone that the black snake produces young in
batches of 15 to 20. The copper-head, which ranges
in color from reddish-brown to black, has more than
double that number at each increase. Now you can
all go to bed.
* * * *
“Trallwm” : It was midnight when an
excited Yarraville (Vic.) resident panted up to the
night-duty 'policeman with a story that there was a
burglar trying to force the lock of his shop door.
Sure enough, when John reached the spot there was
the villain crouched in the deep doorway, a tousled
creature with a grey-black beard ! As the two men
rushed up the would-be robber rose to his full height,
waved a bone-handled blue-steel pistol at his assail-
ants, and lunged at them viciously,, John unslung his'
revolver, knocked aside the burglar’s weapons—-for
there how seemed to be two of them—and lassoed
him skilfully round the neck. Therf he hauled out
his prisoner—an ancient goat, one of many of the
species that roam the streets and vacant lots of
Arcadian Yarraville.
“H.M.” : Lend a hand. I have a valuable
fox-terrier bitch about one year old. Five months
ago she apparently was troubled with worms, but
after a course of pellets she seemed to be right. Not
long afterwards, however, she began to stagger, and
finally dropped. For two days she remained down ;
then she regained her feet, although she was very
groggy for some day's. For three months she has
been well, but she has.peculiar fits, especially when
feeding. She looks at the food, then growls, then
does a whirlwind dance in an endeavor to bite her
rump, yelping all the time. The extreme tip of the
tail is raw with biting.
* * * *
“Bung”; People who advocate that native
trees should be planted along main roads should
first attack the regulation, in \N. S. Wales,
at any rate, that requires every bush within
half a chain of a roadside to be grubbed out. Where
there are overhanging trees that endanger the traffic
the rule is good, but the instruction that guides the
roadman is to pluck up everything that grows higher
than the grass, irrespective of how beautiful or
harmless the shrub may he. On the South Coast
roads many glories that once delighted the eye of
the traveller have been thus demolished. The road-
man is not allowed any discretion in the matter.
Near Cobargo 1 knew an artistically-inclined toiler
who took much pains in trimming-up an avenue of
gum saplings which he had allowed to remain when
cleaning out the bigger stuff. When the inspector
bluchered along he ordered the removal of the orna-
ments at once, if it couldn’t be done sooner.

“Gnzooma”: Coranderrk, where the Vie.
Government looks after the last of the old Yarra
tribe, begins to look very empty; and arrangements
arc being made for moving the remnants to the Lake
Tyers station. There are very few dinkums left,
and the 20-odd relics pale off from King Billy and
his family to the ahnost-vvhite lower classes. The
younger men earn good money on the surrounding
farms, but his Maj. replenishes his exchequer by
holding a sort of levee on Saturday mornings, when
he and a few of the peerage give exhibitions of
boomerang-throwing and fire-making in the old style,
what time the Imperial hat is passed round amongst
the holiday-makers from Healesville. The kiddies
cater for .another section by singing Moody and
Sankey hymns to an accompaniment played by one
of their number; for the corroboree has given place to
the catechism and the bull-roarer to the harmonium.
In the afternoon William lays aside the cares of
state and pTbceeds to invest the weekly revenues in
one of the local billiard-saloons. The old chap is a
great pool enthusiast and handles a cue as well as
any of them.
“Billjim Quiescent” : Have never noticed
any reference- to the singing frog. Its song is one
-of the most interesting sounds of the bush, but one
must fix attention on the quaint noise for a consider-
able time to appreciate the fact that these little
reptiles warble to each other with method, in
noticeable rotation and with many of the attributes
of real harmony. The'bass invariably begins with a
slow rhythmic cadence of five to seven notes. There
is a slight pause and a baritone joins in with a
slightly quicker, but more sedate roll of 10 to 12
notes. Another pause, and a tenor or soprano, with
a fast staccato tremolo, ends the overture. Often the
bass will begin the second round, when some im-
petuous high “C’s” at a distance cut him short, an cl
then there is quiet for fully three minutes, suggest-
ing that the' interruption is resented. The overture
begins again with the bass, and tuns through another
score, this time ending up with a bizarre medley of
the voices in chorus. It seems to me that each
concert is composed of singers within a radius of
30ft. Any voice outside that limit is a hostile
institution. I have noted the same rotation of
voices night after night, and rarely does the bass
exert himself beyond seven notes, whilst the tenor
trebles that number. This coficert-paTty of mine has
four distinct overtures which I recognise each night.
Two are decidedly sentimental; one is suggestive of a
dashing march, and the other is nondescript, or at
the best only up to the standard of “What’s the
Matter with Father?” It W'as on March 2 that the
first singing frog ventured to lift its voice near my
camp last year. For three nights it appeared to be
merely applying for a job. When the answer did
come, there was a stirring chorus within an hour,
and it gained gradually in volume until the singers
settled down for five months.
THE MISPLACED MEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He sagged upon the tender grass
Where twinkling butterflies
Coquetted with the scented mass
Of gum bloom. In his eyes
A dreamy speculation lay ;
His hat was knocked about;
His clothes were old, and fell away,
And from his broken boots in play
His curling toes peeked out.
“I ort ’ave 'bin a dook,” said he,
“Or else a noble earl.
’Ard work ain’t possible to me;
I wasn’t' born to whirl
A nax, or swing a navvy’s pick,
Or even shake a sword.
For all that, I’m amazin’ quick
With hard old drink, or soft young chick.
I ort ’ave bin a lord.
“I ’ate coarse clo’es ’n’ bread ’n’ cheese;
I’d love a royal bed,
With linen sheets ’n’ tapestries
Hung close above me ’ead.
I ’ave no gifts; I’m positive
I cannot do a thing,
’N’ through the changin’ year to live
I have to take what others give.
I ort ’ave bin a king.
“ ’N’ there are dooks, ’n’ lords, ’n’ earls
Who do not want to lie
’N’ watch the lily where it curls
Agin the driftin’ sky.
They’re up ’n’ doin’, so I’m told,
As long as they can see.
What good to them uncounted gold ?
The gift of ease they do not ’old—
They orter have bin me.
“This world is all a sorry mess.
It has its idle poor
Who can’t enjoy their idleness,
But suffer and endure.
It has its wealthy class that feels
For work a fearful itch.
Yet to the worthless poor it deals
Out endless stoush, but never weals
Tile undeservin’ rich!”
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

A HANDY REMEDY FOR
INDIGESTION AND DYSPEPSIA.
For a long time it has been recognised
that bisura ted magnesia forms the best of
all remedies for indigestion, dyspepsia, heart-
burn, flatulence and other forms of digestive
and stomach trouble, many remarkable cases
having been reported by those who have made
a practice of taking half a teaspoonful of
this simple antacid in a little water after
meals. Only one difficulty stood in the way
of the universal adoption of this method of
treatment, and that was in the original pow-
der form bisurated magnesia was not adapted
for use by those who were compelled to take
meals away from home. But even this slight
difficulty lias at last been overcome, and the
genuine bisurated magnesia is now' available
in the form of small compressed tablets, put
up in handy pocket flasks, and obtainable of
high-class chemists everywhere.— Advt.

“Shepherd”: Allow me to have a say as
to the sagacity of birds in finding water. “H.W.F.”
(B. 4/4/T8) is correct in praising galahs, but when
he puts these cockies before the bronzewing pigeon
he’s right out of it, like the Crown Prince at
Verdun. The bronzewing, too, is the easiest of
bushies to follow by sound, and at dusk many a
weary traveller strains his ears for the eaw-caw-coo
of the bird, which always camps near water and
continues his anthem from the time he goes to drink
till he falls to sleep.
* * * *
“Riom”: It was up on a Gulf Country
station, and it was the first visit of a parson for a
donkey’s age. Of course, he must christen the kid.
Mother handed out a Prayer Book saying (with a
happy smile at her luck in finding it): “You see,
Mr. Twiddlethum, we have the Scriptures if we are
far from church.” The pilot reverently opened the
covers, and as he did so the contents floated out in a
cloud of dust. White ants! I was the kid to be
operated on, and I can see the look of horror on
mother’s face yet.
THE PRIZE.
“I wonder what the Germans would like to
take Australia for?”
“Well, y’ oughter seen some of the champion
cows down at the Easter show.”
“Bad Australian”: So “Leo Lear” has
discovered a rock wallaby in the gorges of N.E.
Victoria with a bunch of fur on its further extremity
exactly like a fox’s, and its favorite haunt is the
kurrajong top! This discovery surprises me, as I
thought I knew much about the fauna of N.E.
Vic. May I take the liberty of asking “Leo”: (1)
What part of the Upper Murray country or N.E.
t ic. is the habitat of this curious furry-tailed wal-
laby which climbs the kurrajong? (2) What he
means by “kurrajong” tree (the scientific monniker
will identify the vegetable)? (3) When did the
wallaby or ’roo family quit wearing the coarse,
bristly hair which always ornamented and protected
the farthest end of its reinforcement in the bad old
days of my youth? (4) Is “Leo” prepared to
solemnly deny that he was thinking of the foxy-
tailed, black-pointed, giant silver-grey mountain
’possum—the ’possum with the grunt—which climbs
trees and is as much like a fox as any ’possum knows
how to be?
* •♦ * *
“H.W.F.”: Over 50 years ago I saw an
American, who was making a damper, use “yeast”
made by dropping hot coals into water as described
by “A.V.H.” (B. 11/4/’18). After straining he
used the water to mix with the flour. Probably it
is carbonic-acid gas that causes the dough to rise.
Anyway, the damper when cooked was O.K. and light.
“Dug-out”: Who said that strychnine will
kill any animal? Let me answer that it won’t
stiffen swine. Last year I had a very fine crop
of jam melons that were greatly admired by a
herd of pigs from a neighbor’s farm. In a few
days the crop began to diminish at an alarming
rate, so I strolled over and begged Wire-whiskers
to keep his livestock at home. After a third pro-
test without success I issued an ultimatum, threaten-
ing his hams with destruction by poison if they
persisted in their ravagings. A' deaf ear was
turned to this threat also, so I set to work. Select-
ing half a dozen large melons I cut each into
four parts, carefully inserted poison in the pieces
and served the dessert to the porkers, who ate it
with great relish. Next morning I got up extra-
early expecting to see the countryside strewn
with dead swine. The place was certainly strewn
with swine, but they were all very much alive and
boring into my patch with even greater energy
than before. I brought a 22-caJibre rifle to bear
on the subject then and peace was soon signed.
* * * *
“Umfaan”: Camels are supposed to be
useful animals in Australia, but in Egypt, when I
was in the Camel Trans-
port Corps, we mad,e
racers out of them. Our
captain, a Victorian,
conceived an idea for a
saddle. It was the ordi-
nary-issue, military-pat-
tern affair, but with
padding underneath to fit
the conformation of the
camel’s back. With the
addition of a couple of
surcingles fore and aft
and a crupper of rope,
the wildest camel became
an easy thing for a
horseman to ride. We
guided by the kazam.
Then we threw out chal-
lenges far (and wide for
races—white men up.
We always won. In fact,
we were trying to teach
special Hegeems to jump
when we were recalled to
our respective units on
the departure of the
main body of Anzacs to
Prance. The trot of, the
camel on our saddles' was
easier than a horse.
Other camel transport
commanders rode from
every camp to obtain the
pattern of our saddles.
Incidentally, the camel
of Egypt is an unusual
beast. One day mine
sneezed out of his nos-
tril a thing the size and
approximate shape of a
cockroach, but white —
white with the whiteness
of something taken from
the depths of an under-
ground sunless cave. The
native vet., who was near
by, picked the horror up
and laid it on his palm.
It was nothing, he said,
•merely a parasite which
lives on the brain and
comes out when full
grown!
“Block 10”: The best thing I ever heard
(or saw) come from “Jimmie Pannikin” was when
he was examining my class a couple of years ago.
Asked to give a geography lesson, Pm afraid I played
to the gallery a bit by choosing the cow country as
my subject. When it was over Inspector “Pannikin”
bounded from the table. “And now, boys,” he
said, “did you ever see a cow-cocky shake hands?
No? Well, come here, my lad, and I’ll show you.
‘Good-morning, Mr. Shorthorn, and how are you?
How do you think the war’s going? Wonderful
season this, isn t it ? The rest was drowned in
laughter, for my noble was busy jmlking the kid’s
Angers into an imaginary bucket.
JUSTIFICATION FOR HIS EXTINCTION.
THE ABTIST: “I can’t say Art has ever given me much of a living.”
HIS CANDID FRIEND: “Considering your idea of Art you’re lucky to he allowed to live at

“Cudjewa”: As a cure for rheumatism,
try the Australian clematis, which grows all along
the southern coast of the continent. Binghi used
it frequently for bruises, cuts and sprains, and his
decoction possessed certain anaesthetic powers of a
remarkable strength, removing pain as well as heal-
ing. I have seen cases of severe rheumatism among
the old settlers in the Cape Otway and Mt. Gambier
districts practically cured by the use of this plant.
The leaves and stems are stewed for three or four
hours. After the?' liquid cools, bottle it securely.
For cuts, etc., rub it on the affected part; for
rheumatics, make a bath of it diluted freely with
water. I’ll back it for the above-mentioned pur-
poses against any patent medicine on the market.
“Kell B.”: No wonder Australian horses
are murdered when £1 1 Os. is the price offered
for “breaking in” a good animal. Men who
know their business are not to be had for
that. Last January I finished a mob at
Mvalla station at £3 per head and am
now at work on 50 at Wangoola. These I am
educating—not “breaking” anything about them;
neither spirits, constitution nor anything else should
be broken in order to teach a horse its work —at the
same figure, accommodation for self and understudy
given in. Before the war Germany paid a pupil
of mine a handsome sum (after putting my system
to a severe test, making discipline the cardinal
virtue) to teach remount officers; yet, although,
before this, the system had been offered to the
N. S. Wales Agricultural Department, the “break-
ers” in this country are still at large. They even
“teach” Hawkesbury College students, although
Professor Stewart, who was deputed to report
to the Government on a test in the Sydney Show
Grounds given by the aforesaid pupil of mine
about nine years ago, pronounced it to be a great
improvement on the old, clumsy, unscientific devices.
I’m not saying that many old-style breakers were
not very smart men with a horse; and often good
results were obtained when the horse’s instinct
was keen and true enough to make up for the follies
of unscientific usage. But why doesn’t the R.A.S.
committee settle the question by giving £lOO
prize to the man who gives the most practical
demonstration of horse handling? The Society
spends much to encourage the better breeding of
horses, and surely the better handling of them is
almost equally important. Let anyone ask Dr.
Maekellar what Germany thought of this question
before the war and how much the Hun would spend
on the education of a horse, especially a war horse.
“J.A.G.”: The only cocksure way of pre-
venting mice getting into bagged wheat is to put
it on stands with tin protruding over the supports
in the shape of an arrowhead and then erecting
an iron fence around as a double precaution. It costs
little.
• * • •
“Mick Anick”: In Bananaland I had a
paw-paw tree which would produce only male
flowers. Someone told me to drive an iron nail
into the trunk and I’d have better luck. I
put in three for good measure, and after
that got 75 per cent, of female flowers and a
correspondingly good fruit-crop. I passed
the remedy to others who were troubled in
the same way and it never failed. The posi-
tions of the nails didn’t seem to matter,
nor did the time of driving them.
“Condowrie”: An emu taught me what
great alterations in animal habits a change of environ-
ment makes. We caught this fellow young and
kept him as a pet. Our home had a salt-water
frontage and we kids used to put in our spare time
bathing and paddling in the bay. Always we took
Bill with us, and he soon enjoyed the water like a
duck, and swam nearly as well as one. Another
hobby of his was devouring young crabs and small
fish, and this habit never left him even when he
grew to full size.
* * * *
“Pick” : Bees do not often take up their
residence underground, but in the crevices under a
granite precipice beside the Maclaughlin River, on
Monaro (N.S.W.), many colonies have defied the
chilly winters and honey-hunters for decades. ' Their
comb, hanging in sheets among the clefts 20 feet
inwards, is estimated at tons. On an early summer
day, when the wattle orchard is blooming below,
the myriads of foragers almost shadow the gullies.
A similar bee-city once flourished uninvaded above
the breakers on the sea-wall cliffs of Point Perpen-
dicular. It was demolished by a mighty fall of
rock.
* # * *•
“Bacon Stealer” : Billjim was due for leave
to Blighty, but at the last moment all liberty
was stopped. Discussing his bad luck with his mates
he remarked: “I’m that unlucky that if I was an
angel flying about in Heaven some would shoot
me for a crow!”
QUEERED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Buchanan was an axeman bold ;
No rival had he in the land.
The championship he’d sworn to hold,
And when he stood up, axe in hand,
And set a log, the chips that flew
Hid Angus from the people’s view.
The axeman dearly loved his blade.
It shone like silver in the sun
With every swinging stroke he made ;
And when the sudden chop was done
He patiently for hours would sit
And put a razor-edge on it.
Then came a Tassy, long and slim,
And challenged Angus to a match—
Of course our champion jumped at him.
The chop came off in Thaller’s patch.
The stranger won. Sad Angus said:
“It beats me, boys; my axe was dead!”
That evening Angus took his mate,
And nearly killed the hapless bloke.
We knew he had deserved his fate
When Angus stammered, fit to choke:
“He put me on the bloomin’ shelf—
He used my axe to shave himself!”
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.

“Ornis” : The wharfie whisky-wasters
mentioned by “Oyo” (B. 4/4/T8) were
amateur bunglers compared with the gang
that recently operated on sugar stored in
sheds on the new wharves at Launceston.
As the decking had shrunk somewhat, tar-
paulins were first laid down. The doors
were kept locked, and a watchman put on.
When the time came to empty the sheds,
the bottom tier of bags were found flat -and
empty, each with a slit in the under side.
Each slit corresponded with one in the tar-
paulins directly over spaces in the planking.
The method of the sugar shifters was simple.
When the tide was a&pui half-way up the
piles, a boat was taken under the wharves
as near as possible to the stores, and then
it was only a matter of crawling over the
ties, knife and bucket in hand, until the
right spot was reacted.
“Croson”: No; as The Bulletin says,
the latest scheme of the Commonwealth
Government isn’t likely to result in Aus-
tralia growing much flax. The British
Government has offered the Commonwealth
Government £I7(J a ton for fibre delivered in
England, and the price fixed for the raw
flax is £5 per ton. This is how it works out:
13 tons of raw flax equals one ton of fibre,
and before the war the Victorian flax mills
offered from £2O to £33 for enough raw flax
to produce one ton of fibre, for which they
received from £4O to £43. That paid the
growers so ill that the mills presently had to
shut clown for lack of raw material. Now it is
proposed to pay the grower £O3 for what
will yield the mills £l7O, less freight, etc.
The miller would seem to he on a good
wicket, hut th(> grower—well, considering
the present prices of wool, wheat, mutton,
■etc., the chances are the grower won’t grow
flax.
“T. 0.1.” : The jubilee of St. John’s Angli-
can Church, Glebe (Sydney), recalls that
soon after the tabernacle was built a publi-
can’s license was applied for in respect of a
house near by. The pilot of the church, a
Creole named Saliniere, anil his congrega-
tion violently opposed the application, but
it was a time when J’s.P. crowded the Bench
on licensing days and granted tickets for
anything that had a roof. So the license
was obtained and the publican added insult
to injury by naming his grog-shop St. John's
Tavern. It continued to mock the holy
children till 1882.

“J.S.N.” : How is this for an extra steady
job? One I’riddy, of Gympie, Bananaland,
has for the past 30 years been town grave-
digger. It is the first and only billet he has
had since he came to the country. During his
time he has filled une cemetery, and has a
new one well on the way. Now 82, he is
getting beyond hard digging, but is still
always there to superintend.

“Noana” : Re Victorian doctors and lodges.
“Ato” (B. 11/4/T8) states that it is the
nound-a-week sick-pay that draws people into
lodges and the medico is merely a side-line.
Perhaps “Ato” will explain, then, why the
A.N.A. does not flourish in X. S. Wales and
Bananaland. where cheap medical attention
\yas denied the members. 1 am glad, by the
way. to have “Ato’s” assurance that the
lodges are making a profit out of the medical
pool. Everybody should he satisfied now—the
doctors certainly are.
“Oof Bird”: Among the most audacious
gold thieves who flourished on the Westralian
fields in the bloom days was a German mine-
manager who worked in with another Ger-
man. the owner of a treatment plant, and
stole amalgam and zinc slimes wholesale.
Both grew very wealthy, and when the
Chamber of Mines detective force began to
get around they hurriedly left the country.
Another was an assayer with a small reduc-
tion plant, who had a baker as a partner.
The baker collected telluride ore specimens
on his rounds and secreted them in the
loaves which he delivered to the assayer.
A lucrative business, was done in this way.
A third, head of a syndicate of three, was
metallurgist on one of the big mines. A
barmaid received the stolen stuff from him
and passed it on to the owner of an alleged
gold-producing show for treatment. All
three shared alike and made a lot of money.
Scores got nice-sized parcels of gold to-
gether and then went out into the bush and
made “new finds.” The papers raved about
the prospects of these for a little while and
then the announcement of "a return” enabled
the finders to lodge their gold in the hank
without any questions being asked. Nowadays
the bank officials are more inquisitive and
the police inure watchful.
“Mookumwvbra" : During the currency of
the war the N. S. Wales Forestry Depart-
ment is allowing millers a rebate of 10 p.c.
of the timber royalties. Why are they
singled out for preferential treatment as tax-
payers? Either the royalties are just, and
should be paid in full, or they are inequitable,
and should not have been increased. The
war is no excuse whatever for the rebate.
On the contrary, in fact. Other taxes have
been increased on account of the war, and
sawmiHers are, by reason of the stoppage
of imports, on a much better wicket than
they have been for a long while. And it is
a long while since they were on a bad one.

“0.K.” looks into another cradle :—*
The prettiest of local mechanical industries is
flourishing at Collingwood, where the Australian.
Weaving Co. lias an unpretentious factory, and
holds land available for a large extension when the
times and the tariff are ripe. Some nine or 10
years ago Henry Hatton, a well-known Melbourne
canvasser, suggested to Emery and Regg, a firm of
ticket-writers, that the demand for woven labels,
which all came from Europe, called for local supply.
They put their heads and funds together and started,
in a small way, the Australian Weaving Co., with
Ifatton as manager. Seven years back they brought
out Charles Underwood from England to run the
silk factory, together with a foreman and a consign-
ment of girls learned in the craft. Initiation of
Australian hands has followed in due course. The
eo., as its beautifully-woven card sets forth, manu-
factures woven labels for coats, waterproofs, rugs,
blankets, underclothing and ties; also cap ribbons,
hatbands, badges—in fact, any ticket with silk
lettering on it that isn’t too big for the looms. Thus
the local clothing-manufacturing trade is relieved of
the necessity to import “stylish” labels for its goods;
the Pelaco shirt factory, for instance, keeps one of
the looms constantly employed in turning out 7000
shirt-labels per week. At present the Weaving Co.,
employing 70 hands, confines its energies to the pro-
duction of small fancy goods; and from these to the
weaving of piece goods is a far step. But the co.
has them in prospective.
» » * *
“Floater": “0.K.” says (B. 25/4/’18)
that the local woollen manufacturers have
failed “to seize this war opportunity” and
goes on to remark that “the mills are as
backward as ever.” As a matter of fact,
the Australian mills have grabbed the oppor-
tunity with both hands. To my own
knowledge, one of the Ballarat mills is only
limited in its production by the capacity of
its machinery. It is turning out excellent
all-wool material, which is going directly on
to the hacks of Australians without the con-
nivance of the importers in Flinders-lane.
The reason why this, is not; generally known
is that the local consumer has his absurd
prejudices flattered by being told that his
Australian suit was made by an English
loom. Many a man in the Commonwealth
is wearing alleged “English tweeds” which
came from Mnrrickville, Ballarat or Geelong.
Admittedly the Australian cloth has not
quite the finish of the best imported ; but its
life is greater because there is more of the
real stuff' in it. And nothing can take the
place of that —superior workmanship or any-
thing else.

“E.D.” : The best bit of bluff I’ve met was
pulled off in the bad days following the boom.
Three Bohemians in -Melbourne—a freelance
paragrapber and bard, a promising young
artist, and a gentle hornblower in a local
theatre orchestra —came, by a stroke of for-
tune. into possession of the centre house of
a rather fine suburban terrace. The French
born, in point of fact, had been appointed
caretaker in the absence of profitable tenants.
The three moved in, and on the fifth night the
bard awoke in his bed on the floor to find a
stranger standing bewildered among the few
trifles that furnished the room. “Hello!”
sold the versifier blandly, “who are you?”
The man presented a revolver. , “You won
five hundred at the races to-day.” he said.
“I’ve been put on to you, ’n’ I want it.”
“Some mistake,” said the bard. “Oh. no.
there ain’t. Mr. Wallstein —me information’s
good. So fork it over.” “All wrong,” said
the bard. “You had better try next door on
the right.” “Ah, rats!” snorted the burglar.
“I was in there, ’n’ the bloke told me Wall-
stein lived in here.” The bard sat up to hand
out a clinching argument: “See here,” he
said. “If I’d won £5OO I’d never go to bed.”
With an oath the burelar looked around the
bare room and fled. The man whom he had
first visited on the right was actually Wall-
stein. the party who had won the five
hundred.
THE PITIFUL LACK OF PENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Three men I’ve known of recent years.
James,had a lih’ral miml.
lie felt the cark of all the cares
That torture humankind.
A thousand pounds a year had he.
cripple in the ditch
’Twould tear his gentle heart to see.
lie said: “These evils would not be
If I were only rich!”
Dear Ernest was another friend.
Each fortnight of his life
lie had good eighty pounds to spend,
And kept a frugal wife.
His heart went out in pity to
The wretch without a stitch,
The dozens who in anguish stew.
“To think,” said he, “what I might do,
If I were oiilv rich!”
The third of these an income had
Of sixty pounds a week.
And human suffering made him sad.
I often heard him speak
Of yearnings that possessed his soul.
Said he: “I fairly itch
To lift the fallen, as a whole.
Ah, Snell, I'd play a splendid role,
If 1 were only rich'!”
VICTORIA.
SNELL.
NEW HATS FOR OLD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(Author’s note; The theological purpose of this
poem is to trace, as far as possible, the assimilation
of a hat by a personality.)
Whene'er a nice new hat I get
I hurry round the town and fret;
As stiff as steel and just as dead
Seems that strange curse upon my head
I don’t belong to it, and fear
That old friends sighting it may sneer ;
It is too smooth and brushed, I see.
For that old worried face that's Me.
My old hat is a trusty friend,
Scrapped like most friends are in the end;
This strange new thing I’d like to drop
In some dark drain, reeks of its shop.
It makes the rest of me look old
Till once along the street it’s rolled
.By sportive winds amongst men’s feet;
That makes it lose its stiff conceit.
Then one day, all its newness gone,
It meekly roosts my head upon ;
Battered and shabby as can be,
I know 7 at last it’s part of Me.
N.s.w*.
THE RELIGIOUS EDITOR.
The Inky Way.
Such is the paper shortage in England that,
in addition to those that have gone out
altogether, more than 800 dailies and week-
lies have been forced to raise their selling
price. The Times has gone hack to three-
pence. in order, as it quaintly puts it, to
reduce its circulation. There have, of course,
been pourparlers in Australia too ; but so
far only some of the country papers (which
as a rule don’t compete with one another)
have been able to come to any agreement.
Still, the chances are that something will be
forced upon the bigger offices before the
trouble is all over. And if they come to-
gether for that they will probably come to-
gether for the co-operative purchase of
paper after the war.
* * * *
“Pilk”; Over here a Billjim never appears
without his 01’ Brown Hat, and is not known
in a Tommy cap; yet nearly every Austra-
lian paper produces pictures which show
him as a sort of loose-built Tommy. Tell
them, please, to stop it. Our “slouch” is
our trade-mark ; and how do you think an
angel would feel about it if. when he went
away on an expedition, the papers of
heaven pictured him wearing, not wings, but
a spiked tail?

“Glen Iluntly” : I nominate C. J. Dennis
as the handiest man among Australian
poets. He is as much at home with a spoke-
shave as with a foumtaini-pen. He made
the writing-table at which he works —not
only put it together, but cut and polished
the timber; laid on his own water-supply
at Tolangi, and built most of his own furni-
ture and a good deal of the house. Also
he repairs his own motor-bike and watch,
and a while back, being seized with a desire
to address Doreen in song suitably accom-
panied, set to work and made himself a
banjo.

“J.F.H.” from London : :
I hope things have changed; but in my day -in
Australia the general experience of reporters seemed
to correspond with the facts and impressions recorded
in a strongly-worded “remonstrance” from a leading
London reporter in the latest issue of the Christian
World, the most largely-circulated of the English
Protestant weeklies. Speaking from an experience
of 25 years, this writer says that at R.C. functions
he has invariably found a chair reserved for him
in the best possible position for seeing and hearing.
His work was further facilitated by the provision of
bocks supplying details and explanations of the
ceremonies of the day, and lists of the clergy and
prelates taking part were promptly passed round the
press table. Also a priest was alwai's in the vicinity
to answer with “easy, unobtrusive courtesy” any
questions that might occur to the unfamiliar or for-
getful or heretical inkslinger. And after the func-
tion was over there was generally a pleasant lunch
in the neighboring episcopal palace or presbytery at
which the pressmen were welcome and honored guests.
The agreeableness of the affair did not end even
there, for as a rule the editor next day received a
note from the presbytery or the palace conveying
thanks for the excellence and fidelity of the report.
“Such treatment as this is an enormous contrast to
that I frequently receive from the clergy of the Pro-
testant denominations.” He has repeatedly found it
a hard struggle to get even the “plain, ordinary
faets.” He declares that the most unpleasant
memories of his long journalistic career are asso-
ciated with the discourtesy and lack of consideration
so commonly displayed by clergymen of his own
faith. He wonders they do not perceive that “Rome
has a remarkably intelligent perception of the value
of the Press, even the little local paper.” They
often cavil at the undue notice and space given to
R.C. functions, but “forget that it is quite open to
them to study the comfort and convenience of the
Press and treat its representatives as considerately
and sympathetically as Rome does.”

STUMPED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In print I’m very solemn
(At home I’m only “Dad”) :
For I turn out the column
That deals with fact and fad -
There's not a single answer
I’m not supposed t<r know.
From “What's the cause of eanc-er?”
To “Where is Jellicoe?”
I am the office Solon —
An Inky WayzgoCse T;
From colophon to colon
There’s nought I can’t supply.
For speeches out of Hansard,
Or yearly falls of rain,
In “Information Answered”
You need not seek in vain.
And yet, when home arriving,
My long day’s labor done,
I find my best contriving
In vain with my small son.
Who mocks my aspect legal,
And asks why God made ants,
And wonders where the eagle
Has bought his “feather pants”:
And what's the solid reason
For freckles on my face;
And why there are no trees on
The road in Martin-place ;
And, when the dinner’s cooking,
If I can hear the smell.
And if the snails are looking
To find a better shell;
And “What makes worms go squiggle?”
And “Why are rabbits wild?”
And “Will dead tadpoles wiggle?”
lie asks in accents mild.
And, when my brain is addled,
I pause to wonder why
The paper should be saddled
With such a chump as I!
victoria.
THE OLD POT.

A GOOD HAIR TONIC.
An excellent hair tonic that is invaluable
for keeping the scalp in a healthy condition,
destroying dandruff and making the hair
grow, is made by dissolving dram Menthol
Crystals in 3 ounces Bay Rum and then
adding 1 ounce Lavona de Composee. Let
it stand for half an hour and it is then
ready for use. Rubbed well into the scalp
with the finger-tips once or twice a day this
tonic will be found to be very beneficial,
scalp itching and irritation disappearing
almost immediately on applying. This pre-
scription contains no dye, and can be pre-
pared by any chemist, or you may procure
the ingredients and mix them yourself at
heme. — Advt.

POLITICAL
POINTS
“Always give the Hun the benefit of the
doubt.”-—Motto of many alleged Australians.
After all, there is hope for Australia.
Peter said “No” twice, as Australia has
done, denying its master, which is democ-
racy; yet Peter was able l afterwards to say:
“Stand up; I myself also am a man!” and
no slouch of a man either.
If Watt's jaw doesn’t belie him he will
have a violent row with the military and
probably also the naval heads within two
months- —he may hang out that long; cer-
tainly not longer. The whole circus reeks
of sham and make-believe, and one reason
why the arrival of the American into the
business is so enormously important is that
he isn’t standing the old nonsense. Hence,
for one thing, Focli’s appointment.
DOUBLE SHIFT.
TONY : “You say Toms returning to the
Front. But he's done his hit.”
MILLY : “Yes, I know. But he’s going hack
to do yours.”
At the very moment when the Defence
Department is feeling a little less gloomy
over the recruiting returns, it declares that
the rule about “general enlistment” must
continue. Yet the improvement in the
figures is the best of all proofs that the rule
is a rotten one, for recruiting fell away to
next to nothing until Carmichael came along
with authority to raise “a thousand,” with
the promise that they should "leave together,
train together and light together.”
One of the most humorous performances
of the military and naval administration of
Australia has been in the part of Sherlock
Holmes. For a little while troops used
to march proudly through the cities; and, as
the Americans know and as we have proved
too, nothing creates an atmosphere so favor-
able for recruiting as military marches
accompanied by martial music. Then, when
recruiting was doing quite nicely, Sherlock
Holmes came upon the stage. “Ah.” said
the wizard, “this won't do! If the Ger-
mans see these men marching down to the
wharves, some person among them may
guess that they tire going aboard.” Then
Sherlock Holmes, having made the ■wonder-
ful discovery all by himself, breathed hard,
and ordered the parades to cease and the
martial music to stop. From that moment
“A WEE DEOCH-AN-DORRIS."
troops were brought into the city at early
morn and marched without noise through the
streets and —ha. ha ! —the Germans were
outwitted! They didn’t know that the
troops in the camps were ever sent away;
they couldn’t see the cheerless, dispiriting
early-morning shuffles through the streets;
they were blind, of course, to the gather-
ing of relatives and sweethearts of the men
a*: the wharf ; they didn’t hear the cheerful
coek-a-doodling of every ferry-boat in the
neighborhood as the troopship pulled out;
they didn’t see her lying alongside mostly
for a whole day. So the amazingly
clever Sherlock Holmes saved our sol-
diers from the submarines. And in
due time he succeeded, with his brothers of
the military and his half-brothers of the
navy, in almost putting an end to recruiting.
A new man. Orchard. Minister for Recruit-
ing, has come upon the scene; and of course
he has kicked over the traces. The marches
have been restored. The music plays again.
The crowds cheer: the flags fly. And
recruits come in again. But we shall see.
They have started to tame him already. Or,
at least, they have started on the attempt.
The Boers, who have a rough gift of epi-
gram, divided the adult male community
during their big war into “hands-uppers”
and “bitter-enders.” The terms are worth
adopting. Carmichael might have done even
more than he did if he had advertised:
“Bitter-enders Wanted.”
Pearce has come again to the defence of
somebody or other’s suicidal scheme of
“general enlistment,” which is more or less
still in vogue.. Pearce says that it is quite
a mistake to suppose that all men enlisted
will be put into the infantry: when they get
to the other side they will be drafted into
engineers, artillery or other branch which
needs men and for which they appear most
suitable. This torpedoes Pearce’s own
case. The only justification for “general
enlistment” would be that all hands were
wanted for infantry. If. on the other hand,
men are wanted for other branches, why in
the name of commonsense shouldn’t they be
offered the inducement of service in those
branches? If a man knows that he is fitted
by inclination and training for, say, the
engineers, why not let him enlist for ’ the
engineers if that branch wants men? In
his youth Pearce wanted to be a carpenter.
What hope of getting Pearce’s services
would a contractor have had if he had said
to the young man : “You enlist with me. I
may make a carpenter of you; but then,
again, I may make a cook of you or a
blacksmith”? And what a fool the con-
tractor would be to say this if at that
moment he wanted a carpenter’s apprentice?
Labor in Victoria is of course entitled to
say ait revoir to its wits if it be so minded,
but it was a poor trick to put up solicitor
Holmes for the Flinders seat and then blast
his chances by pronouncements advocating
the repudiation of war debts and the
“immediate cessation of fighting, as peace
with a British victory is impossible.” When
you consider how pleased the Hun must
have felt at these official declarations, it isn’t
an unfair thing to suspect that the enemy
inspired them.
The Dutchman these days is between the
shallow, muddy sea and the deep, muddy
Hun. The Allies have borrowed some of his
ships, and he has protested. The Hun wants
to borrow some of his railways, and he
has protested. Also the
Hun demands a supply
of sa.id and gravel from Hol-
land, and part of the land
is below sea-level already.
If much gravel is dug out of
it the sea may come in. The
maintenance of the country’s
neutrality is like the effort to
balance a gallon of loose
water on a hair. If it gets
one ultimatum it will almost
certainly get another. Then
if it throws in its lot with
the Allies, the Hun will do
his best to make it another
Belgium. If it joins the Hun,
that person will borrow its
army and most of its goods,
giving the glad hand and an
lOU in return, while the
Allies will bombard its coast
and take its colonies. Cer-
tainly they may take them in
a friendly way, making allow-
ance for the country's diffi-
culties. But, then, Holland’s
second-best possession was
taken over in a friendly way
more than 100 years ago,
just to save it from the other
fellow, and the place was
never handed back. So the
man with Van before his
name—to distinguish him
from his neighbor Yon —
drinks his schnapps with no
light heart.
Within the past week or two proposals have
been made for (1) a complete holiday for
purposes of humiliation and prayer; (2)
ditto for Anzac Day; (3) ditto for Empire
Day; (4) ditto every Saturday, the working
week being cut down to 40 hours. How
about having a complete holiday when the
Huns land?
From George D. Meudell: —
The position is much too serious to be disregarded.
The financial heads ought to be got together: their
advice, after consultation, should be made available
to the Treasury. I estimate the ratio of gold to
deposits held by Australian banks at 64 p.c. (Decem-
ber, 1917) against 14 p.c. as at June, 1914 ; and the
total Australian holding of gold as 13 p.c. against 22
p.c. in June, 1914. Three actions are urgent:—
(1) Stop issuing notes.
(2) By edict, stop exporting gold.
(3) Float a gold loan in New York for the sole
behoof of the Treasury note issue, say, £20,000,000.
Two other actions ought to follow:
(4) Forbid importation of liqudr and luxuries.
(5) Offer a subsidy for gold-winning.
I was in the m:|gJstrom of 1890-5 and have financial
goose-flesh to-day.
THE OPTIMIST.
“Poor little darling! It must be his toosey-
pegs, or perhaps his little tummy."
“Anyway, my dear, there's no fear of sleep-
ing sickness."
Melbourne llekald puts up this heading:
Legislation Will Go On In Mr. Hughes's
