DIDN'T WANT TO BE PALE.
COLONEL; “Look here, No. 60, this will
never do! Why don't you salute me
when you see me?"
No. 60: “I dunno, Colonel. I ’ad a kinder
idea you ’ad me set!”
The fowl, having 10 walk all the way, may
be long in getting home to roost; but it
gets there. If Australia manufactured all
the requisites for a railway, railway-building
could progress, on a sound paper-money
basis, much as usual. But almost everything
is imported except the sleepers and the per-
manent way ; wherefore now, thanks to the
scarcity of ships and the ultimate fact that
foreign goods and freights call for gold
payments while gold is unusually scarce, the
mileage run on existing N. S. Wales lines
has had to be reduced by nearly 16 per cent,
through lack of vehicles, and there is a forced
reduction of 19 per cent, on tramway mileage
from the same cause.
OUR GENEROUS ATTITUDE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The more European nations are interested in the
Pacific the less Australia —a remote outpost of the
European race—lias to fear.” —Labor manifesto.
Let them all come! O'er coral isle and reef
Let all their flags to all the breezes blow;
Our own democracy can know no grief
While warships in their thousands come and go.
Their grinning guns along the cowering tide
Will merely be our safety and our pride.
We do not care a rap for anything
The aftermath of indecisive hate
To these far shores of plenty yet may bring.
Mildly we sit at home and calmly wait,
What time the happy pumpkin blossoms blow
And Manly ferryboats move to'and fro.
The greed that fli es them shall not touch us here;
No fear have we of war or battle shock.
Their ships shall pass the land of bread and beer
To grasp the empire of a lonely rock ;
Or else a fever swamp in leprous isles
’Tvvixt seaward sharks and landward crocodiles.
Let them all come! This little tumult o’er
That spreads its death along the Flanders plain
And sprinkles France and Italy with gore,
Each foreign warship we shall count a Stai n.
For this the war has taught us week by week:
The strong-armed never strike the rich and meek.
Their preference is still for husks and chaff;
They gather up what no one else requires.
Ho! Hear their twelve-inch guns begin to laugh,
Finding the ashes of their best desires.
But for a continent full-charged with mutton
The hungry nations do not care a button!
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.
THE WARNING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is danger lurking near us.
Kindly hold your breath and wait.
War with red-hot bolts may sear us;
Foes may knock upon the gate.
We have taken every measure
To defend our menaced shore;
And you needs must wait our pleasure
If you want to hear some more.
Sit down calmly and consider
How Australia really stands,
And of how we best can rid her
Of this danger on her hands;
Watch for lightnings of destruction,
Wings of war above the town,
Or a little trade obstruction
In a building falling down.
Watch and wait. There may be dangers
At your own suburban door.
Footsteps of the raiding strangers
May pollute your office floor.
You may be quite dead by morning
Or before the fall of dew;
But, advice from all men scorning,
We shall see the matter through.
We’re a democratic nation,
Therefore you have naught to say.
Keep your meek and humble station;
If you want to, kneel and pray.
We shall give our whole attention
To the matter now in hand ;
That is why we merely mention
Dangers rife by sea and land.
Dangers ! Watch the way we meet them !
We have handled things before ;
And we're sure that we can beat them
Once the guns begin to roar.
We shall do as well as ever;
You can judge what that may mean,
For our plans are just as clever
As tlie ones already seen.
N.S.W.

THE SECRET of the DIFFERENCE
between DR. MORSE’S INDIAN ROOT PILLS and other Remedies.
THERE seems to be a weakness in Human Nature to try everything new that comes along,
but in every case those making these trials find something lacking in the definite results. Still,
H uman Nature wants to try the new thing. 1 his is so well known that certain clever people
are continually bringing new remedies, or rather old ones, which have been tried and failed, with
new names, to catch these inquisitive folk. Most of these things have little or no merit, and are
tried once or twice and then discarded. When the public continues to take a remedy like
Dr. M orse’s Indian Root Pills year in and year out, there is a good reason why. They try the
other things but soon find something wanting, and they come back to the reliable remedy that
they can depend on. There is no use saying, “Don’t waste your money experimenting,” because
it will be waste of time. Try these things by all means. Try internal lubricants and fill your
system with mineral oil to do the work of the Bile, Nature’s own lubricant. Try every new
brand of salts on the market, every new tablet that has claims made for it that any kind of food
can be taken in any quantity if only this tablet is taken to digest it. Try them all, and soon it
will be found there is something lacking. There will be something that does not seem just right
in the after effect, and then come back to Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills and note the change.
There is a general feeling of satisfaction and buoyancy not experienced from these other so-called
remedies. This is what has occurred for years. After trying many experiments with things
that are heavily boomed, it is found that the satisfactory result is absent. The reason is very
simple. In Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills there is a proved combination of ingredi-
ents that form one compound that is highly efficient for the Liver and Allied Organs, not merely
flushing the system, as these other remedies do, without getting at the cause, but aiding in the
digestion of the food, and, above all, acting definitely on the Liver. This is the secret of the
difference and the reason why many of these new fads are tried a few times and found so ineffec-
tive that they are quickly abandoned, and Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills again resorted to and
found to accomplish everything that is claimed for them.

There were 37 starters in the Newcastle
Cup, a mile and three furlongs, run on Saturday.
This is only another way of saying that at least 25
could expect to have their chance settled in the first
hundred yards. The Club ought to alter the dis-
tance. As it stands now neither horses nor owners
get a fair deal. From all points of view it would
be better for everybody concerned, if the distance is
maintained, to have a draw at the scales and ballot
out everything over a score of runners. The subse-
quent proceedings might at least turn into something
that would be a test of the remaining horses. With
37 going over so short a journey the test is of nothing
unless it be the Law of Chance. On Saturday 30 of
the horses were helping to bury one another.
Silent Way, the winner, was brought over from
Maoriland last year with the ’chaser New York by
George Studholrne. When the owner died unex-
pectedly at Melbourne, Silent Way passed into the
ownership of blood-stock salesman Chisholm, of
Sydney, and had done nothing Since. A third in the
Winter Stakes at Randwick to Sobrina and First
Lesson was only a moderate performance. But the
stable had its eye on Newcastle, and the horse was
put through a preparation for the event. He went
well for a while, and was backed at long prices for a
fair wad, but latterly his track-work had been quite
ordinary, and neither Bracken nor trainer 11. J.
Robinson gave him much of a chance. In a recent
gallop Baltic Sea ran all over him. But he got
quickly off the mark on Saturday, and after a saloon
trip to tlie distance Bracken rode his ears off and
struck the mark' a head in front of the old-time-boom
Celano. Backers collected 20’s. But it was Bracken
who landed the bacon for them. lie rode a great
finish. Last year he was aboard Chantemerle, and
arrived third to Norbury.
If Celano had got there the books woidd have had
a tremendous haul. They did very well as it was,
but the other one would have given the volumes that
opened when the weights appeared almost a skinner.
Of all the runners Celano’s chance appeared about as
hopeless as any. He was given a few runs in Sydney
last year, and most punters wished he had died
before they heard of him. He is an imported horse
and a good-looker, owned by a Newcastle odds-mer-
chant. Camp Out, who scrambled home third, is
raced by Barney Allen, his breeder. Most of the
Randwick heads reckoned Bruyere to be a good thing,
with Mehi King" next best. The mare was bunkered
from the start. The King got himself chopped out
in the second furlong. Yet in a race in which it
was long odds against anything getting a run,
punters were backing these at 4’s and s’s. People
who do that sort of thing should be locked up.
* * » *
A young lady who has not been hurried
too much had a win at Newcastle last week. This
is Cragrose, a sister to First Principle, Assouan and
Petalster, winner of the Nursery. The filly is trained
at Randwick. She was tried a couple of times at
Flemington in March, and once got third money. But
there was no mistake about the matter on Saturday,
odds-on having to be given at the deaths The five
furlongs were run in “2-1.” Cragrose cost 750
guineas. She may prove to have been worth it.
* -X- * *
When the Sports’ Union saw that Barden
was going to ride Garibaldi in -the Maiden at Moore-
field (Sydney) on Saturday the members rushed into
Israel and took as much as there was to get. Appren-
tice riders were on most of the others, and it hap-
pened that Garibaldi looked something like a picture.
It was an easy win. The Italian patriot’s namesake
ought to be heard from some more. He is full-
brother to Prince Bardolpb. At Randwick (City
Tattf’s meeting) last month he ran unbacked in a
huge field of novices and got well flattened out.
* * * *
An imported gelding named Early Morn
gave a field of second-raters no hope in the Moore-
field Handicap on Saturday. It wasn’t a deed to
sing a song about, but was done so easily as to
suggest that Early Morn may arise and awake to
bigger deeds. Before coming to these parts he had
made a fair reputation in Westralia, where all the
champions are.
» * * *
Trainers of jumping horses get little
chance to practise deep deception on the handicapper
prior to the midwinter meetings in Melbourne.
Weights for the G.N. Hurdles are published only 31
weeks before the event, and anyone who tried to
keep a new hurdler dark by not running him in
public or jumping him on the track until the
Nationals were near at hand would probably regret
his tactics when the time came for the gee to fly
over all the timber that bars the way in a National
journey. There’s a lot of talk about “natural
jumpers, but the novice that jumps easily is still
wanting in /the muscular development necessary for
“a real good thing” in, a three-mile race. And as
to the policy of running byes with a nag that is
nearly fit —well, what are the stipes for ? Even if
the stipes' don’t put the stiffened animal out of the
question shortly .before the weights appear, the handi-
capper has eyes and ears to aid his estimate of the
newchum’s form. Great Surprise, by Cunning—
Quietude, hasn’t much chance to bring off a coup in
the G. N. Hurdle race,
• * * * •*
S.A. Jockey Club is making a trifling
change in its St. Leger conditions. The queer dis-
tance, 132 yards over 1} miles, was borrowed from
English precedent. The other States do not bother
about the' odd yards, so this year and hereafter S.
Aus. is going to drop them too.
There is a great reverence for kings on
the turf, anyway. The first day of the recent Adel-
aide meeting, for example,- was a re-union of royalties,
so to speak. In the first race Happy King got a
place. 'ihe second event was won by Encounter
King; Common King was nowhere. There were,
strange to say, no monarchs listed in the third item
on the programme, but the fourth made amends. This
was won by a daughter of Comedy King, while
Momba King was the runner-up and King Bardolph
was third, King of the Sea being also a starter. In
the fifth race we had Kingbier, who was third past
the post. An also-ran in the last race was William
the Silent. This title is understood to stand for
King Billy Hughes.
* ' * * *
Adelaide has practically put a stop to all
games, except bowls, during the war'; but the racing
—the little it has —flourishes exceedingly. There are
no ponies and no unregistered meetings in S. Aus.,
and the whole Adelaide district does not average
one race-meeting a fortnight. But the public appe-
tite tor what there is seems to be increasing. The
Adelaide Racing Club’s entries for the minor events
in the Birthday Cup meeting near at hand beat
the previous record by nearly 100. There are over
550 (including 20 for the one weight-for-age race).
For the Three chief events, Birthday Cup, Alderman
Cup and City Handicap, there are about 200 more.
John Hill, for years perhaps the foremost
trainer in S. Aus., announces his retirement, on
account of ill-health. His memory goes back to
Adam Lindsay Gordon and the time when the Adel-
aide Cup was run at Thebarton. He trained Auraria
when she won Cup, Oaks and C. B. Fisher (dead-heat)
in Melbourne. Newstead, The Assyrian, First Water,
Cowra and Totalisator were other good horses he
turned out. He has won the S.A.J.O. Derby live
times and the Adelaide Cup eight.
***•*•
Pentland Allen, killed in France, was a
crack amateur rider, son of the Edithburgh (S.A.)
owner, F. W. Allen. He trained a Grand National
Steeple winner once. Horses were his hobby, and if
there had been more scope for cavalry in this war he
would ha,ve been in a higher rank than lance-corporal
at the end.
» * # *
Eulahess, a winner among the ponies at
Ascot (Sydney) on Saturday, is bred on good stout
lines. She is by Bruce—Mulpera. Bruce is well-
remembered on the pony-courses, where he carried
all sorts of weights to victory. Mulpera was one of
the best of her day in the 14.2 class.
* * * *
The Commercial Travellers’ Assoc, of Vic.
has undertaken the job of rattling Aleconner, at a
shilling a time, on behalf of the Red Cross; and
with every traveller carrying books of tickets the
output should run into several tens of thousands.
The brilliant little gee, who ranks as an Epsom
Handicap and Williamstown Cup winner, is still
sound in wind and limb, and should be fully worth
the £5OO he is insured for.
* * * *
The Derby and Oaks meeting of the
National Coursing Association (N.S.W.), held last
week at Rooty Hill, was the best of the kind since
war began. The ground was very hard, and the
weather was rather hot for some of the dogs, and
these cracked up before the finals. But the trials
were all good. The Derby puppies, though not up to
the best standard, were an even lot. None showed
great, pace, and they were all a bit inclined to be
cunning. The bitch puppies in the Oaks were a very
good bunch—fast and keener workers than the dogs.
Wagering on both Stakes was brisk, and in both
Stakes the favorite went down on the second day.
For the Derby Glenwood was favorite. He went
moderately on his first run and won his second course
in good style, but was badly beaten in the third.
The Oaks favorite, Black Silk, won her first two
courses brilliantly, but was beaten in the second ties
by Hope Australia. The two dogs that beat the,
favorites ultimately came through winners of the two
blue-riband events. The Derby was won by Rowdy
Micky, a black dog whelped in October, 1916. The
Oaks went to Hope Australia, a black bitch puppy
whelped in September, 1916. The winner was un-
doubtedly the best lady in the stake. She ran her
last three courses in slashing style with a badly-
broken toe.
* * * #
Although only three coursing meetings
have been held in the Sydney metropolitan area this
year the imported dog White Hope has put up a
wonderful record as a sire of winners. At Liverpool
during the opening meeting Ducky Darling, Little
Yera and Hope Australia, all by White Hope, were
among the last four dogs left in the stake. Little
Vera (winner) and Ducky Darling (runner-up) were
the last pair left in. Bridal March, another White
Hope, won the Merrylanda Stakes at the same meet-
ing, and Hidden Hope, by White Hope, was runner-
up in the April Stakes. At the Derby and Oaks
meeting three White Hope puppies were among the
last four left in the Stakes, and White Wonder was
runner-up. In the Oaks two White Hope bitches,
Hope Australia and Hope’s Bride, were in the last
four, and they filled the two places in the final. The
Penrith Stakes at the same meeting went to Stepper’s
Bride, another White Hope puppy. Achieved, who
divided the Woodstock Gup with Sail On, also claims
White Hope as his sire. The hare with a White
Hope puppy astern has a busy day.
James W. Thompson, whose enthusiasm
breaks loose when smashing back-stroke swimming
records, put world’s figures on the book at Brisbane
the other day by swirling through 440yds. in 6min.
34sec. This knocks 22sec. off his own figures put up
in the first quarter of the recent mile-record spin
(29min. 4 l-ssee.) at Brisbane.
Fanny Durack, who, in an exhibition
swim at the Sydney Grammar carnival on March 18,
beat the world’s record 500 yards swim for women
by something like 20sec., is the first of the sex to
receive a prize from the school. The boys wildly
cheered their new mate when she received a certifi-
cate the other day setting forth the details of the
aforesaid whirl through the wet.
* * * *
L. A. Cody, who packs up his kit to go
coaching in India, is a much-travelled cricketer.
Years ago he played for Paddington in Sydney grade
cricket, and in 1913-4 had a hand in winning the
Sheffield Shield for N. S. Walesl Next year he
crossed the border, and was in the Victoria XI
which recovered the implement, and still keeps it,
owing to the war. This season Cod}' averaged 38.3
with Fitzroy (runners-up) in the Vic. pennant.
* * * *
“Pencil”: C. G. Wilson, of Wellington
(M.L.), who this season piled up 762 runs —the
local record aggregate for first-grade cricket —is
another of the old ’uns. Thirty years ago “Gillie”
lifted a bat in Victorian pennant games on behalf of
St. Hilda.
* * *
In Brisbane cricket this season the batting
averages of the Goodna team are strangely varie-
gated. In 13 innings Farquhar averaged 140.8 and
Burns 115.8. Both men were helped by seven not
outs, but they always went in among the first three.
The most brilliant bat of the team, Haddock, was
content with 74".7, but he was only twice not out.
The story ends here, as the rest of the averages
begin at 19.
* * * »
The Hun closed the hook of a fine athlete
when Lieut. Stuart Frank Spratt went out to
wounds in France on April 18, aged 23. He was
born at Burwood (Sydney) and educated at Fort-
street, and later on Sydney Y.M.C.A. reckoned him
its best all-round athlete. He landed at Gallipoli,
and was wounded soon after, but got fit again in
time for Lone Pine. In April, 1916, he won the
Anzac Cup and the obstacle race at the big swim-
ming carnival arranged for Billjim after the Evacua-
tion. Later, when training for a commission at
Worcester College (Eng.), he captained the football
15 and its cricket 11, and scored six wins and
a second out of seven events at the local regatta.
Afterwards he assisted the A.I.F. Headquarters Staff
football team to dish an English 15 by five points
to nil —and did all the scoring himself. Out of 197
points scored by Worcester College during the season
Spratt contributed 89. His own total was 101 points
for 13 matches.
S. ,T. Young, who won East Sydney all-
round championship for 1918, is a versatile athlete.
He tackled all kinds of events, and on the final day
took the 220 yards and the 121 b. shot putt and
arrived third in the three miles. Altogether Young
scored 32 points. T. R. Cole was next with 20J, the
others so far astern that a telescope was needed to
find them.
* * * *
“Rangan”: At a patriotic entertainment
up our way one of the attractions was a heavyweight
contest between a hefty Greek and the local black-
smith. The pubkeeper was referee, and he had a
quiet £5 on the Greek. The fight soon developed
into the question of who should land the first wallop.
The luck was with the blacksmith, and in the middle
of the second round Greece thought he had stopped
the anvil. The count went something like this:
‘‘One, two —get up, Mike— three —none of your kid-
ding, get up ! — four —-get up, will you now !—five—
don’t disgrace yourself, Michael—.six—get up or I’ll—
seven—for ’lvven’s sake, get up, Mike ! — eight —
there’s a quid if you’ll get up!— nine —are you
going to get up?” Only a gurgle came from Mik-
opoulos. “Well, out, and be damned to you 1” roared
the referee.
* * * #
There is nothing German except the name
about Alfred Von Doussa, who has just had a 70th
birthday at Mount Barker (S.A.). He was born in
Adelaide, and has been a hard-working citizen as
clerk of a District Council for 30 years and secre-
tary of the local Agricultural Society. He has been
a member of S.A. Leg. Council since 1901 ; but
probably he is most widely known as secretary and
chief promoter of the Easter Monday race-meeting
at Oak bank, which always attracts a crowd of inter-
state visitors. That post he has held since 1876.
* * * *
Jean Pierard, the Belgian wrestler, will
not be the winner of the big grappling tournament
now being run in New York, partly because he is
50 and weighs SJcwt. But it looks as' if Jean has
got away with the Matrimonial Stakes. He has been
married 23 times. The third effort lasted 13 years.
Then he discovered that there was a triangle on the
premises. Jean threw the interloper over 37 sky-
scrapers and started drinking and marrying heavily.
When last heard from he reckoned he had settled
down.
Two up-country S. Aus. farmers now
claim the record for confiding in an uncle from
Fiji. They met in Adelaide the usual friendly
stranger, who introduced them to a second, who
had a friend with a real certainty for the races.
The story runs exactly the usual course most of
the way. The special feature is that the two from
the country actually went home per motor, an'd in
cold blood obtained all they had, £770, to hand to
the sharpers.
“Morwang”; Once or twice lately when
handling freshwater catfish I have been spiked. The
pain is awful. Any fisherman know a speedy cure
for it ?
In the Manning River (N.S.W.) last week
an outsize in eels was hooked. It was 4ft. 2in. long,
14in. round the waist and weighed 181 b. when skinned
and cleaned.
“Suwarrow”: In fishing for Murray cod I
have always got more and better fish when the river
was slowly rising. With the water in that state the
fish feed close to the bank, but when the river is
falling they are usually caught well out in the
stream. For bait I give preference to small live
bream. The best cod I’ve lifted out was a sprat of
just on 501 b.; but I’ve seen one of 941 b.
* * * *
“Fifth Wheel”: The steam-driven car has
arrived in the U.S.A. It doesn’t look a bit like a
loco. —is just as neat and racy, in fact, as the car
that shoots itself along with, petrol. It employs
kerosene to boil the water, and as the steam is used
over and over again, the water tank only needs re-
filling every 2500 miles. Running costs are said to
be lower than those of the petrol car, and it can
creep like a tortoise or fly like a high-speed angel.
As for smell, it’s the best. None.
* * * *
“Paddy Star”: I went to a liuckjumping
contest at Queanbeyan, alongside Canberra, a few
days ago. Riders were very good and good, with one
exception—the winner of a second prize. This rider
was not shifted in the saddle, but his horse went as
the wind goes, “where it listeth.” Why? Because the
rider hung frantically to a “monkey strap.” I have
seen many buckjumping contests in my day. But
never before have I seen a competitor allowed to use
a “monkey strap,” much less win a prize by so
doing. The judges were elderly bushmen of the
Tumut and Monaro Plains who in their young days
would have laughed to scorn the professing rider
who sought adventitious aid. When I was a young-
ster horsebreaking on the Upper Murray we had
many spills from good performers, but it was
always a fair go. I can understand a horse-breaker
using a “monkey strap” to avoid strain, but a com-
petitor in a horsemanship contest —never!
“303’’: G. L. Hobson offers £lO to the
Red Cross (B. 25/4/’18) if “Pick” will produce 20
expert riflemen who aim “off” for elevation and
windage. I will double G. L. Hobson's stake and
will ask “Pick” to find only five William Tells.
He can’t do it. Hobson is a Boer War veteran who
got much musketry instruction in a hard school, for
the Boers were and still are the best all-round rifle-
shots in the world. Incidentally he lost a finger in
that kindergarten. He is a champion rifle-shot in
his district, and at game in the field is a "quick
hand with the tube without the sling. Therefore
he is not talking through his hat. But all this
desultory writing about the rifle doesn’t take us very
far. The fact is that everything is in -the melting-
pot. Old teaching methods are npw silly. For
instance, the landscape targets used at Randwick
school of musketry are too funny for words. Ask
any soldier from any front if he ever saw anything
like them in Flanders, France, Palestine or any-
where else. After the war, a “conference” —the
word makes me shiver —should be convened to decide
upon the best methods to obtain intense training in
musketry and machine-gunnery. At that conference
should appear officers (marksmen only) who have had
war-experience, experts from the rifle-clubs, men like
“Centurion,” a landscape artist in water-colors
or oils (two artists would be better), and a kanga-
roo shooter. Between each yearly sitting two
men—one a staff-officer (a musketry crack), and the
other a rifle-club expert—should go round the world
getting information. Only by some such course
can we keep up to date. “Pick’s” claim would have
been good 40 years ago, but not now. The round-
the-world delegates would do more good than
Hughes and Cook.
* * * *
“Pick”: “G.L.H.” (B. 25/4/TS) chal-
lenges me to name 20 Sydney riflemen who shoot
without aids or pass a tenner to the Red Cross funds.
Righto. In my remarks (B. 4/4/’IS) I didn’t con-
fine myself to Sydney ; there are many rifleshots out-
side the city, but I’ll give that bit in. The Wunder-
lich Club; Paterson, Laing and Bruce; Anthony
Hordern’s—these and every other club shooting in
the warehousemen’s quarterly competitions at Rand-
wick discard slings and wind-gauges, and therefore
necessarily get their windage, etc., by aiming off the
bull. Any of these clubs can muster more than 20
members on the range. Now let “GX.11.” forward
£lO to the Red Cross to prove himself a sport and
not a bluffer.
* * * *
“Alphonse”: An old-time driving record
was made by the late George Watson, who, while
with Robertson and Wagner, the coaching partners
of early Victorian days, drove from Wangaratta to
Melbourne, 165 miles, in a little, over 13 hours. The
journey was made over bush roads with a buggy
carrying three passengers. Of course the famous old
whip had frequent changes of horses.
* * * *
Killed in France, Thomas Roger Macnee,
who before the war pulled a. strong oar with the row-
ing club of his native suburb, Balmain (N.S.W.),
and was al3o on the committee of the Ma State’s
Rowing Association. In his spare time in those
days he was first a Customs official and later had
a job under the Inter-State Commission. Macnee had
climbed from private to captain in the A.I.F. in 2}
years, and was adjutant of Iris battalion when cox-
swain Death called “Easy all!”
* * * *
“Gil.”: Out on leave from -the big local
military hospital, I dropped in at a quiet little
saloon at Randwick the other day and looked for
someone to have a couple of hundreds up with. A
dapper old gentleman who was doing a little lone-
handed practice at a table said he would oblige.
After I gave a miss in baulk he played a fine
four-cushion cannon and went on with the break to
43. He won. During the game we discussed horse.
“Yes, Biplane is good,” he remarked ; “but I think
a horse I trained that won the Melbourne Cup in
1904 and got beaten by a head for the same race the
following year was his equal.” “What was his
name?” I inquired. “Lord Cardigan,” he answered.
I had been talking to John Mayo. He is 82 now, but
as active as a two-year-old. Until a year ago he
never had to wear glasses. By the way, he also
trained Lord Nolan, another Cup winner. His son
John is owner and trainer of the Fortune Hunter.

This co, confines itself mainly to the back-
country—to the north of Victoria, the rivers
and the far-west of N. S. Wales. The
branches opened in the past year pretty well
indicate its pioneering habits; they are at
CargeUigo, Matakana and Roto, which you’ll
find, if your map is good enough, out at the
farthest points of the new railways that creep
from Sydney towards Broken Hill, with the
great east-and-west transcontinental beyond
it. Matakana, for instance, is the present
terminus out from Condobolin —of no im-
portance whatever except that the railhead
halts for a while and Mt. Hope loads its
copper matte there. Now this kind of trade
is precarious; and that is all that is the
matter with quite a lot of what Permewan,
Wright and Co. have been doing for years,
including the river portion. Occasionally
the co. makes a bunch; but there is very
little chance of working up anything per-
manently big. However, the dividend, though
it bobs about a good deal, averages pretty
well; and, as the next table shows, after
paying it there has been a fair accumulation
of assets in the past 10 years:—
1908. 3916. 1917. 1918.
Liabilities. . . , £34,854 £41,855 £44,092 £51,681
Assets—■
Plant and property 37,297 46,284 44,104 39,757
Book debts . . 40,986 45,355 48,527 60,066
Merchandise ; . 10,356 » 20,496 21,371 23,144
Miscellaneous . . 3,045 3,378 3,269 4,759
Thus while liabilities are £17,000 larger
than they were 10 years ago the co. has
nearly £20,000 more on its books and £13,000
more in stocks, besides, say, five thousand
extra in plant and cash, etc. Of course, it is
very difficult to say what the plant is worth.
Wear and tear is very severe; but that
seems to be provided for. Last year, for
instance, besides paying £l5OO for repairs,
the co. wrote off over £3OOO of profit against
depreciation.

The co. seems to have turned the corner,
though it has a good way to go yet before it
reaches the most salubrious end of the
street. At the end of 1913, when the last
actuarial valuation was made, the co. had
its capital (£21.000) intact and a bit over
for bonuses. The latter were distributed
among the policy-holders, but the 10 p.c. of
the total which, in accordance with the con-
stitution, was to have been made available
for shareholders, was held back. They
have, however, had their regular 5 p.c. from
the profits of 'the Industrial branch. As
the very substantially improved business
since 1913 has been done at steadily reduced
cost, there is every reason to expect that
the investigation at the end of the current
year will disclose another surplus.
But expenses are still high, especially in
the Industrial section :
Expenses in Proportion to Income.
Ordinary. Industrial.
1913 .. 23.9 p.c. 60.5 p.c.
1916 .. 18.3 „ 49.3 „
1917 . . 16.3 „ 49.0 „
Considering that the Ordinary branch policies
don’t average more than about £l5O apiece,
the figures on that side are not at all bad;
but when half of the Industrial premium
go,es in chasing the policy and collecting the
fourpence a fortnight or whatever it is, leav-
ing rather less than half of the fourpence
for actual insurance, one can’t help feeling
that, though it is better than nothing, it
isn’t what it ought to be. Of the total
funds of £183,781 no less than £112,617 is
in municipal and Harbor Board debentures—
Maoriland local securities, no doubt, most of
them probably issued by Dunedin, where the
co. has a big hold on the available Industrial
business. For the rest, there is £22,911 out,
on mortgage ; and everything that has been
liquid of late has gone into M.L. and Com-
monwealth war loans. The whole lot brings
in a very satisfactory average of £4 12s. Bd.
p.c.
* * * *
Grains of gold are often found in the
sands of the Ninety-Mile Beach (Vic.), and
as traces of gold have also been discovered
along the coast about Waterhouse Island,
on the other side of Bass Straits, the
chances are that there is a submarine gold-
field somewhere out in the straits. But
there would be too much water in the stock
of any co, that tried to work it.

Do you live in the Lome
.A STUENT PIANO?
THERE it stands, in lonely gloom close by the wall,
an expensive tombstone on the grave of vanished
joy. You personally never could play very much,
and lack of practice has still further stiffened your
fingers. The children are only in the five-finger exercise
stage and other interests have impaired your wife’s
never-to-be-much-talked-about technique.
And so your“silent” Piano stands
deteriorating through lack of
use. Why not alter it all ? Why
not take advantage of the liberal
allowance we make on “silent”
Pianos and enjoy all the pleasure
which a Beale Player - Piano
affords. It is an instrument which
supplies faultless technique with a
rich, resonant tone. Eight ex-
pression devices give absolute
tone control, enabling one to get
the fullest wealth of colour and
feeling out of every selection
played. A knowledge of music is
quite unnecessary. All that is
required is an intelligent appre-
ciation of rhythm, accent and
expression. Informal concerts are
held daily.
Call and let us demonstrate how easy it is for you to
play all your favourite music the ‘Beale” Player-way.

IMPORTANT.—LOOK TO YOUR TEETH.
There are no teeth like those furnished by Nature. For service, comfort and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be compared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to preserve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap and water are useful, but somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttlefish, crabseyes. crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifically prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free 1/3. Free sample posted. ROCKE, TOMPSITT & CO., Melbourne.

Every day we are told to be economic;
To see stacks of food alas! my poor stomach!
It begins to make one feel very comic.
Where there’s plenty for every one it dosen’t seem
Jonic.
Why not give us something more definite,
Seeing that we are in the land of wheat and meat ?
Is it we must live on just perquisite,
To leave off cultivating to take a back seat?
Are we to go right back on our tracks?
If so we will want to know more of the facts;
To come down to the lowest, like our blacks,
Before we come down to think of the last act.

SONNET—COPY.
Verse have I known both lumbersome and hoppy,
Some that must leap and some that scarce can run,
Some that is made to love and some to shun,
And some that must be still described as sloppy.
There is a passion in the crimson poppy,
A glory in the rise and set of sun;
And out of these may music still be spun—
But, of your grace, sweet bards, write decent copy!
I hate the Kaiser with his evil works
And all his loud effrontery of lies.
I do not love the Austrians and their guile;
And in a large contempt I hold the Turks.
But more I hate the man who spoils my eyes
With tangled copy in handwriting vile.
N.S.W.
THE TASTER.

Our Relation with the Motorist
A great business like this isn’t so
difficult to carry on, when you re-
duce practise to first principles, as
we do.
We look upon the thousands of
Australian and New Zealand motor-
ists with whom we do business as
one gigantic buyer, and the many
thousands of tyres we market yearly
as one immense transaction.
We feel that if we disappoint this buyer in any
degree, either as to quality of product or in our
trade relations with him, our business will
suffer.
So we are careful in our own interests not to
disappoint him, to make everything we build
good enough, and worthy enough to invite and
deserve his further custom and good-will.
The Goodyear Tyre you buy is built to uphold
the good name of all Goodyear Tyres. The
nature of our trade relation with you is of the
same complexion as our relation with the
motoring public at large.
For it is of men like yourself that the motor-
ing public at large is composed—and it is to
the individuals in that public that every Good-
year Tyre must give account of its worth,
every Goodyear transaction bear evidence of
its sincerity,
Naturally we make each tyre and transaction
of a character to bear this responsibility.
Goodyear Tyres, Heavy Tourist Tubes and “Tyre Saver" Accessories are easy to get from Goodyear Service Station Dealers everywhere.

A WAR-TIME BEAUTY SECRET.
lIOW TO LOOK BEAUTIFUL ON LESS
MONEY.
In these days of economy we welcome any-
thing which enables us to cut down expenses,
and many lady readers will gladly accept my
advice and try this excellent and inexpensive
recipe for the complexion. Any chemist will
mix it up for you, and it is made up from
- oz - rose-water, 1 dram tincture of benzoin,
and 2oz. flowers of oxzoin. When well
shaken it should be applied with a sponge
and allowed to dry, then dust the face with
a soft cloth. Even if you have been using
expensive creams, powders or rouge, you
will be surprised how much nicer this lotion
will make your complexion and how delight-
fully smooth and soft your skin will become
under its influence. It will save you money,
too. because it takes the place, in your daiiy
toilet, of the best cream, powder or rouge
,vou can obtain, and the cost is compara-
tively small.—A dvt.

Curtain, after a long illness, for Aus-
tralian soubrette Hilda. Fraser (Mrs. God-
frey Castieau), who in her time played many
parts. Her best years were spent with the
Broughs, but she had plenty of work later on
in Andersonian and other melodramas. Mrs.
Castieau was a daughter of Scots-parson
Duncan Fraser, of Heidelberg. Her sister
Fanny, who has been a prop and stay td the
girl-boarders of the Presbyterian Ladies’
College these many years, lias spilt much ink
in the Mel bourne weeklies and elsewhere.
An aeroplane accident in England last
week put Albert Whelan and Bert Levy into
mourning. Each lost a son born in' Mel-
bourne. Albert Whelan, the brilliant mimic,
is one of the Waxman family and a brother
of Louis Waxman, the Melbourne solicitor
and bowls champion who died last year. Bert
Levy, the clever black-and-white artist and
showman, married a Miss Waxman. The
two boys in the aeroplane were first cousins.
THE MEETING.
“Charlie Chaplin has been drafted.”— U.S.A. cable.
BAIRNSFATHER : “Now, where have I seen
this blinkin' cove before?”
Dorothy Brunton, it seems, is returning to
Australia under re-engagement to the J. C.
Williamson firm. She went to America at a
very inauspicious time. Her festive girlish-
ness is pretty much of the American type;
so she was taking musical-comedy coals to
Newcastle when the war had affected busi-
ness and numbers of rival merchants were
battling for orders. Fortunately she hasn’t
lost much time over the trip.
“First Old Man”: There is no rule without
an exception. The visiting ingenue usually
travels with her mother. Thais Lawton, the
leading lady of the G.B.P. combination, has
created a new precedent by bringing dad.
“ J.F.H.”: Ellen Terry celebrated her 70tli
anniversary on February 27 ou the stage of
London Coliseum, where she is playing Por-
tia in the trial scene from “The Merchant of
Venice”—her best Shakspearean role.
Crowded house, much enthusiasm, flowers,
congratulations, presents and telegrams at
curtain-fall—including one from Bernhardt:
“a million salutations” to her ‘'dear sister in
art.” They wanted a speech; and after
shaking her head again and again she sobbed
a few words.

Norman Campbell, of 101 shows, lias left
the road. The Primary Industries Develop-
ment League (N.S.W.) has taken advantage
of his knowledge of Australia and induced
him to be its secretary.
Mrs. Victor Grayson, wife of the ex-
Socialist member for Colne Valley, has died
in M.L. On the stage she was known as
Ruth Norreys, and she played for a while in
Sydney before her husband, then in very bad
health, went to Maoriland. He enlisted a
few months ago.
“8.J.”: Australia will not see the Omar
Khayyam play in which Guy Bates Post
scored a success in the U.S. So far as this
country is concerned, Guy’s second string—
which he will pull should “The Masquer-
ader” fail to run for the full eight weeks of
his Melbourne and Sydney seasons—will be
“The Nigger,” manufactured by Edward
Sheldon, whose “Romance” was brilliantly
introduced to Australia by Madge Fabian.
Post created the name-part of “The Nigger”
in the country that occasionally fries ’em.
The story deals with the love of two men for
a woman, and the trouble that happens when
one fincis out that his rival has tar in his
blood.
“J.F.H.” (London) : Pinero’s latest, “The
Freaks,” produced by Dot Boucicault at the
New, tends to confirm the suspicion that the.
old man’s stock of stage ideas is almost ex-
hausted. This “idyll of suburbia” is a far-
fetched, unconvincing, discursive, incoherent
story of a company of circus freaksv spend-
ing a week in the society of a typical subur-
ban household in compliance with a provision
in an eccentric will. Their presence leads to
amatory and other complications; also the
giant gets sick and the local vicar holds a
prayer-meeing for his recovery, which duly
transpires. Here and there are patches of
smart . Pinerotie dialogue, but the general
effect is unequal, incongruous and mystify-
ing. Pinero suggests that he meant it to be
a ‘sermon-comedy,” enforcing the moral that
the people who stare at freaks are more
freakish themselves; but he does not develop
this promising idea or make it dramatically
effective.
“The Bing Girls,” a first attempt to trade
on the popularity of this family name was a
comparative failure, but “The Bing Boys on
Broadway,” produced at London Alhambra
lately, is bringing in the shekels. Counter-
parts of Emma and .the two Bings (one of
them American) are introduced, and some-
thing of a plot, with crooks in it. gives the
revue some coherence.
Cecil Brooking, who supplied such a
natural study of the fatuous Alaric in “Peg
o’ My Heart,” is out of grease-paint' and
into war-paint. He has passed the medical
officer and gone to Broadmeadows.
“F.N.T.”: “Stargazer” says something
when he refers (B. 18/4/T8) to the un-
stinted applause, arranged by the manage-
ment for every first-night show, good or bad.
It has always caused .me hearty amusement,
during a long experience of opening perform-
ances, to observe the ushers loudly clapping
their hands in dark corners whenever the
time seemed ripe; and further amusement,
sitting three rows of stalls away from the
orchestra, to notice the kettle-drummer keep-
ing the enthusiasm going, steady and strong,
whenever there seemed enough gamp-banging
and footwork to make his efforts not too
obvious. But there—l’m easily amused.
She comes to light again in “The Rain-
bow”—the American Iceberg Heroine: that
compound of virtue and frigidity which
Yankee writers and artists of the Dana Gib-
son type used to be so fond of representing
as an object for hopeless adoration by male
worms. Ruth Sumner left her husband 10
years before the curtain went up because,
in circumstances over which he had very
little control, he broke a promise not to play
cards with her rotter of a brother. After
10 years their 17-year-old daughter visits
pop and loves him for his grey hairs and
winning ways, but the play’s heroine drags
the child from him because he is a vicious
person who plays auction bridge and goes to
races. Advances by dad towards a reconcilia-
tion are of no avail until the last act, when
the chilly lady graciously consents to live
with him again, probably in the hope that she
will give him fatal chilblains or incurable
chapped lips. This sort of play does more
than anything to delude pap-brained girls
into thinking tlpat man’s love can best be
won by treating him with studious contempt
and will thrive upon a diet of iced insults.
Roydon Purves, manager of the Tivoli at
Brisbane, was married a. few days ago to
Lorna Paterson. She was w r ith the original
Follies. The young man, who is a son of
Maud Brandon, a well-known Shakspearean
actress of 20 years ago, has repeatedly been
turned down by the recruiters.
From London :
Marie Lohr lias hit the bull’s-eye with her first
shot. “Love in a Cottage” has settled down for
a long run, and “Daddy’s” girl will be under no
necessity to requisition any of the other plays she
has up her sleeve until the approach of next
Christmas.

to feel like before I go on. You would be

A couple of imported damsels were strug-
gling through their first songandance the
other day. The man who had imported them
tried hard to make out the words of the song
they were gasping and squeaking in a
strange jargon. At last he gaVe it up.
“Here! Somebody take a dictionary round
to those women !,”
“Kopai” : I’ve been to hear Charles Wald-
ron, and it's made me home-sick. I was born
near Birmingham (Eng.), where the Senti-
mental Blokes of the Black Country—we
call them “peakyblinders” there, from the
pent-house style of jockey-cap they affect-
squeak a quaint r-less argot found nowhere
else even among England’s countless dialects.
But Waldron has it. He says "way-uld” for
world, “gay-ul” for giifJ. and, as he’s
making love to Katlilene Macdonell most of
the time to the effect that she’s the nicest
little gay-ul in the way-uld. he says it pretty
often. Now how on earth does an American
man of culture come to talk pure Stafford-
shire—and why does he keep on doing it?
“Fax” : To my way of thinking, there h
no greater incongruity in the performance of
“The Willow Tree,” which is a tissue of in-
congruities, than the association of Miss
Macdonell’s harsh masculine voice with a
gentle, spiritual Japanese character. If she
only talked in her sweet singing tones Miss
Mac’s performance would be more persua-
sive. though the assumed innocence of the
Willow Tree Girl could never give a tinge of
poetic color to the conventional indelicacy of
her reference to eggs and roosters, or her
suggestion of a meal of dog’s flesh when she
objects to boiled fish.
“Ancient Prompter”: The author of
“Within the Law” has scored another with
his “13th Chair.” After a long run in
America, the creepy melodrama is filling the
Duke of York’s, London, with Mrs. Pat
Campbell in the lead, and lately it wafe
staged in Paris for the only Rejane to star
in. It comes to Australia soon, with the
playwright’s better-half as leading-lady.
Jack Seymour Mann, one of the best
scenic artists ever sent to Australia by
America or any other country, was prompted
by the war to go into the making of pig-
ments, and has now a kalsomine factory in
Sydney. Anti-corrosive paint was mostly a
German production, but Mann has started on
this now in a commercial way, despite diffi-
culties of finding the raw material in dis-
tricts whence transport does not kill profit.
AS IT SOMETIMES SEEMS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The moving-picture industry is suffering from the
all-round increase in the cost" of materials in U.S.A.
The film producer tore his hair.
He saw the cost of movies rise
And profits fall; so in despair
Decided to economise.
He paid the star “a thousand per”
(Per week not year), but who was he
To even dare suggest to her
That times required economy!
The cash the “vamp.” was wont to pay
For flimsy microscopic dress
tie could not cut, lest she display
An unembargoed nakedness.
Five hundred pounds had gone to meet
The cost of dinners and the like;
But banquets he could not delete
For fear the supers went on strike.
The needless things he vainly sought,
Till through his brain a notion shot.
“Eureka 1 ’Tis a brilliant thought!
We’ll do without a yoldarn plot!”
N.S..V.
BOB DEXTER.
A PROVINCIAL SUCCESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Mr. Solly Seekum’s Stellar Dramatic Co. has just
returned from a most successful season in the pro-
vinces, and is now resting, preparatory to starting
' ’ on a tour of the northern centres.”
The gifted Dana Gibbs came back
From distant Teddywaddy.
His face was cut, his eye was black,
He’d bruises on the body.
He’d been with Solly Seekum’s Co.
In homely drama acting.
“I could not stay,” said he. “You know,
The pace was too exacting.
“We played six shows a week, and that
Wretch Seekum is so stilted.”
We asked him why his hose was flat,
And why his ear had wilted.
“Ah that ?” he asked. “The work I do
No rural favor gat me—
A varlet in the audience threw
A ripe banana at me.”
*‘A ripe banana ? And you’ve got
Some damage to your traction,
An eye that’s just an azure blot,
And one arm out of action! ”
“My head likewise is very sore;
I’ve unseen wounds,” sighed Dana.
“With frenzy when the act was o’er
We fought for that banana!”

London's Beauty Writers,
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
By Olga Amsdell, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
The Killing of Superfluous Hair.
It is easy to remove superfluous hair tem-
porarily, but to remove it permanently is
quite another matter. Not many women
know that for this purpose such a simple
substance as powdered pheminol may be
used, applied directly to the hair. The recom-
mended treatment is designed not merely to
instantly remove the lmir. but also to event-
ually kill the roots entirely. Almost any
chemist could supply an ounce of pheminol,
which quantity should be sufficient.
Renewing- Complexions by Absorption.
If your complexion is marred with sallow-
ness, moth patches, pimples or freckles, it is
useless to potter with powders and paints,
lotions, creams and things in an effort to get
rid of the trouble. Unless you have some
ability as an artist, you will mar your ap-
pearance still more. The new and rational
way is to take off the complexion itself, with
all its offensive marks. Just get some pure
mercolized wax at the chemist’s and use at
night the same as cold cream. Remove next
morning with water and soap, following with
dash of cold water. The mercolized wax
absorbs the half-dead scarf skin in flaky
particles, so gradually that no one guesses
you are treating your face—unless it be by
the result, which is truly wonderful. There
is nothing like it for bestowing a natural,
healthy and beautiful complexion.
A “Blackhead” Secret.
Blackheads, oily skins and enlarged pores
usually go together, but can be instantly
corrected by a unique new process. A tablet
of . stymol, obtained from the chemist, is
dropped in a tumbler of hot. water, which will
then of course “fizz” briskly. When the
effervescence has subsided the face is bathed
with the stymol-charged water and then dried
with a towel. The offending blackheads, of
their own accord, come right off on the
towel, the large oily pores immediately con-
tract and efface themselves naturally. There
is no squeezing, forcing, or any drastic
action. The skin is left uninjured, smooth,
soft and cool. A few such treatments should
be taken at intervals of three or four days'
thereafter in order to insure the permanence
of the pleasing result so quickly obtained.
Don’t Have Grey Hair.
Grey hair is often a serious handicap to
both men and women while still in the prime
of life. Hair dyes are not advisable because
they are always obvious, inconvenient and
often down-right injurious. Few people know
that a very simple formula, which is easily
made up at home, will turn the hair back to
a natural color in a perfectly harmless man-
ner. You have only to get two ounces of
tammalite concentrate from your chemist and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum to prove
(his. Apply tills, simple and harmless lotion
for a few nights to the hair with a small
sponge, and the greyness will gradually dis-
appear. The lotion is neither sticky nor
greasy and has been proved over and over
again for generations past by those in pos-
session of the formula.
About Shampoos.
To quote an eminent London beauty
specialist: “The slimy egg cannot dissolve
the scalp impurities, but only adds to the
trouble by completely choking the pores with
animal matter.” The substance sticks, de-
composes, thereby causing tjie hair to assume
a dead, “dull color.” Avoid eggs (and soap
too) on your hair. Try the delightful pre-
paration made by stirring a teaspoonful of
stallax in a cup of hot water. It will bring
out new beauty possibilities in vour hair,
will clear and prevent dandruff, and leave
(hat fine fluffy effect so much sought after.
It also removes excess oil or greasiness. [1

Tweed Heads (N.S.W.) gave Three
Cheers for Australia when war began. Each
had a bayonet at the end of it. Their mother,
Mrs. Isabella Cheers, died last month.
J. M. Barrie, after having made himself
a millionaire by many years of successful
play-writing, is reverting to his literary
love, and will shortly publish another novel.
His earliest story brought him three guineas.
It, is expected that he will make at least
five out of his latest.
As an f.a.q. sample of the tired Australian
call Richard Victor Shields. B.A. The youth
was an Arts student at Sydney Uni. when
Kaiser Bill got busy. Throwing away his books
he fell into khaki, went to the Front, was
wounded, won the Military Cross, returned
to Sydney, tackled his books again, and
scored his B.A. with a tiptop pass. He
hobbled along on crutches to receive his
ticket.
“Marigold”: Looking back, it seems to me
that the Hun is one of the world's champion
legpullers. When the German training-ship
Ilertzogen Sophia Charlotte was in Sydney
some 12 or 14 years ago, we entertained some
of the officers at our home, and. later, were
invited on board for afternoon-tea. After
being supplied with samples of German cook-
ery, singing, dancing, etc., we asked the
officers what they thought of Sydney. “Veil,
your city is very nice, and your harbor pcau-
tiful. verypeautiful.” “And what of our
people?” “Your ladies, like your harbor, are
peautiful; but your men—ach! They are
rude! They have no manners!” And we
took that and more as meekly as we had
taken-their afternoon-tea ! Well, I’Ll he ready
for the next smirking butcher who libels my
countrymen.
From the Great Fog
Glasgow was selected by Zangwill for the first
performance of his new play, “Too Much Money"
—our Lillah McCarthy in the lead. An aesthetic
young person is married to a wealthy, prosaic
man with little or no knowledge, understanding or
sympathy with her outlook. She is constantly be-
moaning her fate in being tied to such an impossible
husband. To cure her he pretends to go bankrupt,
and they are suddenly transformed from luxury to
lodgings in a garret. Of course it works in accord-
ance with his desires: the lady’s tastes are revolu-
tionised, and she becomes a new woman under the
“cleansing fires of poverty.” The play had an
excellent reception and its early transference to
London is assured. By the way, the population of
Glasgow has now reached 1,105,000, recent gains
having been extraordinarily rapid. The Millions
Club that was so ambitious to make Sydney the
acknowledged “Second City of the Empire” would
have had a pretty stiff contract.
“F.N.T.”: Tbe registration of ice-cream
makers under the Vic. Health Act is all
very well, but Bumble doesn’t help as he
should in protecting the kids from filth
diseases. At Hawthorn, for “failing to
keep their premises in a proper condition,”
a pair of ice-cream merchants named
Williams (Alfred and William) were re-
cently fined a tenner between them, with a
couple of sovs. for allied misdemeanors. The
Health inspector said that “in the ice-cream
room he noticed a very objectionable odor.
Underneath a dilapidated box was a bag
blocking a drain.” A tenner for a murderous
offence of this kind ! One child’.a funeral on a
moderate scale costs more than that.
“De Oriel”: The brewer’s poster, well
known in the South, depicting a bearded and
ruddy old man delightedly scoffing a pint,
is a reproduction of old Sam Nott, who
passed in his check at Wood’s Point (Vic.)
a while ago. Sam had been quite thriftless,
so his neighbors agreed to give him his
send-off free of charge. Time being limited
the burial party commandeered all kinds of
boxes and built a shell for the departed.
It weighed about scwt. Rain fell pitilessly
as six men bore old Sam aloft, while curi-
ous residents read the trade signs on the
coffin. The parson got shipwrecked on the
road, and when the honorary undertakers
started to lower the box into the grave,
which was half-filled with water, one rope
slipped, and down went Sam with a mighty
splash, almost drowning his audience.
“8.D.”: Now that prices are jumping
shopkeepers are making a good part of
their living by buying each other’s goods.
Whenever the Price Fixing Board puts up a
new record or someone in the trade boldly
shoves on another 25 or 50 p c., the fellows
who happen to hear of it. send round and
buy all they can get at the old prices. Some
of the little suburban tobacconists didn’t
hear of the recent push for a day or so and
they did a roaring trade —until they tumbled
to it. A grocer and mixed-goods dealer had
a line of slates which used to give him a
fair profit at 4d. each. Noticing that- the
demand was livening up he raised the price
to sd. on general principles, and has been
kicking himself ever since he found out that
most of the buying was done by emissaries
of a big rival who is now selling at 9d. One
result of all this is that price-tickets are
getting unpopular; they give too much in-
formation to the enemy.
Another Sydney war-baby, the No-
Imperial Federation League, has died, or,
rather, has permitted itself to be swallowed
by a newly-formed Advance Australia
League. The anti-imperial hoof was raised
by W. J. Miles in 1916, and Miles, who up
to that time had never taken an active part
in politics, has been the life and brain of the
movement ever since. The first and main
plank in the Advance Australia League is
to resist any reduction in Australian
autonomy, and the vehemence with wliich
this has been maintained has given the im-
pression that the League is really a Cut-the-
Painter Society, though Miles is emphati-
cally against that. The new league is com-
posed mainly of Labor supporters, pacifists
and Socialists, though Miles, who is secre-
tary, treasurer and general controller, is
none of these things. The president is
George. Waite, a well-known Socialist, who
when in normal health does hard manual
work all day and spends his evenings and
Sundays lecturing and organising for the
international brotherhood which he sees loom-
ing in the distance. When Miles is not
planning to defeat the aims of the Imperial-
ists he is working on freethought propa-
ganda. He is all accountant by profession,
but well-gilded enough to make a struggle
for his daily crust unnecessary.

Hupmobile Breaks Fremantle to Sydney Record by

FROM PORT AUGUSTA MR. BURTON LEFT THE BEATEN TRACK, via Adelaide and Melbourne, and
struck out across the desert and WASHOUTS FOR BROKEN HILL." The nature of this country can be
imagined when it is shown above that it took 38 hours to do 404 miles on an average of only 10.6 miles per hour.
It is worthy of note that the only spares carried by the motorists were a Crown A heel, a Stub Axle and two Main Spring Leaves, none of
which were needed. They depended entirely on their Electric Installation for Lights.
The whole performance speaks volumes for Hupmobile Reliability and Service .

DON'T JVORRY,
Anxiety and worry have most harmful
effects upon the nervous system. Overwork
also causes a man to become nervous and
inclined to worry. This feeble state of the
nervous system often ends in what the doctors
jail neurasthenia—which is nervous ex-
haustion. If you cannot devote your mind
fully to business or work; if you are not
sure of yourself; feel excitable and depressed ;
take heed. You will find sleep does not rest
you, your memory will play tricks, your limbs
ache and tremble after any walk or exer-
tion, and there will be a dull pain in your
back.
You must take prompt steps to stop this
progressive mischief that may develop into
neurasthenia; you must nourish your nerves
with the one food they need—rich new blood.
Nothing so surely renews the supply of
strong red blood as Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills, and this is the reason they are so valu-
able in nervous diseases of both sexes. Even
children benefit from the use of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills. It is often said that Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills for Pale People are as
good as a holiday both for workers and the
wealthy.

Returned A.I.F. men, even those without
monarchical sympathies, speak approvingly
of young Edward of Wales. He is reckoned
an efficient officer and takes after grandpa
Edward rather than the royal George, his
father. Despite maternal efforts to make
him a mollycoddle, the youth' is “one of the
knuts” and, like dead Edward, something of
a connoisseur of feminine loveliness.
“Inquirer”: I wish somebody would coin
a new trade phrase to take the place of the
sick-room attendant’s “doing as well as can
be expected.” When I get that drawled back
at me instead of the real information I am
after, I want to shout: “Well, how well
should he be expected to be?”
The steward of King George’s farms at
Windsor advertised recently for a married
couple “with no children” to serve as cattle-
man and cook. The same objection doesn’t
apply to bulls and cows, or pigs and poultry,
in this hour of England’s need.
A little one from France: —
One of the Chinese laborers reported himself sick.
I told him to take a rest, and afterwards asked Pills
to have a look at the man. Cause of illness: a
baby! We then discovered that no less than seven
of our gang of reputed men were women. They
all look alike to us and but for the arrival of that
little bit of China, we would never have been the
wiser.
“Ivan Pentland”: Re “T.F.M.’s” state-
ment (B. 18/4/T8) that “the chief of the
Macdonells of Glengarry,” who landed at
Port Albert (Vic.) in 1842, was “the son
and heir of the man who figures as Fergus
Maclvor in Scott’s ‘Waverley.’ ” In the book
Fergus is a lieutenant of the “Young Pre-
tender,” and is captured by the English and
hanged at Carlisle in 1745. The novelist
makes him die a bachelor; and even if that
is a departure from historic fact for the pur-
poses of the yarn, his “son and heir” would
have been a bit too long in the tooth to be
leading a cow-cockying enterprise in Aus-
tralia 97 years after Fergus’s decease.
Laggard Australia is putting a cruel strain
on its young veterans. A Sydney boy who
has proved his courage writes home to his
mother:—
I’m a grown man now, but when the time came
for me to get back to the front line, I leant my head
against the wall and cried like a kid.
That’s what modern war reduces the bravest
to if they are not spelled sufficiently. What
is Australian Labor going to do about it?
A singularly aptly-named person is Mrs.
Rosina Flutter, of Chertsey (Eng.). Mrs.
Flutter charged a young munition-worker
with assaulting her, but admitted the soft
impeachment that she was infatuated with
her Joseph. He had asked her to discon-
tinue her attentions, and had even got his
lawyer to write her on the subject. Then her
advances had provoked him to assault her
unkindly. Her age is 45, and she is mother
of 25 children, including five pairs of twins.
The lady’s remaining years of usefulness
ought not to be wasted. No 45-year-old
mother of 25 children would be allowed to
go begging for endearments in Germany.
Major Gerald Ashton, who has been
appointed Assistant-Director of Resources at
Bagdad, is a son of The Bulletin’s old
friend G. Rossi Ashtbn, who once looked
after the paper’s Melbourne cartoons. Major
Ashton has been in the Indian Army for
three years, is the youngest man of his rank
in the corps, and has been recommended for
the Military Cross. Another son of the
artist’s is also serving. Their mother was
actor George Coppin’s daughter Blanche.
Morris Crawcour, a relative of that well-
remembered Crawcour who died standing up
to a burglar at Williamstown years ago,
came back from France in 1916, all shot up
—a cot case. He spent a long time in hospi-
tal after he reached Melbourne, and was
by-and-bye discharged as unfit for further
mix-ups. But the other week he decided that
the Time was once more Ripe, and so joined
uo again with the A.I.F. Two fighting bro-
thers, by the way, have each parted with a
leg on active service.
“Peter”: “Cherokee’s” reference to Deem-
ing and the dismembered animal details (B.
18/4/T8) reminds me that Deeming’s land-
lord in Windsor (Vic.) was a local butcher
whose soul waxed fat on the prestige that
had been brought on his dwelling. After
the discovery of Mrs. Deeming the meat-
merchant had the idea of bestowing “relics”
on favored friends, and offered my respected
parent a packet of the original cement con-
taining a bit of the victim’s hair. My rela-
tive’s disgusted rejection of the parcel was
regarded as unnatural sensitiveness by
numerous neighbors.
The Eleanor Lucas whose name four-
figured in Vic. War Loan lists is probably
the humblest and least-known lender of thou-
sands to the Commonwealth. Put in her
rightful place as the head of E. Lucas and
Co., of Ballarat, and identification is easy
in rag circles. Not 20 years ago this twice-
widowed little Yorkshire dame couldn’t raise
much more than her voice. To-day,
approaching the allotted span of life, she sits
back in her picturesque Ilealesville humpy
and practises charity quietly, while her only
son, Edward Hargreaves Price, looks after
the business. In days less halcyon for the
Lucases mother and daughter arduously foot-
worked a few sewing-machines in their cot-
tage home in Ballarat East. To-day the firm
commands 500 electrically-pushed machines.
The industry flourishes on the spot where
the once great Phocenix Foundry flourished.
“D.D.”: “Felice” suggests (B. 25/4/T8)
that Matilda Maud Hennessey, C.P.S. at
Esk (Q.), is the first woman anywhere to
“unite a brace of adventurers in the toil of
matrimony.” She is not. The position of
Registrar at Paddington (N.S.W.) was
held for some years by a woman. In the
early days of this century she married two
couples I knew —the brides were sisters; and
doubtless there were others. There was a
humorous side to these two marriages. The
brides’ male parent —a Quaker, or Plymouth
Shaker, or something—objected to a parson,
and also held strong views about woman’s
place in the universe. When it was ascer-
tained that the Registrar was a woman
there were heartburnings; but as marriage
by the Registrar must be in the district
where the bride resides, there was no help
for it.

Care and I.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
CLOSEST friends, these twelve years
gone,
Care and I have always been;
Hand in hand we sit alone,
Silent both and staid of mien.
Many nights she shares my bed;
In her arms I, waking, lie;
We are as a couple wed,
Care and I,
Care and I.
Gentle mistress of my home,
Startled if a bell she hears,
Care is “out” when callers come;
Like a ghost she disappears.
When they’re gone I wait awhile
Ere my friend returns, perchance
With a shadow of a smile
In her glance,
In her glance.
Ours remains a secret bond.
If discovered with my mate
Never seem I kind or fond—
Lightly I dissimulate.
Scandal does us twain no ill;
Lovers only on the sly
We are unsuspected still —-
Care and I,
Care and I.
Pleasure, in a summer gown,
Light of heart and ever gay,
Loves to lure me out of town,
Take me for a holiday.
Sweet are those occasions rare;
Recreative for the mind ;
Mistress Care is half-forgot,
Left behind,
Left behind.
When the holiday is o’er,
Being once again restored
To my mistress as before,
Partner of her bed and board,
I am quietly content;
Life again a long-drawn sigh,
We are one in sentiment,
Care and I,
Care and I.
Not in black is she arrayed:
Pearly grey becomes her best.
Tastefully her robes are made—
Care is ever neatly dressed.
Very little change I see
In her face, despite the years;
Young as when she came to me
Care appears, . '
Care appears.
Constant Care, my faithful mate,
Teaches me what patience means ;
Whilst the others rail at fate,
Others envy kings and queens ;
Hand in hand we sit alone.
Breast to breast we oft-times lie,
Like a Darby and,a Joan —
Care and I,
Care and I.
VICTOBIA.
EDMUND FISHER.
Nerves.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AS Lawson turned his back to walk
away, Jimmy Lachlan levelled the
tomahawk and drew his arm
steadily back. It would not do to miss.
Boss Lawson had a nasty temper. More-
over, he objected to being killed. As
Jimmy’s arm flexed at its limit it hesitated
for an instant. In that instant Lawson
turned. Straight at Jimmy he walked and
knocked the chopper from his hand now
hanging limply at his side. His left hand
he placed at the back of Jimmy’s head
while he gripped the chin with his right.
The next moment Jimmy was wriggling
grotesquely on the ground with a broken
neck, much as a chicken wriggles after its
neck has been screwed. No, it did not do to
miss with Boss Lawson.
When Jimmy ceased his antics and lay
still, Lawson had gone. There were low
mutterings as the tribe gathered round, for
was not Jimmy Combo’s son? The only
remaining son of King Combo. And it was
not good to call down the wrath of the
King. Some night, no doubt, Lawson would
die with great suddenness. But not yet.
At present he was Boss Lawson, white
blac-kman. More loved, and hated, and
feared than King Combo himself. Loved
secretly by the gins of the tribe ; hated and
feared right down to the mongrel dogs in-
festing the camp.
The wrath of the tribe to Lawson was as
the wrath of the mouse to the lion. Always
did the thunder of his roar out-thunder the
roar of the tribe, and always did he con-
trive to prevent any black getting behind
him. The rest was simple. Lawson of all
men knew that the only dangerous black is
the one who walks behind.
When, after satisfying themselves that
Boss Lawson slept, the bucks held a secret
council in the scrub at the rear of the camp
long after midnight, Lawson watched from
the shelter of an ironbark. The log rolled in
his blankets would, he knew, appease the
curiosity of any wandering nigger.
After listening to the decision of the
council, that the gin Harriette should be
detailed to kill him while he slent —for
always was the white man foolisli and
would not strike a woman—he returned to
his bed to sleep out his contempt for the
decree.
* * * *
The first faint flush of grey in the east
told that it was morning. Calmly scrutinis-
ing the objects slowly taking shape ai’ound.
Lawson became aware of a figure creeping
silently towards him. Sometimes it ad-
vanced only a yard a minute, sometimes
only a foot: but always it moved in his
direction. Whether human or beast, male
or female, the light refused to reveal; but
a something at its side flashing brightly, a
single glint of the dawn told him that the
thing was human, that its name was
Harriette and that it carried a tomahawk
low along the ground as it crept.
Watching carefully, he became aware of
other figures following in the wake of the
first, some twenty feet in the rear. They
came on in a semi-circle, and in the hand
of each was a spear. A look of pleasurable
expectancy was on the faces of all.
It would never do. They had tried it
before, though never quite like this.
Familiarity was breeding contempt. Jump-
ing quickly fi'om beneath his blanket he
bore down upon them, rolling back his
sleeves as he went. Instead of scattering
into the scrub as formerly the attackers
stood their ground. It was unaccountable.
When thirty feet away he halted and glared
at the faces into which he had so often
brought a look of almost canine fear. Still
they stood their ground.
“You, Sargent! What?” he cried, glaring
fiercely into the face of the black in the
centre of the semi-circle.
The wolf-like grin on Sargent’s face
spread, and a spear whistled over Lawson’s
head.
In an instant he saw that the game was
up. The rabble which he had so long
scourged and taunted had turned. He might
come through—if the life of indolent gorg-
ing had not stiffened his joints. If it
had !
Knowing that every second counted, he
raced for the scrub on the far side of the
camp, stirring into wakefulness a pack of
yelping mongrels as he ran.
Turning his head he observed the woman
Harriette still in the lead, but at the end of
twenty minutes she had dropped out of the
hunt. For this was no race for weaklings.
Gaining his second wind, Lawson noted
with delight that idleness had not knotted
his sinews, and as the first state of panic
passed, leaving his brain clear, he set into
a steady lope which threw the miles behind
into the faces of his pursuers.
Throughout the morning he maintained
his lead, until the sun passing overhead
brought the first faint pangs of thirst. As
the first dryness tickled his throat he cast
the thought of drink from him and increased
his pace. But after a quarter fit an hour
the pangs returned, and this time he could
not cast the thought out. Still hurrying, he
tried to spit. His throat was not dry ; lie'
could spit with as much ease as when chew-
ing yams in front of King Combo’s gunyah.
He was sure of it.
The only response was a dry. sticky bead
of white saliva which stuck to his lips and
dropped heavily on to his sleeve.
With the knowledge that his spittle was
dry came the realisation that; soon he would
be unable to spit at all. Then his throat
would crackle. His tongue would swell
and fill up his mouth, clinging to the roof
until he would be unable to breathe. Thus
his pace would slacken and the pursuers,
now running grimly two hundred yards in
the rear, would draw nearer, and gaining
in distance, would gain heart also.
With the thought panic again seized him
and he doubled hig pace, leaping over logs
and boulders as he ran.
But this would not do. At first he had
gone round these. This continual jumping
was sapping his vitality more than yards
of straight running. And why should he
get flurried? Even if his throat was dry
and parched, he could still breathe through
his nose. Closing his mouth he sucked the
breath through his nostrils and found it
good. He was independent of his mouth
and could continue indefinitely now. Look-
ing back over his shoulder he hurled a shout
of derision at his pursuers and again
steadied into an even lope.
After another mile the tickle in his throat
returned, causing him to cough dryly. But
he; could still breathe freely and his pace
was unimpaired. Topping a rise, a line of
she-oaks stretched away before him in the
hollow below, and he knew he was approach-
ing Condamine Creek. He would find water
there. It would mostly be stagnant pools
at this time of year, and it might have a
coating of green slime which he would have
to brush aside with his hand ; but it would
be wet and would take the dryness from
his throat. He would lose a priceless half
minute in getting it, but the accelerated
speed which it would give would more than
compensate. He might even contrive to
carry some of it in his hat. If not, he
could follow the bank and slake his thirst
at will.
Racing down the hillside he rushed
blindly into the creek- —and stood still.
There was no water. The sand in the bed
was crisp and dry, and the pebbles were
white and shining. They had not felt the
soft caress of water for more than a month.
A shout from the pursuers on the hillside
brought him to his senses with a start,
sending him stumbling wildly along the
watercourse. For the best part of an hour
he followed it without sight of the slightest
moisture. Several times he overturned
promising stones with his feet, hut even the
froes which generally inhabited the damp
spots beneath had disappeared. And then
the creek ran itself out into a desert of
sand which stretched away to the horizon.
With the knowledge that his last chance
of water had gone the thirst returned, and
with it the panic which at the start had
confused his brain and knotted his feet. He
could never cross that sand, he knew, and
the hounds at his heels were gaining fast.
The halt in the creek bed was responsible
for that. Casting a quick glance backwards
he saw that they were not more than 30
yards away.
For the first time since the hunt com-
menced a cry escaped his lips. It was a
harsh cry. and the words rumbled slowly
through his swollen lips. Cursing as he ran.
he sucked on the pebble which he had thrust
into his mouth at the creek.
Constantly now the thirst gnawed at his
throat, and he ran with his mouth wdde
open. But he did not breathe through his
mouth ; the tongue swollen double its usual
size forbade that. The pebble had dropped
out when he stumbled.
Another glance behind showed the hunters
still 30 yards away. It occurred to him as

their great work of filtering out uric acid and kidney poisons,

important is their work that, even though they get only a little
behind, no part of the system is free from danger; the back
aches, the limbs drag, the eyelids seem like lead ; there are
dropsical swellings, rheumatic pains, dizzy spells, urinary
troubles.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills are special kidney help.
They correct urinary disorders, gravel, bladder troubles—cure
backache, rheumatism, dropsy and every kidney complaint. The genuine Doan’s Backache
Kidney Pills are reliable for men and women, old and young.
