strange. His own pace had slackened, he
knew. That theirs should do likewise he
could not understand. They were without
water equally with himself, but they were
blacks, and used to it. It was no hardship
for them. A hundred yards further on he
stumbled again, but saved himself with his
outstretched palms. Next time his arms
were slow to do the bidding of his sluggish
brain, and he measured his length upon the
earth. Scrambling to his feet he looked
fearfully over his shoulder, di'eading what
the glance would reveal. The pursuers
were still there, 30 yards in the rear. He
was sure they grinned. Frequently, be-
tween his stumblings, he looked behind now,
always to be met with that tantalising
smirk, more terrible and exasperating than
all the spear-waving and shouting which
had gone before. Sargent he saw ;. Sargent,
whose blood had, bhforetimej frozen at his
approach. And Martin, and Sammy Lach-
lan. Sammy, cousin to Jimmy, Combo’s
son, whose neck he had twisted the day be-
fore. And one and all they grinned, and
remained 30 yards in the real - .
For half an hour past he had felt the
finish approaching. The stumblings were
more frequent, and he climbed slowly and
painfully to his feet only by the aid of his
hands and knees. The scant breath which
found its way to his lungs left a passage
of molten fire; the exudation left him sick
and dizzy. Without water with which to
moisten his burning tongue and thx - oat, he
knew his next fall must be the last.
As he stood and swayed erect for the last
time, an inspiration came to him which
brought a chuckle from his parched and
broken lips. As the thought took definite
form he gibbered at the idea. AVhat if the
creek was dx - y ! There would be water on
the other side of the sand stretch, and he
could get tliei’e now.
Feeling in his pockets as he staggered on,
his hopes were momentarily shattered when
he failed to discover his knife. It was
there when he started, he was sure; it
must have dropped out in one of his fre-
quent falls. Well, he still had his teeth.
The tag of his boot-lace or a pointed stick
would serve, but he could not spare the
time requisite to take out his lace, and there
were no sticks. So it had to be his teeth.
Raising his arm he selected the largest
vein. It was blue, and stood out like whip-
cord beneath the sun-dried skin of his wrist.
As the teeth sank in, bringing forth the
dark red fluid with,a spurt, he pressed it to
his mouth and sucked as he staggered on.
* * * *
It was morning. The sun had not yet
pierced the heavy hanging fog, risen un-
accountably from the sun-baked earth. As
though many eyes bored into hex - , the woman
Ilarriette stirred uncomfortably in her sleep.
At hex - side something moved—moved convul-
sively several times and then remained still.
With the sound of a great shouting in hex-
ears she sat erect, rubbing her eyes with the
knuckles of her fists. The shouting con-
tinued. It was a glad shouting as if the
tribe rejoiced over some great event-
Looking surpidsedly around, she saw a
semi-circle of blacks watching with great
intei-est and evident pleasure something on
the gi-ound at her side. From time to time
shouts proclaimed her prowess that all the
tribe might hear.
Glancing down she sidled away a yard,
quickly, on her haunches. Then she re-
turned. She could not undei-stand the tribe’s
acclamation of herself just because Boss
Lawson had bitten himself in his sleep and
died.
G. L. THOMAS.
One Moonlight Night.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FROM the end of the pier Jimmy Black
looked out across the moonlit sea. Save
for his sharply-silhouetted figure, the
night held only silver and silence; a silence
accentuated by the lap-lapping of the reced-
ing tide against the piles of the pier, resem-
bling the sound made by a woman’s skirts.
In the distance it looked as if, from a cornu-
copia in the sky, thousands of small silver
coins had been poured on to the blue cai’pet
of the bay. The air, stirred by the faintest
of breezes, was as the warm bx - eath of a
sleeping girl.
Unconscious of the beauty of the night, the
watcher on the pier stood with bent shoul-
ders and brooded. In his mind was a
thought, such as has at some time come to
every embittered egotist, of how remorseful
Lil would feel were she to learn of his death.
And drowning was said to be such an easy
and pleasant way of leaving the world. Only
a few nights befoi’e he had listened to a dis-
cussion in which the opinions of persons who
had nearly drowned were quoted to the effect
that drowning was a “beautiful death.”
Black had succeeded in convincing himself
that he seriously contemplated suicide, and
that this was the reason he was standing at
midnight at the end of a deserted pier. As a
matter of fact, he was merely playing with
the idea, as an angry child does with the
thought of leaving home and plunging its
parents into unconsolahle grief. Jimmy
Black lacked initiative to take any step so
definite. He was the sort of man who would
keep on living and would continue misei - able.
Two years of marriage—periods of tranquil
happiness and sweet companionship, inter-
rupted by petty quarrels and mutual irrita-
bility—had left him disappointed and dis-
illusioned. His had been more or less an
average matrimonial experience, but your
egotist always regards his own case as ex-
ceptional. Lately the absurd quari - els had
become more fi’equent, and were followed by
periods when for days Black and his wife
lived in the same house, met at meals, and
sat and read in the same room in the even-
ing without exchanging a word.
One of these ridiculous stalemates had now
continued for nearly a fortnight, and Black
was wishing he had the pluck to put to the
test the theory about the beauty of drown-
ing. But a poor little sulky clerk, without
the ability either to kiss or to kick an equally
sulky woman into a proper frame of mind,
does not commit suicide. Despite platitudes
mouthed by comfortable persons in arm-
chairs. there is a strain of the heroic in the
man who deliberately destroys his entire uni-
verse and sets out on a journey into the
unknown.
Jimmy gazed into the water and thought
how peaceful it looked. For long he stood
thex-e watching the tide going out, until it
seemed that silver threads were being di’awn
through his brain in the same direction.
Everything—tide, pier and Jimmy’s immor-
tal soul —seemed to be moving out to w’hei'e
those gleaming threepences and sixpences
were strewn. He had foi-gotten all about
Lil and his grievances, and the office in which
his days passed wretchedly. The gentle
motion of the water was making him drowsy.
Lifting his head, he felt suddenly giddy; he
tried to draw his hands from his pockets to
steady himself, swayed for a second —then
the water seemed to rise to meet him, and he
fell gently upon the bosom of the bay.
Despite the warmth of the night, the water
was unpleasantly cold. Jimmy received a
salty mouthful and tried unsucessfully to
spit it out. Two thoughts flashed through
his mind: that he was a d—d fool not to have
learned to swim, and that the people who
talked about the ease and beauty of drown-
ing were bigger d—d fools. Splashing about
in a futile way, he went under.
He came to the surface, ears and mouth
filled with water, some 10 yards from the
pier. To Black the distance seemed miles
and the chance of ever reaching the pier re-
mote. A terror of death seized him and he
strove wildly and unscientifically to keep
himself afloat. Each insignificant wave ap-
peared mountain high, and the gentle lap-lap-
ping of the sea became as rhythmic thunder.
The thought of drowning there, alone in the
moonlight, was intolerable, and yet he
realised the futility of his struggles. For
what seemed many minutes he stared with
wild eyes at the moon; then it was sud-
denly hidden from his sight. Once again
he was going down, down, thinking the
while that he would rise once more and then
sink for the last time. Unconsciousness
came upon him with a rush.
When his senses returned .Timmy Black
was aware of a dazzling light that hurt his
eyes. At first he thought it was the moon
still shining down upon his face, but a
moment later it became the electric light in
his own bedroom. He gave a long sigh and
closed his eyes again. A clean white sheet
came up to his chin and he could hear Lil’s
lowered voice at the other end of the room,
and, answering her, a man’s voice—Dr.
West's, he fancied. It was not worth his
while to open his eyes and find out, he felt
so nice and comfy.
Once again there was that gentle lap-lap-
ping, but this time the sound was made not
by the water, but by Lil’s skirts as she moved
across the room. He looked up at her and
gave a little whimsical smile that was meant
to convey: “Well, old girl, I’m not going to
be the first to speak.” Then he shut his
eyes to avoid the light.
Jimmy felt Lil’s cool hand upon his fore-
head. Bending over him she broke the
silence of a fortnight. “Poor old dear!” she
murmured. That curl, which he had simply
adored in his courting days but had scarcely
noticed for a year or more, tickled his nose,
Lil’s heart beat against his breast and their
lips met in a long kiss of reconciliation.
Tired, but happy, .Timmy closed his eyes
more tightly and his mind became a blank.
The body was washed upon the beach
next day. At the inquest the Coroner’s verdict
was that James Richard Black was found
drowned in the bay at Brighton; there being
no evidence to show how he got into the
water.
HARRISON OWEN.
Haunted Memory.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WILL go on to the sunrise, taking the
road as it winds
Beyond three trees and a broken gate
and a great house that cannot see
Because the windows are shuttered over the
ragged blinds —•
And there is none within it to open the
door to me.
But there is a hedge in blossom and a scent
of honey is blown
Always out of the garden if one should loiter
and pass;
And it seems like a place that sometimes at
evening I must havf known,
Walking with shining feet when the dew
was wet on the grass.
But I will go on to the sunrise, for over the
hill is the sea,
Making a murmur on rocks and lifting the
salt brown weed
And a yellow flower on the cliff that is
flaunting a petal free,
While the stem below the blossom is heavy
with ripening seed.
I never have looked from the hill, but I
know how the headland runs,
Caved and crumbling, to shelter a small boat
near to the sand ;
And the quiet water flashes a thousand swift
• little suns
That the breeze chases out to the ocean and
hurries back to the land.
I will come back from the sunrise, taking the
road past the door,
By the rusted gate that is broken and the
hedge and the silent trees;
For surely a ghost walks with me who has
been here too often before,
Hearing a sob in the water and a grief in
the moan of the bees.
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
